The Syllogism
beginner
7 min
Definition of Syllogism and Its Essential Terms
Author defines qiyas and its basic parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor term, major term, and middle term in any categorical syllogism and explain their roles.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a machine that extracts a hidden conclusion from two premises by revealing the connection through the middle term.

In this chapter, the author defines the syllogism as a composite of premises that necessarily entail a conclusion. He then introduces the fundamental division into categorical and conditional syllogisms, and breaks down the categorical syllogism into its three essential terms: the minor, the major, and the middle. Understanding these terms is the first step to constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor term (ḥadd aṣghar)
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion. In the syllogism, it is the term that is included in, or falls under, the major term. For example, in 'Every human is mortal; Socrates is human; therefore Socrates is mortal,' the minor term is 'Socrates'.
The poem says: 'وأصغر فذاك ذو اندراج' – the minor is the one that is included.
2
Identify the major term (ḥadd akbar)
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion. It is the term into which the minor is included. In the same example, the major term is 'mortal'.
The major premise is the premise containing the major term.
3
Identify the middle term (wasṭ)
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It is the link that connects the minor and major terms. In the example, the middle term is 'human'.
The poem states: 'ووسط يلغى لدى الإنتاج' – the middle is omitted at the conclusion.
4
Verify the syllogism's structure
Ensure that the minor premise is the premise containing the minor term, and the major premise contains the major term. The middle term must appear in both premises. If these conditions hold, the syllogism is well-formed.
The author says: 'وما من المقدمات صغرى فيجب اندراجها في الكبرى' – the minor must be included in the major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القياس
إن القياسَ مِنْ قَضايا صُوّرا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرا
ثُمَّ القِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِالاقْتِراني
وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ على النتيجة بِقُوَّة وَاخْتَصَّ بالحَمْلِيَّةِ
فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تركيبَهُ فَرَكِّبا مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ عَلى مَا وَجَبَا
وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّماتِ وَانْظُرا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ مُخْتَبِرا
فَإِنَّ لازِمَ المُقَدِّماتِ بِحَسَبِ المُقَدِّماتِ آتِ
وَما مِنَ المُقَدِّماتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِراجُها فِي الكُبرى
وَذَاتُ حَدٍّ أَصْغَرٍ صُغْراهُما وَذَاتُ حَدٍّ أكْبَرِ كَبْراهما
وَأَصْغَرُ فَذاكَ ذُو انْدِراج وَوَسَطٌ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنتاج
تعريف القياس
أقول: القياس عبارة عن قول من قضايا رُكبت تركيباً خاصاً بحيث تستلزم لذاتها قولاً آخر. فالمفرد ليس بقول، فهو ليس بقياس، فالقول عند المناطقة هو المركب. وبالتركيب من القضايا تخرج القضية الواحدة، والمراد بالقضايا قضيتان أو أكثر، ليشمل القياس البسيط وهو المركب من مقدمتين، كقولنا:
Chapter on Syllogism
Indeed the syllogism is [composed] of premises, formed as necessarily entailing by themselves another statement.
Then the syllogism according to them is of two types: from it is what is called categorical,
And it is that which indicates the conclusion by force, and is specific to categorical [propositions].
If you intend its composition, then compose its premises as is necessary,
And arrange the premises and look at their sound from the false, examining.
For the necessary consequence of the premises comes according to the premises.
And whichever of the premises is a minor, then its inclusion in the major is necessary.
And that which has a minor term is the minor premise of them, and that which has a major term is the major premise.
And the minor is the one that is included, and the middle term is omitted at the conclusion.
Definition of Syllogism
I say: The syllogism is an expression composed of premises arranged in a special composition such that it entails by itself another expression. So a single word is not an expression, so it is not a syllogism, for 'expression' according to the logicians is the composite. And by composition from premises, the single proposition is excluded, and what is meant by 'premises' is two or more, to include the simple syllogism which is composed of two premises, like our saying:
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on qiyas (syllogism) and provides its definition. The author begins with a poetic summary: the syllogism is composed of premises that necessarily entail another statement. It is divided into two types: categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i, implied later). The categorical syllogism is characterized by using categorical propositions and produces its conclusion by force. The poem instructs the reader to compose the premises properly, arrange them, and test them for validity. The conclusion follows from the premises. The minor premise must be included in the major premise. The minor term is the one that is included (the subject of the conclusion), and the major term is the predicate of the conclusion. The middle term is the one that disappears in the conclusion. The prose expands: qiyas is a composite of premises arranged specially; a single word is not a statement; a single proposition is not syllogism; the minimal syllogism has two premises. The example is cut off but would typically be a standard categorical syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس
الاقتراني
حد أصغر
حد أكبر
وسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which term is omitted (لغا) in the conclusion of a categorical syllogism?
A) The minor term
B) The major term
C) The middle term
D) All three

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism consists of exactly three terms: the minor (subject of conclusion), major (predicate of conclusion), and middle (connecting term omitted in conclusion).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism on a fiqh topic: use the form 'Every A is B, Every C is A, therefore Every C is B' with an Islamic example. Identify the minor, major, and middle terms.
?
Given the syllogism 'All intoxicants are forbidden, Nabidh is an intoxicant, therefore Nabidh is forbidden', write down which term is the minor, which is the major, and which is the middle.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Definition of Qiyas and Its Essential Conditions
Author defines qiyas with its conditions and gives examples of valid and invalid forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given composite statement qualifies as a qiyas according to the classical definition, by checking the conditions of intrinsic entailment and repetition of the middle term.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a composite of propositions that intrinsically entails another statement.

In this passage, the author defines the qiyas (syllogism) and sets out its essential conditions. You must understand that not every combination of two or more propositions forms a valid syllogism; only those that intrinsically entail a conclusion and have a repeated middle term qualify. This lesson will guide you through the definition and the conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-qiyas'. The author states it is the most important topic in logic because demonstration is completed through it.
2
Extract the genus
The author says qiyas is a 'statement composed of propositions' (qawl min qadaya). This is the broader category.
3
Extract the differentia
The composition must be such that the premises 'necessarily entail by essence another statement' (mustalziman bi al-dhat qawlan akhara). This is what distinguishes qiyas from other composites.
The condition 'by essence' excludes accidental entailment.
4
Examine what is excluded
A single proposition is excluded because it is not composite. Composites that do not intrinsically entail another statement are excluded, such as those lacking a repeated middle term. Examples include 'Every human is an animal, every horse is a neighing animal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القياس
إن القياس من أهم المباحث في علم المنطق، فبالقياس يتم البرهان، وكل ما مضى من المباحث كان مجرد مبادئ للوصول إلى تركيب القياس، وأركانه. قال:
لإن القياسَ مِنْ قَضايا صُوّرا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرا
ثُمَّ القِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِالاقْتِراني
وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ على النتيجة بِقُوَّة وَاخْتَصَّ بالحَمْلِيَّةِ فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تركيبَهُ فَرَكيا مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ عَلى مَا وَجَبَا وَرَ تٌـبِ المُقَدِّماتِ وَانْظُرا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ مُخْتَبِرا
فَإِنَّ لازِمَ المُقَدِّماتِ بِحَسَبِ المُقَدِّماتِ آتِ
وَما مِنَ المُقَدِّماتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِراجُها فِي الكُبرى
وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرٍ صُغْراهُما وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أكْبَرِ كَبْراهما
وَأَصْغَرُ فَذاكَ ذُو انْدِراج وَوَسَطٌ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنتاج
أقول: القياس عبارة عن قول من قضايا رُكبت تركيباً خاصاً بحيث تستلزم لذاتها قولاً آخر. فالمفرد ليس بقول، فهو ليس بقياس، فالقول عند المناطقة هو المركب. وبالتركيب من القضايا تخرج القضية الواحدة، والمراد بالقضايا قضيتان أو أكثر، ليشمل القياس البسيط وهو المركب من مقدمتين، كقولنا: العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث، والقياس المركب من أكثر من مقدمتين، كقولنا: النباش آخذ للمال خفية، وكل آخذ للمال خفية سارق، وكل سارق تقطع يده. وأما القول المركب من القضايا التي لا تستلزم لذاتها قولاً آخر، فليس قياساً أيضاً، وقد يكون عدم إنتاجه لعدم تكرر الحد الوسط، كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل فرس صهال، وكالقضيتين المركبتين من ضرب عقيم لا ينتج، كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر وكل حجر جسم، فهذا لا يستلزم شيئاً لعدم إيجاب الصغرى كما سنبين في أضرب القياس المنتجة والعقيمة.
Chapter on the syllogism. Indeed the syllogism is among the most important topics in the science of logic, for by the syllogism demonstration is completed, and everything that has passed of topics was merely principles for reaching the composition of the syllogism and its pillars. He said: Indeed the syllogism is from propositions formed, necessarily entailing by essence another statement. Then the syllogism according to them is two divisions: among it is what is called the categorical, and it is that which indicates the conclusion by force and is specific to categorical [propositions]. If you want its composition, then compose its premises as required, and arrange the premises and look at its sound from its corrupt, testing. For the necessary consequence of the premises comes according to the premises. And whichever of the premises is the minor, then its subsumption under the major is necessary. And that which has a lesser term is its minor, and that which has a greater term is its major, and the lesser is that which is subsumed, and the middle is omitted at the production. I say: The syllogism is an expression of a statement composed of propositions arranged in a specific composition such that they necessarily entail by essence another statement. So the single [word] is not a statement, so it is not a syllogism, for the statement according to the logicians is the composite. And by the composition of propositions, the single proposition is excluded, and by 'propositions' is meant two or more, to include the simple syllogism which is composed of two premises, such as our saying: The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated; and the compound syllogism composed of more than two premises, such as our saying: The grave-robber is a taker of wealth secretly, and every taker of wealth secretly is a thief, and every thief's hand is cut off. And as for the statement composed of propositions that do not necessarily entail by essence another statement, then it is also not a syllogism. And its non-production may be due to the non-repetition of the middle term, such as our saying: Every human is an animal, and every horse is a neighing [animal]; and like the two propositions composed from a barren mood that does not produce, such as our saying: No human is a stone, and every stone is a body, so this does not entail anything due to the non-affirmation of the minor, as we will explain in the productive and barren moods of the syllogism.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines the qiyas (syllogism) and lays down its essential conditions. The author begins by stating the importance of the syllogism in logic, then presents a versified summary (matn) that defines the syllogism as a composite of propositions that intrinsically entails another statement. The verse distinguishes two types: the iqtirani (categorical) syllogism, which uses categorical premises. The prose commentary expands on the definition: a syllogism is a statement (qawl) composed of premises (qadaya) arranged in a specific way such that by themselves (li-dhatiha) they entail a conclusion. The author clarifies that a single proposition is not a syllogism, nor is any composite that does not intrinsically entail another proposition. Examples are given: the simple syllogism with two premises (e.g., 'The world is changing, every changing thing is originated') and the compound syllogism with more than two (e.g., 'The grave-robber takes wealth secretly, every secret taker is a thief, every thief's hand is cut'). The author then gives examples of invalid compositions: where the middle term is not repeated (e.g., 'Every human is an animal, every horse is a neighing animal') or where the premises are from a barren mood (e.g., 'No human is a stone, every stone is a body'). The key point is that a valid syllogism must have a repeated middle term and the premises must be affirmative in the right way to produce a necessary entailment.

Key Terms
القياس
الحد الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid simple syllogism according to the definition in this passage?
A) Every human is an animal, and every horse is a neighing animal.
B) The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated.
C) No human is a stone, and every stone is a body.
D) Zayd is standing, and Amr is sitting.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism must be a composite of at least two propositions that intrinsically entail a conclusion, and it requires a repeated middle term between the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following composite: 'Every human is mortal; Socrates is a human; therefore Socrates is mortal.' Identify the genus and differentia of this composite if it were to be defined as qiyas. Explain why it qualifies as a valid simple syllogism according to the definition.
?
Construct a simple categorical syllogism on a fiqh issue (e.g., wine is intoxicating, intoxicants are forbidden) and identify the middle term.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Why Equality and Disparity Syllogisms Are Invalid
Author explains why equality and disparity syllogisms are invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why qiyas al-musawa and qiyas al-mubayana are invalid and explain the condition of intrinsic entailment in a valid syllogism.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism must produce its conclusion intrinsically, without needing an extraneous premise.

In this passage, the author refines the definition of qiyas by excluding forms that appear to yield a conclusion but do so only through an external premise. He focuses on two invalid forms: qiyas al-musawa (equality syllogism) and qiyas al-mubayana (disparity syllogism). Understanding why these fail is crucial for grasping the condition that the middle term must be repeated and entailment must be intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for a valid syllogism
A valid syllogism must produce its conclusion by itself (li dhatihi). If the conclusion follows only with the help of an external premise, it is not a true syllogism.
The author states this at the beginning: 'the intended meaning of syllogism is the statement that produces by itself.'
2
Examine the equality example
Consider: 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr.' This seems to entail 'Zayd is equal to Bakr', but the entailment depends on the extraneous premise 'the equal of the equal to a thing is equal to that thing'. Without this premise, the conclusion does not follow.
3
Examine the disparity example
Consider: 'The human is disparate from the horse, and the horse is disparate from the rational.' It does not entail 'The human is disparate from the rational' because 'the disparate of the disparate is not necessarily disparate.' The needed extraneous premise is false.
4
Discover the deeper reason: middle term not repeated
The author adds that the real reason these are invalid is that the middle term is not repeated. In the equality example, 'Amr' appears as subject in the first premise and predicate in the second. In a valid categorical syllogism, the middle term must occupy the same role (e.g., subject of both major and minor).
He shows that if we repeat the middle term properly, e.g., 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and what is equal to Amr is equal to Bakr', the syllogism becomes valid.
5
Summarize the conditions for intrinsic entailment
A valid syllogism must have a repeated middle term and must not rely on any extraneous premise. Both conditions ensure that the conclusion follows intrinsically from the premises alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود من القياس هو القول المنتج لذاته، فلا يدخل فيه ما يستلزم قولاً آخر لا لذاته كقياس المساواة، وهو المركب من قضيتين متعلق محمول إحداهما موضوع الأخرى، كقولنا: زيد مساو لعمرو، وعمرو مساو لبكر، فإنه يستلزم زيد مساو لبكر، ولكن هذا الاستلزام ليس لذات القياس بل بواسطة صدق مقدمة أجنبية وهي أن مساوي المساوي لشيء، مساوٍ لذلك الشيء، ألا ترى أنك لو قلت الإنسان مباين للفرس، والفرس مباين للناطق، لم يلزم منه أن الإنسان مباين للناطق، لأن مباين المباين للشيء لا يلزم أن يكون مبايناً لذلك الشيء والقول الآخر الذي ينتجه القياس المراد به النتيجة، فإنها قول مغاير لقضيتي القياس، فيخرج به القضيتان المستلزمتان لإحداهما كقولنا: زيد قائم وعمرو جالس، فهاتان القضيتان يستلزمان إحداهما ولا يسميان قياساً لأن إحداهما ليس قولاً آخر. =(1) الدليل على أن : زيد مساو لعمرو، وعمرو مساو لبكر قضيّتان لا يستلزمان لذاتيهما أن زيداً مساو لبكر، هو أنه لو لم تصدق أنّ مساوى المساوى لشيء، مساو لذلك الشيء لم يلزم أن زيداً مساو لبكر. ومعنى ذلك أن استلزام هاتين القضيتين لقول آخر ليس لذاتيهما ، بل هو موقوف على صدق قضيّة أخرى أجنبيـه عنهما. ولذلك قال المحقق الشارح، ألا ترى أنك لو قلت الإنسان مباين للفرس، والفرس مباين للناطق، لم يلزم منه أن الإنسان مباين للناطق، أى مع أنّ القياس يبدو لأول وهلة صحيحاً، إلا أنه ليس قياساً لأنّ مقدمتيه لا تستلزمان لذاتهما قولاً آخر، بل يتوقف ذلك على صحة مقدمة أجنبية وهى أن مباين المباين للشيء مباين لذلك الشيء، وهى كاذبة، فلم يلزم منهما شيء. فافهم. والذي أراه هو أن قياس المساواة والمباينة في هذين المثالين ليس قياساً صحيحاً لأمر آخر أهم مما يمثل بهما عليه ههنا تقريباً للمفهـوم عـلـى تسليم صحة القياس صورةً؛ فأقول إن صورة القياس ليست صحيحة أصلاً لأن الحد الوسط فيه لم يتكرر. ولـو تكرر لاستلزمت القضيتان لذاتهما قولاً آخر. ألا ترى أنك لو قلت زيد مساو لعمرو، والمساوى لعمرو مساو لبكر أنه يلزم عنهما ان زيداً مساو لبكر. وكذا لو قلت مثلاً الإنسان مباين للفرس، وبعض المباين للفرس ناطق أنه يلزم عنهما أن بعض الإنسان ناطق. (2) لأن مباين مباين الشيء لا يعلم إذا كان مبايناً للشيء أو مساوياً له. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت القاتل مباين لزيد، وزید مباين لعمرو لم يلزم من ذلك أن القاتل ليس عمرواً ولا أن عمرواً هو القاتل. (3) يعنى بالقضيّتين مجموع القضيتين، فإنهما من حيث ما هما مجموع فإنهما يستلزم مجموعهما لإحداهما أى لكل واحدة منهما على حدته. وهذا من استلزام الكلّ من حيث ما هو كل لجزئه. لأن الكل موقوف علـى الجزء، فحصول الكل يستلزم حصول الجزء قطعاً. وفي مثاله، فإن مجموع زيد قائم، وعمرو جالس يستلزم أنّ
And the intended meaning of syllogism is the statement that produces by itself, so what entails another statement not by itself is not included in it, like the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions where the predicate of one is related to the subject of the other, such as our saying: Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr, for it entails that Zayd is equal to Bakr, but this entailment is not by the essence of the syllogism but by the truth of an extraneous premise, namely that the equal of the equal to a thing is equal to that thing. Do you not see that if you said: The human is disparate from the horse, and the horse is disparate from the rational, it does not entail that the human is disparate from the rational, because the disparate of the disparate to a thing is not necessarily disparate to that thing. And the other statement that the syllogism produces is intended as the conclusion, for it is a statement different from the two premises of the syllogism, so by it are excluded the two propositions that entail one of them, such as our saying: Zayd is standing and Amr is sitting, for these two propositions entail one of them and are not called a syllogism because one of them is not another statement. (1) The proof that 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr' are two propositions that do not intrinsically entail that Zayd is equal to Bakr is that if it were not true that the equal of the equal to a thing is equal to that thing, then it would not follow that Zayd is equal to Bakr. That means the entailment of these two propositions to another statement is not by their essence, but depends on the truth of another extraneous premise. Therefore, the verifying commentator said: Do you not see that if you said 'The human is disparate from the horse, and the horse is disparate from the rational', it does not entail that the human is disparate from the rational, meaning that even though the syllogism appears at first glance valid, it is not a syllogism because its two premises do not intrinsically entail another statement, but rather that depends on the correctness of an extraneous premise, namely that the disparate of the disparate to a thing is disparate to that thing, and it is false, so nothing follows from them. Understand. And what I see is that the syllogism of equality and disparity in these two examples is not a valid syllogism for another more important reason than what is exemplified here for approximating the concept, assuming the syllogism is valid in form; so I say that the form of the syllogism is not valid at all because the middle term is not repeated. If it were repeated, the two propositions would intrinsically entail another statement. Do you not see that if you said 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and what is equal to Amr is equal to Bakr', then from them it follows that Zayd is equal to Bakr. Similarly, if you said for example 'The human is disparate from the horse, and some disparate from the horse is rational', then from them it follows that 'some human is rational'. (2) Because the disparate of a disparate thing is not known whether it is disparate or equal to that thing. Do you not see that if you said 'The killer is disparate from Zayd, and Zayd is disparate from Amr', it does not follow from that that the killer is not Amr nor that Amr is the killer. (3) He means by 'the two propositions' the sum of the two propositions, for they, as a sum, entail their sum to one of them, i.e., to each one separately. This is from the entailment of the whole as a whole for its part, because the whole depends on the part, so obtaining the whole necessarily entails obtaining the part. In his example, the sum of 'Zayd is standing and Amr is sitting' entails that
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of qiyas by excluding cases that appear to produce a conclusion but do so only by virtue of an external premise, not intrinsically. The author first states that a valid syllogism must produce its conclusion by itself (bi dhatihi). He gives the example of qiyas al-musawa (equality syllogism): 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr' entails 'Zayd is equal to Bakr', but this entailment depends on the extraneous premise that 'the equal of the equal to something is equal to that thing'. Without that premise, the entailment fails. Similarly, the disparity syllogism 'The human is disparate from the horse, and the horse is disparate from the rational' does not entail 'The human is disparate from the rational' because 'the disparate of the disparate' is not necessarily disparate. The author then adds his own insight: the real reason these are invalid is that the middle term is not repeated. In the equality example, 'Amr' appears as subject in the first premise and predicate in the second, but the middle term in a valid categorical syllogism must appear in the same role in both premises. He shows that if we rephrase to repeat the middle term properly, e.g., 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and what is equal to Amr is equal to Bakr', then the syllogism becomes valid. The passage also clarifies that two propositions that merely entail one of them (like 'Zayd is standing and Amr is sitting') are not a syllogism because the entailed proposition is not different from the premises. The footnotes further elaborate on these points, including the argument that the whole entails its parts but that is not a syllogism.

مقدمة أجنبية

The Syllogism

Definition of Qiyas: Self-Entailing Discourse
Author defines qiyas as self-entailing discourse and excludes equality syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism qualifies as a valid qiyas according to the condition of self-entailment.

Core Idea
A valid qiyas must produce its conclusion intrinsically, without needing an external premise.

In classical Islamic logic, the definition of qiyas is precise: it must be 'al-qawl al-muntij li-dhatih' – discourse that produces its conclusion by itself. This lesson unpacks that definition, distinguishing genuine syllogisms from apparent ones that rely on external principles, and clarifies that the conclusion must be a new, distinct statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and essential condition
The term being defined is 'al-qiyas' (القياس). The essential condition is that it must produce a conclusion 'li-dhatih' (by itself). This means no external premise is needed for the entailment.
Contrast with qiyas al-musawat which requires an external premise about transitivity.
2
Recognize the counterexample: equality syllogism
The author gives 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr' which only entails 'Zayd is equal to Bakr' if we add an external premise about equality being transitive. Thus, it is not a valid qiyas by the given definition.
The example with 'mubayana' shows that if the relation is not transitive, the entailment fails entirely.
3
Understand the requirement of a distinct conclusion
The conclusion must be a new statement different from the premises. Two premises that merely entail one of them (e.g., 'Zayd is standing and Amr is sitting' entailing 'Zayd is standing') do not count as a qiyas because the conclusion is not separate.
This excludes cases where one premise is repeated as the conclusion.
4
Note the inclusion of false syllogisms
The definition applies to both true and false premises. A formal syllogism like 'Every human is a donkey, every donkey is a brayer' still produces a conclusion (that every human is a brayer) even though the premises are false. The qiyas is valid in form regardless of truth.
This guards against the misconception that a qiyas must be sound in content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود من القياس هو القول المنتج لذاته، فلا يدخل فيه ما يستلزم قولاً آخر لا لذاته كقياس المساواة، وهو المركب من قضيتين متعلق محمول إحداهما موضوع الأخرى، كقولنا:[زيـد مسـاو لعمرو، وعمرو مساو لبكر]، فإنه يستلزم [زيد مساو لبكر]، ولكن هذا الاستلزام ليس لذات القياس بل بواسطة صدق مقدمة أجنبية وهي أن مساوي المساوي لشيء، مساوٍ لذلك الشيء]، ألا ترى أنك لو قلت [الإنسان مباين للفرس، والفرس مباين للناطق]، لم يلزم منه أن [الإنسان مباين للناطق]، لأن مباين المباين للشيء لا يلزم أن يكون مبايناً لذلك الشيء والقول الآخر الذي ينتجه القياس المراد به النتيجة، فإنها قول مغاير لقضيتي القياس، فيخرج به القضيتان المستلزمتان لإحداهما كقولنا: [زيد قائم وعمرو جالس، فهاتان القضيتان يستلزمان إحداهما ولا يسميان قياساً لأن إحداهما ليس قولاً آخر. والقياس متى سُلّم استلزم النتيجة سواء كان صادقاً، كما مر أو كاذباً كقولنا: أكل إنسان حمار، وكل حمار صهال] فإنه يستلزم بحيث لو سُلّم أن كل إنسان صهال. وإنما وجب قولنا ذلك لأن التعريف يجب صدقه على القياس الصادق والكاذب كالسفسطة.
And the intended meaning of qiyas is the discourse that produces [a conclusion] by itself; so it does not include what entails another discourse not by itself, like the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions where the predicate of one is related to the subject of the other, as in our saying: 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr,' for it entails 'Zayd is equal to Bakr,' but this entailment is not by the essence of the qiyas but through the truth of an external premise, namely that the equal of the equal of something is equal to that something. Do you not see that if you say 'Man is different from horse, and horse is different from rational,' it does not follow that 'Man is different from rational,' because the different of the different of something does not necessarily entail that it is different from that something. And the other discourse that the qiyas produces is intended as the conclusion, for it is a discourse different from the two premises of the qiyas. Thus excluded are the two premises that entail one of them, as in our saying: 'Zayd is standing and Amr is sitting,' for these two premises entail one of them but are not called a qiyas because one of them is not another discourse. And the qiyas, whenever it is admitted, entails the conclusion, whether it is true, as has passed, or false, as in our saying: 'Every human is a donkey, and every donkey is a brayer,' for it entails, such that if admitted, that every human is a brayer. And we must say that because the definition must apply to the true and false qiyas, like sophistry.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) and sets its essential condition: that it must produce its conclusion by itself, without reliance on an external premise. The author explains this by contrasting it with qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality), where the entailment depends on an additional premise (e.g., the transitivity of equality). The example of Zayd, Amr, and Bakr illustrates that without the external premise, the conclusion does not follow. Similarly, the example of mubayana (difference) shows that difference is not transitive; thus, such a composition does not constitute a valid qiyas. The author further clarifies that the conclusion must be a new statement distinct from the premises; hence, two premises that merely entail one of them (like 'Zayd is standing and Amr is sitting' entailing 'Zayd is standing') are not a qiyas. Finally, the definition applies to both true and false syllogisms, as a false syllogism still produces a conclusion formally. This guards against the misconception that a qiyas must be sound in content, whereas the definition covers formal validity.

Understanding al-Qiyas al-Iqtirani: Composition and Scope
Author divides qiyas into iqtirani and sharti, defines iqtirani, and discusses its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-qiyas al-iqtirani and explain why it can be composed of either categorical or conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Iqtirani is defined by its conjunctive form, not by proposition type.

In this passage, the author classifies qiyas into two primary types and then focuses on the first type, al-qiyas al-iqtirani. He clarifies a common misunderstanding by demonstrating that iqtirani can include conditional as well as categorical premises. Through concrete examples, we learn the essential features of iqtirani, including its naming, structure, and validity conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of qiyas
The author states that qiyas is divided into iqtirani and sharti. Note that sharti is just mentioned here but not elaborated.
2
Understand the naming and definition of iqtirani
Iqtirani means 'conjunctive' because the terms are conjoined without an exception particle (like 'illa'). The definition emphasizes that it indicates the conclusion potentially, meaning the conclusion's parts are scattered in the premises.
3
Compare the categorical and conditional examples
The author gives a categorical example (the world is changing, every changing is originated) and then a conditional example (if the sun shines then day exists, etc.). Notice that both follow the form of iqtirani: terms are connected directly without 'istithna'.
4
Examine the correction of the nazim's opinion
The versifier (nazim) restricted iqtirani to categoricals, but the author deems this a weak opinion and presents the conditional example as proof that iqtirani can be composed of conditionals.
5
Review the composition requirements
The passage lists conditions: must have a repeated term (middle), minor premise contains subject/antecedent, major contains predicate/consequent, minor before major, formal constraints (e.g., minor affirmative, major universal in first figure), and material truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القياس: القياس قسمان: الأول اقتراني، والثاني شرطى القياس الاقتراني وسمي بالاقتراني لاقتران الحدود فيه وعدم فصلها بأداة استثناء كقولنا: [العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث، والقياس الاقتراني هو الذي دلَّ على النتيجة بالقوة بأن كانت فيه متفرقة الأجزاء. ألا ترى أو قولنا [العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث ] يدل على النتيجة وهي [العالم حادث]، لكن بالقوة، بمعنى أن أجزاءها متفرقة فيه، لأن موضوعها موضوع الصغرى ومحمولها محمول الكبرى. ولا يركب القياس الاقتراني إلا من القضايا الحملية، ولا يستعمل فيه القضايا الشرطية. كذا قال الناظم، ولكن هذا رأي مرجوح والصحيح أن القياس الاقتراني يؤلف من القضايا الحمليـات كما تقدم، ومن القضايا الشرطيات كقولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجوداً، وكلما كان النهار موجودا كانت الأرض مضيئة، فينتج [كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كانت الأرض مضيئة]. ويركب القياس من مقدماته على الوجه الذي وجب تركيبه عليه من الإتيان بوصف جامع بين طرفي الحد المكرر، وبه حصلت المقدمتان إحداهما مشتملة على موضوع النتيجة أو مقدمها، والأخرى على محمولها أو تاليها، ومن اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط في الاقتراني كما سيأتي. ثم ترتب المقدمات بأن تقدم الصغرى منها وهي المشتملة على موضوع النتيجة، أو مقدمها، على الكبرى وهي المشتملة على محمولها أو تاليها، ويكون ذلك على الوجه الخاص ككون الصغرى موجبة، والكبرى كلية في الشكل الأول مثلاً. ويصح استعمال مقدمات صحيحة، من حيث النظم فلا استعمال مقدمتين سالبتين ولا مقدمتين جزئيتين، إذ لا إنتاج لسالبتين أو جزئيتين، ومن جهة المادة بأن كانتا كاذبتين أو إحداهما كاذبة. ويجب اختبار المقدمات بالاستدلال عليها إن كانت نظرية هل هي يقينية أو لا.
Divisions of Qiyas: Qiyas is two divisions: first, conjunctive (iqtirani), and second, conditional (sharti). The conjunctive qiyas is named conjunctive due to the conjunction of the terms in it and their not being separated by an exception particle, as in our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' And the conjunctive qiyas is that which indicates the conclusion potentially, because its parts are scattered in it. Do you not see that our saying 'The world is changing and everything changing is originated' indicates the conclusion, which is 'The world is originated,' but potentially, meaning that its parts are scattered in it, because its subject is the subject of the minor and its predicate is the predicate of the major. And the conjunctive qiyas is composed only of categorical propositions, and conditional propositions are not used in it. Thus said the versifier, but this is a weak opinion, and the correct is that the conjunctive qiyas is composed of categorical propositions as previously, and of conditional propositions like our saying: 'Whenever the sun is shining, then day exists, and whenever day exists, then the earth is illuminated,' producing 'Whenever the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.' And the qiyas is composed from its premises in the manner that it must be composed, by bringing a comprehensive description between the two ends of the repeated term, and by it the two premises are obtained: one containing the subject of the conclusion or its antecedent, and the other its predicate or its consequent, and by the subsumption of the minor under the middle in the conjunctive as will come. Then the premises are arranged by advancing the minor, which contains the subject of the conclusion or its antecedent, before the major, which contains its predicate or its consequent, and that is in a special manner like the minor being affirmative and the major being universal in the first figure, for example. And it is valid to use sound premises, in terms of structure: so no use of two negative premises nor two particular premises, for there is no production from two negatives or two particulars; and in terms of material: if they are both false or one of them false. And it is necessary to test the premises by inference upon them, if they are theoretical, whether they are certain or not.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage classifies qiyas into two types: iqtirani (conjunctive) and sharti (conditional). It defines iqtirani as one where the terms are conjoined without an exception particle, and it indicates the conclusion potentially (bil-quwwa), meaning the parts of the conclusion are scattered across the premises. The author then addresses a disagreement: the versifier (nazim) claimed that iqtirani is composed only of categorical propositions, but the author argues this is weak (marjuh) and states the correct view that iqtirani can also be composed of conditional propositions, giving the example of a syllogism about the sun and day. The passage then outlines the conditions for composing a valid iqtirani: the middle term must be repeated, one premise contains the subject of the conclusion (minor) and the other the predicate (major), the minor is placed before the major, and certain formal conditions apply (e.g., minor affirmative, major universal in the first figure). Materially, premises must be sound (true) and formally, two negatives or two particulars do not yield a conclusion. Finally, theoretical premises must be checked for certainty. This passage guards against the misconception that iqtirani is limited to categorical propositions and emphasizes both formal and material validity.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني (al-qiyas al-iqtirani): A syllogism where the terms are conjoined without an exception particle, indicating the conclusion potentially; can be composed of categorical or conditional propositions. [Note: Some earlier logicians restricted iqtirani to categoricals; the author here accepts conditionals, reflecting a broader later usage.]
القياس الشرطي (al-qiyas al-sharti): The other division of qiyas, not elaborated here but contrasted with iqtirani. [Note: No significant shift; it is the standard term for conditional syllogisms.]
الحد المكرر (al-hadd al-mukarrar): The repeated term (middle term) that appears in both premises to connect them. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
الصغرى (al-sughra): The minor premise, which contains the subject of the conclusion (or antecedent in conditionals). [Note: Standard usage.]
الكبرى (al-kubra): The major premise, which contains the predicate of the conclusion (or consequent). [Note: Standard usage.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) If Zayd is a human, then Zayd is an animal; Zayd is a human, therefore Zayd is an animal.
D) Either the world is originated or it is eternal; it is not originated, therefore it is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Al-qiyas al-iqtirani is defined by the conjunction of terms without an exception particle, and it can be composed of both categorical and conditional propositions.

The Syllogism

The Three Terms and Dependence of Conclusion on Premises
Explains how truth of conclusion depends on premises, and defines the three terms: asghar, akbar, awsat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three terms (asghar, akbar, awsat) in a categorical syllogism and determine whether the truth of the conclusion is necessary or accidental based on the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion's truth depends on the premises; the middle term is the tool that connects the smaller term to the larger.

In this passage, the author explains two foundational principles of syllogistic reasoning: first, that the truth of the conclusion hinges on the truth of the premises; second, that every categorical syllogism contains three terms—smaller, larger, and middle—which must be correctly identified and subsumed. Understanding these concepts is essential for evaluating the validity and soundness of any argument in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion of the syllogism
Find the statement that the syllogism aims to prove. In the author's examples, the conclusion is 'The world is originated' or 'Every human is a hee-hawer'.
2
Extract the subject (asghar) and predicate (akbar) from the conclusion
The subject is the smaller term, the predicate is the larger term. In 'The world is originated', asghar = 'the world', akbar = 'originated'.
3
Locate the middle term (awsat)
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the valid example, 'changing' is the middle term.
4
Verify subsumption
Check that the asghar is subsumed under the awsat in the minor premise, and the awsat under the akbar in the major premise. This ensures the connection.
5
Assess truth dependence
If both premises are true, the conclusion is necessarily true. If any premise is false, the conclusion may be false or accidentally true. Use the author's test: is the truth consistent?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النتيجة تأتي بحسب المقدمات، فإن كانت المقدمات صحيحة صادقة كانت النتيجة صادقة، وإن كانت المقدمات فاسدة أو كاذبة لم يلزم صدق النتيجة بل تضطرب فتصدق تارة وتكذب أخرى. مثلاً: إذا قلنا: [العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث]، فهذا قياس صحيح، مقدمتاه صادقتان فنتيجته كذلك. وإن قلت كل إنسان فرس وكل فرس صهال]، فهو قياس كاذب، لكذب إحدى المقدمتين، فلا يلزم صدق النتيجة بل تكذب تارة كهذا المثال فإن نتيجته كل إنسان صَهَّال]، وهي كاذبة، وتصدق تارة كما لو أبدلت الكبرى بقولك كل فرس ناطق، فإن نتيجته كل إنسان ناطق وهي صادقة، لكن صدقها اتفاقي. وفي القياس يجب اندراج المقدمة الصغرى في المقدمة الكبرى، وذلك باندراج أصغرها وهو موضوعه المطلوب في أوسط الكبرى، مثلاً إذا قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم]، فالأصغر هو [إنسان]، وقد اندرج في [الحيوان] لينسحب عليه حكمه. والمقدمة الصغرى هي المقدمة ذات الحد الأصغر الذي هو موضوع النتيجة. والمقدمة الكبرى هي المشتملة على الحد الأكبر الذي هو محمول النتيجة ، كقولنا في المثال السابق وكل حيوان جسم] فإنها مشتملة على الحد الأكبر، وهو [جسم]، الذي يكون محمولاً في النتيجة.
وسمي موضوع النتيجة أصغر لأنه أقل أفراداً غالباً من محمولها الذي سمي أكبر لكثرة أفراده، وسمي كل منهما حداً، لأنه طرف القضية وعند القياس، يندرج الحد الأصغر في مفهوم الأكبر بسبب اندراجه في الأوسط، ويلغى الحد الأوسط الإنتاج، والحد الأوسط هو الحد المكرر في المقدمتين، يترك عند الإنتاج، فهو كالآلة يؤتى به عند الاحتياج إليه في التوصل إلى المطلوب، ويترك عند حصوله.
عند
(1) وهذا هو مقصودهم بالاختلاف الموجب للعقم، فالقضيّة العقيمة هى التى لا يقطع بصدقها دائماً، بل تصدق أحياناً وتكذب أحياناً أخرى، فعندئذ لا يكون صدقها مطرداً فلا تصلح قاعدة عامة، وحين لا تكون قاعدة مطردة فلا يعدو صدقها أن يكون اتفاقياً لا قانونياً.
(2) أى طرف قضيّته، لأنّ الحد الأصغر طرف القضية الصغرى، والأكبر طرف القضيّة الكبرى. كما أن الحدين طرفا النتيجة.
And know that the conclusion comes according to the premises: if the premises are correct and true, the conclusion is true; if the premises are corrupt or false, the truth of the conclusion does not necessarily follow, but rather it fluctuates, being true sometimes and false at other times. For example, if we say 'The world is changing and every changing thing is originated,' this is a correct syllogism, its two premises are true, so its conclusion is likewise. And if you say 'Every human is a horse and every horse is a hee-hawer,' it is a false syllogism, due to the falsity of one of the two premises, so the truth of the conclusion does not follow; rather it is false sometimes, like this example, for its conclusion is 'Every human is a hee-hawer,' which is false, and it is true sometimes, as if you replace the major premise with your statement 'Every horse is a speaker,' then its conclusion is 'Every human is a speaker,' which is true, but its truth is accidental. And in the syllogism, the subsumption of the minor premise under the major premise is necessary, and that is by the subsumption of its lesser, which is its desired subject, under the middle of the major. For example, if we say 'Every human is an animal and every animal is a body,' then the lesser is 'human,' and it has been subsumed under 'animal' so that its ruling applies to it. And the minor premise is the premise having the smaller term, which is the subject of the conclusion. And the major premise is that which contains the larger term, which is the predicate of the conclusion, as in our previous example 'and every animal is a body' – it contains the larger term, which is 'body,' which is the predicate in the conclusion. And the subject of the conclusion is called 'smaller' because it is usually fewer in individuals than its predicate, which is called 'larger' due to the abundance of its individuals. And each of them is called a 'term' because it is an end of the proposition. And in the syllogism, the smaller term becomes subsumed under the concept of the larger due to its subsumption under the middle, and the middle term is omitted in the production. And the middle term is the term repeated in the two premises; it is left aside in the production. Thus it is like a tool brought when needed to reach the desired, and left when it is obtained. (1) And this is what they mean by the difference that causes barrenness: the barren proposition is that whose truth is not always certain, but it is true sometimes and false at other times. Then its truth is not consistent, so it is not suitable as a general rule. And when it is not a consistent rule, its truth is nothing but accidental, not lawful. (2) That is, an end of its proposition, because the smaller term is an end of the minor proposition, and the larger is an end of the major proposition, just as the two terms are ends of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between premises and conclusion, and introduces the three terms of a syllogism. The author begins by stating that the truth of the conclusion depends on the premises. If both premises are true, the conclusion is true; if one or both are false, the conclusion may be false or accidentally true. An example is given: 'Every human is a horse and every horse is a speaker' yields 'Every human is a speaker,' which is true (since some humans speak), but this truth is accidental because the premises are false. This illustrates the concept of a 'barren' proposition (عقيم) explained in the footnote: a proposition that is not consistently true and thus cannot serve as a general rule. Next, the author explains the necessity of subsumption: the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise through the middle term. Using the example 'Every human is an animal and every animal is a body,' the smaller term (human) is subsumed under the middle term (animal), which then connects to the larger term (body). The minor premise contains the smaller term (subject of the conclusion), and the major premise contains the larger term (predicate of the conclusion). The smaller term is called 'smaller' because it usually has fewer individuals than the larger term, which is more extensive. The middle term is the repeated term in both premises; it is used as a tool to connect the smaller and larger and is omitted from the conclusion. The footnote explains that the smaller and larger are called ends of their respective propositions, just as they are ends of the conclusion.

Key Terms
حد أصغر
حد أكبر
حد أوسط
قضية عقيمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Every human is a horse, and every horse is a speaker,' why is the conclusion 'Every human is a speaker' considered accidentally true?
A) Because the conclusion is false.
B) Because the premises are true.
C) Because the conclusion is true but the premises are false, so the truth is not necessary.
D) Because the syllogism is invalid in form.

Key Takeaway
The three terms (asghar, akbar, awsat) are the backbone of categorical syllogisms; the middle term must be present and properly subsumed for a valid connection, and the truth of the conclusion is guaranteed only when both premises are true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the three terms and verify the subsumption. Then suppose the minor premise is false (nabidh is not an intoxicant). What can you say about the conclusion?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism from the following: 'All acts of worship are means to taqwa. Fasting is an act of worship. Therefore, fasting is a means to taqwa.' Identify asghar, akbar, and awsat.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Conclusion Truth and Premise Truth
Explains truth dependence of conclusion on premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conclusion is necessarily true or only accidentally true based on the truth of its premises.

Core Idea
True premises guarantee a true conclusion; false premises do not force falsehood.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational principle of syllogistic logic: the dependence of the conclusion on the premises. The passage shows that if premises are true, the conclusion must be true; if premises are false, the conclusion may be true or false. This understanding is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle
Read the opening statement: the conclusion follows the premises. If premises are sound and true, the conclusion is true. If premises are corrupt or false, the conclusion is not necessarily false but fluctuates.
This is a key distinction between necessary truth and accidental truth.
2
Analyze the Valid Example
Examine the example: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.' Both premises are true, so the conclusion is necessarily true.
This example is taken from Kalam, the Islamic theological tradition.
3
Analyze the False Example and Accidental Truth
Consider the false syllogism: 'Every human is a horse; every horse is a neighing thing; therefore every human is a neighing thing.' One premise is false, so the conclusion is false. But if you replace the major premise with 'every horse is a speaking thing', the conclusion becomes true accidentally.
The truth is not essential but accidental; it does not follow necessarily.
4
Practice with a New Example
Construct a syllogism on an Islamic topic where one premise is false but the conclusion happens to be true. Verify that the conclusion is not guaranteed.
Example: 'Every animal is a plant; every horse is an animal; therefore every horse is a plant' – false, but if you replace 'plant' with 'living being', the conclusion may be true accidentally.

Source Text

واعلم أن النتيجة تأتي بحسب المقدمات، فإن كانت المقدمات صحيحة صادقة كانت النتيجة صادقة، وإن كانت المقدمات فاسدة أو كاذبة لم يلزم صدق النتيجة بل تضطرب فتصدق تارة وتكذب أخرى. مثلاً: إذا قلنا: العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث، فهذا قياس صحيح، مقدمتاه صادقتان فنتيجته كذلك. وإن قلت كل إنسان فرس وكل فرس صهال، فهو قياس كاذب، لكذب إحدى المقدمتين، فلا يلزم صدق النتيجة بل تكذب تارة كهذا المثال فإن نتيجته كل إنسان صهال، وهي كاذبة، وتصدق تارة كما لو أبدلت الكبرى بقولك كل فرس ناطق، فإن نتيجته كل إنسان ناطق وهي صادقة، لكن صدقها اتفاقي. وفي القياس يجب اندراج المقدمة الصغرى في المقدمة الكبرى، وذلك باندراج أصغرها وهو موضوعه المطلوب في أوسط الكبرى، مثلاً إذا قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم، فالأصغر هو إنسان، وقد اندرج في الحيوان لينسحب عليه حكمه. والمقدمة الصغرى هي المقدمة ذات الحد الأصغر الذي هو موضوع النتيجة. والمقدمة الكبرى هي المشتملة على الحد الأكبر الذي هو محمول النتيجة، كقولنا في المثال السابق وكل حيوان جسم فإنها مشتملة على الحد الأكبر، وهو جسم، الذي يكون محمولاً في النتيجة. وسمي موضوع النتيجة أصغر لأنه أقل أفراداً غالباً من محمولها الذي سمي أكبر لكثرة أفراده، وسمي كل منهما حداً، لأنه طرف القضية وعند القياس، يندرج الحد الأصغر في مفهوم الأكبر بسبب اندراجه في الأوسط، ويلغى الحد الأوسط الإنتاج، والحد الأوسط هو الحد المكرر في المقدمتين، يترك عند الإنتاج، فهو كالآلة يؤتى به عند الاحتياج إليه في التوصل إلى المطلوب، ويترك عند حصوله. وهذا هو مقصودهم بالاختلاف الموجب للعقم، فالقضيّة العقيمة هى التى لا يقطع بصدقها دائماً، بل تصدق أحياناً وتكذب أحياناً أخرى، فعندئذ لا يكون صدقها مطرداً فلا تصلح قاعدة عامة، وحين لا تكون قاعدة مطردة فلا يعدو صدقها أن يكون اتفاقياً لا قانونياً. أى طرف قضيّته، لأنّ الحد الأصغر طرف القضية الصغرى، والأكبر طرف القضيّة الكبرى. كما أن الحدين طرفا النتيجة.
Know that the conclusion comes according to the premises. If the premises are sound and true, the conclusion is true. If the premises are corrupt or false, the truth of the conclusion is not necessary; rather it fluctuates, sometimes true and sometimes false. For example: if we say: 'The world is changing and every changing thing is temporally originated' – this is a valid syllogism, its two premises are true, so its conclusion is likewise. And if you say: 'Every human is a horse and every horse is a neighing thing' – it is a false syllogism, due to the falsity of one of the premises, so the truth of the conclusion is not necessary; rather it is false sometimes, like this example, for its conclusion is 'Every human is a neighing thing', which is false; and sometimes true, as if you substituted the major premise by saying 'Every horse is a speaking thing', for its conclusion is 'Every human is a speaking thing', which is true, but its truth is accidental. In the syllogism, the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise, by subsumption of its minor term, which is the subject of the desired conclusion, under the middle term of the major premise. For example, if we say: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is a body' – the minor term is 'human', and it is subsumed under 'animal' so that its ruling applies to it. The minor premise is the premise containing the minor term, which is the subject of the conclusion. The major premise is the one containing the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion, as in our previous example 'and every animal is a body' – it contains the major term, which is 'body', which becomes the predicate in the conclusion. The subject of the conclusion is called 'minor' because it is usually fewer in individuals than its predicate, which is called 'major' due to the abundance of its individuals. Each of them is called a 'term' because it is an extremity of the proposition. And during the syllogism, the minor term is subsumed under the concept of the major due to its subsumption under the middle, and the middle term is eliminated for production. The middle term is the term repeated in the two premises; it is left when producing, so it is like a tool brought when needed to reach the desired conclusion, and left when it is attained. This is what they mean by 'the variation causing sterility' – the sterile proposition is that whose truth is not always certain, but is sometimes true and sometimes false; thus its truth is not consistent, so it is not suitable as a general rule, and when it is not a consistent rule, its truth is only accidental, not lawful. i.e., an extremity of its proposition, because the minor term is an extremity of the minor premise, and the major term is an extremity of the major premise, just as the two terms are extremities of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the nature of the syllogism, its validity conditions, and its terminological components. The author begins by stating that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises: if premises are true, the conclusion is true; if premises are false, the conclusion may be true or false accidentally, not necessarily. He gives two examples: a valid syllogism with true premises (the world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated) yields a true conclusion, and an invalid syllogism with one false premise (every human is a horse, every horse is neighing) yields a false conclusion, but if the major premise is replaced with a true one (every horse is speaking), the conclusion becomes true but only accidentally, not necessarily. The passage then explains the foundational requirement of a syllogism: the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise, meaning the minor term (subject of the conclusion) must be included under the middle term of the major premise. It defines the minor term as the subject of the conclusion, the major term as the predicate of the conclusion, and the middle term as the term repeated in both premises. The minor term is called 'minor' because it usually has fewer individuals than the major term, and the major term is 'major' due to its broader extension. The middle term is the tool that enables the deduction; it is used in the premises but eliminated in the conclusion. Finally, the author clarifies the concept of a 'sterile proposition' (al-qadiyya al-'aqima), which is a proposition that is not always true; its truth is not consistent and thus cannot serve as a general rule. This section is foundational for understanding syllogistic structure in Islamic logic.

The truth of the conclusion is guaranteed only when both premises are true; false premises leave the conclusion uncertain.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Identifying the Three Syllogistic Terms
Defines minor, major, and middle terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, the major is the predicate, and the middle is the repeated term that links them.

Every categorical syllogism consists of three terms: minor, major, and middle. In this lesson, we learn how to locate these terms using examples from the passage. This skill is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic law and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Conclusion
Find the conclusion (natīja) of the syllogism. It is usually the statement that is being proved. For example, in 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore every human is a body', the conclusion is 'Every human is a body'.
The conclusion is often introduced by 'therefore' (إذن).
2
Identify the Subject and Predicate of the Conclusion
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (ḥad aṣghar). The predicate is the major term (ḥad akbar). In 'Every human is a body', 'human' is the minor term, 'body' is the major term.
The passage says the minor term is usually fewer in individuals than the major term.
3
Find the Middle Term
The middle term (ḥad awsaṭ) is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the example, 'animal' appears in both premises and is absent from the conclusion.
The middle term is the linking tool; it is eliminated when producing the conclusion.
4
Label the Premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (muqaddima sughrā). The premise containing the major term is the major premise (muqaddima kubrā). Check: 'Every human is an animal' is the minor premise (contains 'human'); 'Every animal is a body' is the major premise (contains 'body').
5
Verify Subsumption
Ensure that the minor term is subsumed under the middle term in the minor premise, and the middle term is subsumed under the major term in the major premise. This guarantees the conclusion follows.
The passage states: 'the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise, by subsumption of its minor term under the middle term of the major premise.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Know that the conclusion comes according to the premises. If the premises are sound and true, the conclusion is true. If the premises are corrupt or false, the truth of the conclusion is not necessary; rather it fluctuates, sometimes true and sometimes false. For example: if we say: 'The world is changing and every changing thing is temporally originated' – this is a valid syllogism, its two premises are true, so its conclusion is likewise. For example, if we say: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is a body' – the minor term is 'human', and it is subsumed under 'animal' so that its ruling applies to it. And during the syllogism, the minor term is subsumed under the concept of the major due to its subsumption under the middle, and the middle term is eliminated for production. The middle term is the term repeated in the two premises; it is left when producing, so it is like a tool brought when needed to reach the desired conclusion, and left when it is attained. This is what they mean by 'the variation causing sterility' – the sterile proposition is that whose truth is not always certain, but is sometimes true and sometimes false; thus its truth is not consistent, so it is not suitable as a general rule, and when it is not a consistent rule, its truth is only accidental, not lawful. i.e., an extremity of its proposition, because the minor term is an extremity of the minor premise, and the major term is an extremity of the major premise, just as the two terms are extremities of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the nature of the syllogism, its validity conditions, and its terminological components. The author begins by stating that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises: if premises are true, the conclusion is true; if premises are false, the conclusion may be true or false accidentally, not necessarily. He gives two examples: a valid syllogism with true premises (the world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated) yields a true conclusion, and an invalid syllogism with one false premise (every human is a horse, every horse is neighing) yields a false conclusion, but if the major premise is replaced with a true one (every horse is speaking), the conclusion becomes true but only accidentally, not necessarily. The passage then explains the foundational requirement of a syllogism: the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise, meaning the minor term (subject of the conclusion) must be included under the middle term of the major premise. It defines the minor term as the subject of the conclusion, the major term as the predicate of the conclusion, and the middle term as the term repeated in both premises. The minor term is called 'minor' because it usually has fewer individuals than the major term, and the major term is 'major' due to its broader extension. The middle term is the tool that enables the deduction; it is used in the premises but eliminated in the conclusion. Finally, the author clarifies the concept of a 'sterile proposition' (al-qadiyya al-'aqima), which is a proposition that is not always true; its truth is not consistent and thus cannot serve as a general rule. This section is foundational for understanding syllogistic structure in Islamic logic.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Subsumption Condition and the Middle Term
Explains subsumption condition and role of middle term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the minor term must be subsumed under the major via the middle term for a valid categorical syllogism, and identify violations.

Core Idea
Subsumption of the minor under the middle, and the middle under the major, is the logical mechanism that forces the conclusion.

A valid categorical syllogism requires a specific relationship: the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise through the middle term. This lesson unpacks the concept of subsumption (indiraj) and how the middle term functions as a tool that is eventually discarded. Understanding this is key to constructing sound arguments in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Three Terms
Review the definitions: minor term (subject of conclusion), major term (predicate of conclusion), middle term (repeated in premises). Example: S=human, M=animal, P=body.
2
Understand Subsumption (Indiraj)
The minor term must be included (indiraj) under the middle term in the minor premise. In the example, 'human' is included under 'animal'. The middle term must be included under the major term in the major premise: 'animal' is included under 'body'. This chain of inclusion ensures the minor is included under the major.
The passage says: 'the minor term is subsumed under the concept of the major due to its subsumption under the middle.'
3
Visualize the Chain
Draw the chain: S ⊂ M ⊂ P. If S is part of M, and M is part of P, then S must be part of P. This is the essence of the first figure.
This is analogous to the transitivity of inclusion in set theory.
4
Recognize the Middle Term as a Tool
The middle term is present in both premises but is eliminated in the conclusion. It is like a scaffolding used to build the conclusion and then removed. The passage says: 'it is like a tool brought when needed and left when attained.'
If the middle term appears in the conclusion, the syllogism is invalid or redundant.
5
Test for Violations
Check a syllogism where the minor is not subsumed under the middle, e.g., 'Every human is a mammal; every horse is a mammal; therefore every human is a horse.' Here the middle term 'mammal' does not link the minor to the major; the syllogism is invalid.
The middle term must be the same in both premises and must relate properly to minor and major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النتيجة تأتي بحسب المقدمات، فإن كانت المقدمات صحيحة صادقة كانت النتيجة صادقة، وإن كانت المقدمات فاسدة أو كاذبة لم يلزم صدق النتيجة بل تضطرب فتصدق تارة وتكذب أخرى. مثلاً: إذا قلنا: العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث، فهذا قياس صحيح، مقدمتاه صادقتان فنتيجته كذلك. وإن قلت كل إنسان فرس وكل فرس صهال، فهو قياس كاذب، لكذب إحدى المقدمتين، فلا يلزم صدق النتيجة بل تكذب تارة كهذا المثال فإن نتيجته كل إنسان صهال، وهي كاذبة، وتصدق تارة كما لو أبدلت الكبرى بقولك كل فرس ناطق، فإن نتيجته كل إنسان ناطق وهي صادقة، لكن صدقها اتفاقي. وفي القياس يجب اندراج المقدمة الصغرى في المقدمة الكبرى، وذلك باندراج أصغرها وهو موضوعه المطلوب في أوسط الكبرى، مثلاً إذا قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم، فالأصغر هو إنسان، وقد اندرج في الحيوان لينسحب عليه حكمه. والمقدمة الصغرى هي المقدمة ذات الحد الأصغر الذي هو موضوع النتيجة. والمقدمة الكبرى هي المشتملة على الحد الأكبر الذي هو محمول النتيجة، كقولنا في المثال السابق وكل حيوان جسم فإنها مشتملة على الحد الأكبر، وهو جسم، الذي يكون محمولاً في النتيجة. وسمي موضوع النتيجة أصغر لأنه أقل أفراداً غالباً من محمولها الذي سمي أكبر لكثرة أفراده، وسمي كل منهما حداً، لأنه طرف القضية وعند القياس، يندرج الحد الأصغر في مفهوم الأكبر بسبب اندراجه في الأوسط، ويلغى الحد الأوسط الإنتاج، والحد الأوسط هو الحد المكرر في المقدمتين، يترك عند الإنتاج، فهو كالآلة يؤتى به عند الاحتياج إليه في التوصل إلى المطلوب، ويترك عند حصوله. وهذا هو مقصودهم بالاختلاف الموجب للعقم، فالقضيّة العقيمة هى التى لا يقطع بصدقها دائماً، بل تصدق أحياناً وتكذب أحياناً أخرى، فعندئذ لا يكون صدقها مطرداً فلا تصلح قاعدة عامة، وحين لا تكون قاعدة مطردة فلا يعدو صدقها أن يكون اتفاقياً لا قانونياً. أى طرف قضيّته، لأنّ الحد الأصغر طرف القضية الصغرى، والأكبر طرف القضيّة الكبرى. كما أن الحدين طرفا النتيجة.
Know that the conclusion comes according to the premises. If the premises are sound and true, the conclusion is true. If the premises are corrupt or false, the truth of the conclusion is not necessary; rather it fluctuates, sometimes true and sometimes false. For example: if we say: 'The world is changing and every changing thing is temporally originated' – this is a valid syllogism, its two premises are true, so its conclusion is likewise. And if you say: 'Every human is a horse and every horse is a neighing thing' – it is a false syllogism, due to the falsity of one of the premises, so the truth of the conclusion is not necessary; rather it is false sometimes, like this example, for its conclusion is 'Every human is a neighing thing', which is false; and sometimes true, as if you substituted the major premise by saying 'Every horse is a speaking thing', for its conclusion is 'Every human is a speaking thing', which is true, but its truth is accidental. In the syllogism, the minor premise must be subsumed under the major premise, by subsumption of its minor term, which is the subject of the desired conclusion, under the middle term of the major premise. For example, if we say: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is a body' – the minor term is 'human', and it is subsumed under 'animal' so that its ruling applies to it. The minor premise is the premise containing the minor term, which is the subject of the conclusion. The major premise is the one containing the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion, as in our previous example 'and every animal is a body' – it contains the major term, which is 'body', which becomes the predicate in the conclusion. The subject of the conclusion is called 'minor' because it is usually fewer in individuals than its predicate, which is called 'major' due to the abundance of its individuals. Each of them is called a 'term' because it is an extremity of the proposition. And during the syllogism, the minor term is subsumed under the concept of the major due to its subsumption under the middle, and the middle term is eliminated for production. The middle term is the term repeated in the two premises; it is left when producing, so it is like a tool brought when needed to reach the desired conclusion, and left when it is attained. This is what they mean by 'the variation causing sterility' – the sterile proposition is that whose truth is not always certain, but is sometimes true and sometimes false; thus its truth is not consistent, so it is not suitable as a general rule, and when it is not a consistent rule, its truth is only accidental, not lawful. i.e., an extremity of its proposition, because the minor term is an extremity of the minor premise, and the major term is an extremity of the major premise, just as the two terms are extremities of the conclusion.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures Based on Middle Term Position
Defines the four figures by the middle term's position in the premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism by locating the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure is defined only by the middle term's position in the premises.

The logicians realized that the arrangement of premises affects the validity of a syllogism. The key factor is the position of the middle term (ḥad awsaṭ) – the term repeated in both premises. In this lesson, we learn the four possible arrangements, called figures (ashkāl). Mastering these is essential to understanding how to produce valid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the term that appears in both premises. This is the middle term (ḥad awsaṭ). Ignore quantifiers (aswār) like 'all' or 'some'; they do not affect the figure.
For example, in 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden', the middle term is 'intoxicant'.
2
Locate the middle term in the minor premise
The minor premise is the one with the subject of the conclusion. In our example, 'every nabidh is intoxicant' is the minor premise. Here 'intoxicant' is the predicate.
Remember: The minor premise usually comes first in standard order.
3
Locate the middle term in the major premise
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. In our example, 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is the major premise. Here 'intoxicant' is the subject.
The major premise is the other premise.
4
Match the positions to a figure
If the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it is the first figure. For predicate in both, it is the second. For subject in both, it is the third. For subject in minor and predicate in major, it is the fourth. Our example matches the first figure.
The fourth figure is the converse of the first; it is less natural but still valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فضل في الأشكال إن القياس الذي قلنا عنه إنه عبارة عن قول، وهو قضايا مرتبة بحيث تنتج لذاتها قولاً آخر، تتغير بعض أحكامه عند تغير القضايا التي يتألف منها؛ فإننا قد عرفنا أن القضايا أنواع مختلفة، فمنها المسورة كلياً وجزئياً، ومنها الموجبة ومنها السالبة ويختلف أيضاً من حيث طريقة ترتيب القضايا بعضها مع بعض ويؤثر على قوة إنتاجه عوامل أخر كقوة كل قضية بالنسبة لصدقها، أي مطابقتها مع الواقع أو عدم مطابقتها، وقوة مطابقتها، فإن القضية قد تكون ظنية وقد تكون قطعية. ولهذه الأسباب كلها ولغيرها، فقد اهتم المناطقة بالشكل الذي يتألف منه القياس، من الحيثيات السابقة، وضبطوا كافة أوضاعه ليتمكنوا من التحكم بكيفية الإنتاج. وهذا الفصل نشرح فيه بعض هذه الهيئات. قال: الشكل عند هؤلاء الناس يطلق عن قضيتي قياس من غير أن تعتبر الأسوار إذ ذاك بالضرب له يشار وللمقدمات أشكال فقط أربعة بحسب الحد الأوسط حمل بصغرى وضعها بكبرى يدعى بشكل أول ويدرى وحمله في الكل ثانياً عرف ووضعه في الكل ثالثا ألف ورابع الأشكال عكس الأول وهي على الترتيب في التكمل فحيث عن هذا النظام يعدل ففاسد النظام أما الأول فشرطه الإيجاب في صغراه وأن ترى كلية كبراه والثان أن يختلفا في الكيف مع كلية الكبرى له شرط وقع والثالث الإيجاب في صغراهما وأن ترى كلية إحداهما
A Section on the Figures. Indeed the syllogism which we said is an expression composed of propositions arranged such that they produce by themselves another proposition – some of its rulings change when the propositions composing it change. For we have known that propositions are of various types: some are quantified universally or partially, some affirmative, some negative, and they also differ in the manner of arranging propositions with each other. Other factors affect the strength of its production, such as the strength of each proposition regarding its truth – i.e., its correspondence with reality or lack thereof, and the strength of its correspondence. For a proposition may be probable or certain. For all these reasons and others, the logicians have paid attention to the figure (shakl) that composes the syllogism, from the aforementioned aspects, and they have regulated all its positions to be able to control the manner of production. In this chapter we explain some of these configurations. He said: The figure, according to these people, is defined by the two premises of the syllogism, without considering the quantifiers; for at that time, by the mood, it is indicated. The premises have only four figures according to the middle term: (middle) predicate in the minor and subject in the major is called the first figure, and is known; its being predicate in both is known as the second; its being subject in both is the third, known; and the fourth figure is the converse of the first. They are in order of completion. So wherever one deviates from this system, the system is corrupt. As for the first, its condition is affirmation in its minor, and you see its major universal. The second is that they differ in quality, with the universality of the major being a condition for it that occurs. The third is affirmation in its (the minor's) two premises, and you see one of them universal.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a dual section: first, a prose introduction explaining why logicians study the figures (ashkāl) of the syllogism – because the truth and arrangement of premises affect the strength and validity of the conclusion. Then it presents a matn (poetic text) that defines the four figures based on the position of the middle term in the premises. The matn states that a figure is defined by the two premises without considering quantifiers (aswar); the mood (ḍarb) is what indicates the specific type. There are exactly four figures, determined by whether the middle term is a predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major (first figure), predicate in both (second), subject in both (third), or the converse of the first (fourth). The order of figures is arranged from most perfect (first) to less perfect (fourth). The matn then gives conditions for the first three figures: the first requires the minor premise to be affirmative and the major universal; the second requires the two premises to differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major must be universal; the third requires both premises to be affirmative and one of them universal. These conditions ensure that the syllogism produces a necessary conclusion. The passage is a foundational mnemonic for students to memorize the figures and their validity conditions.

Key Terms
شكل
حد أوسط
شكل أول
شكل ثان
شكل ثالث
شكل رابع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Subject in both premises.
B) Predicate in both premises.
C) Predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
D) Subject in the minor and predicate in the major.

Key Takeaway
There are only four figures, defined by the middle term's position in the premises; the first figure is the most natural and productive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the figure in this syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' State the middle term and its position in each premise.

The Syllogism
intermediate
