Conditions for Validity in the First Three Figures
States the conditions for the first three figures to produce a necessary conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for the first three figures to check whether a syllogism produces a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Each figure demands specific conditions – without them, no valid conclusion follows.

Knowing the figure is not enough; each figure has strict conditions for the premises to produce a necessary conclusion. The matn lists these conditions for the first three figures. In this lesson, we break down each condition and see why it is necessary. We will then practice applying them to Islamic science examples.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for the first figure
The first figure requires: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) the major premise must be universal. Check both.
Example: 'Every nabidh is intoxicant' (affirmative) and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal) – both conditions satisfied.
2
Recall the condition for the second figure
The second figure requires: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal.
If both are affirmative or both negative, no valid conclusion follows.
3
Recall the condition for the third figure
The third figure requires: (1) both premises must be affirmative, and (2) at least one premise must be universal.
If one premise is negative, the conclusion would be negative, but the third figure cannot produce a universal conclusion.
4
Apply conditions to a real example
Take a second-figure syllogism: 'No thief is honest; some servant is honest; therefore some servant is not a thief.' Here the major premise 'No thief is honest' is universal negative, the minor 'some servant is honest' is particular affirmative. They differ in quality, and the major is universal – condition satisfied.
Always identify the figure first, then check its specific conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فضل في الأشكال إن القياس الذي قلنا عنه إنه عبارة عن قول، وهو قضايا مرتبة بحيث تنتج لذاتها قولاً آخر، تتغير بعض أحكامه عند تغير القضايا التي يتألف منها؛ فإننا قد عرفنا أن القضايا أنواع مختلفة، فمنها المسورة كلياً وجزئياً، ومنها الموجبة ومنها السالبة ويختلف أيضاً من حيث طريقة ترتيب القضايا بعضها مع بعض ويؤثر على قوة إنتاجه عوامل أخر كقوة كل قضية بالنسبة لصدقها، أي مطابقتها مع الواقع أو عدم مطابقتها، وقوة مطابقتها، فإن القضية قد تكون ظنية وقد تكون قطعية. ولهذه الأسباب كلها ولغيرها، فقد اهتم المناطقة بالشكل الذي يتألف منه القياس، من الحيثيات السابقة، وضبطوا كافة أوضاعه ليتمكنوا من التحكم بكيفية الإنتاج. وهذا الفصل نشرح فيه بعض هذه الهيئات. قال: الشكل عند هؤلاء الناس يطلق عن قضيتي قياس من غير أن تعتبر الأسوار إذ ذاك بالضرب له يشار وللمقدمات أشكال فقط أربعة بحسب الحد الأوسط حمل بصغرى وضعها بكبرى يدعى بشكل أول ويدرى وحمله في الكل ثانياً عرف ووضعه في الكل ثالثا ألف ورابع الأشكال عكس الأول وهي على الترتيب في التكمل فحيث عن هذا النظام يعدل ففاسد النظام أما الأول فشرطه الإيجاب في صغراه وأن ترى كلية كبراه والثان أن يختلفا في الكيف مع كلية الكبرى له شرط وقع والثالث الإيجاب في صغراهما وأن ترى كلية إحداهما

For we have known that propositions are of various types: some are quantified universally or partially, some affirmative, some negative, and they also differ in the manner of arranging propositions with each other. Then it presents a matn (poetic text) that defines the four figures based on the position of the middle term in the premises. The matn states that a figure is defined by the two premises without considering quantifiers (aswar); the mood (ḍarb) is what indicates the specific type. There are exactly four figures, determined by whether the middle term is a predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major (first figure), predicate in both (second), subject in both (third), or the converse of the first (fourth). The order of figures is arranged from most perfect (first) to less perfect (fourth). The matn then gives conditions for the first three figures: the first requires the minor premise to be affirmative and the major universal; the second requires the two premises to differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major must be universal; the third requires both premises to be affirmative and one of them universal. These conditions ensure that the syllogism produces a necessary conclusion. The passage is a foundational mnemonic for students to memorize the figures and their validity conditions.

Key Terms
إيجاب
سلب
كلية
جزئية
كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which figure requires both premises to be affirmative?
A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The first three figures have specific conditions for the premises (quality and quantity) that must be met for a necessary conclusion to follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check the validity of this first-figure syllogism: 'Some knowledge is obligatory; every obligatory thing is rewarded; therefore some knowledge is rewarded.' Does it satisfy the conditions? If not, why?
?
Construct a valid second-figure syllogism using Islamic rulings (e.g., 'No idolatry is permissible; some practices are permissible; therefore some practices are not idolatry') and verify the conditions.

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
The Four Figures: Form Without Quantifiers
Defines the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of any categorical syllogism by identifying the position of the middle term in the premises, and distinguish form (shakl) from mood (darb).

Core Idea
Only the middle term's position matters for the figure; quantifiers and quality belong to the mood.

The passage begins by reminding us that the arrangement of premises affects syllogistic production. Logicians defined 'shakl' as the bare structure of the premises ignoring quantifiers. The text then presents the four possibilities for the middle term's location, using the symbols A, B, C. This lesson will show you how to recognize each figure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between shakl and darb
Shakl is the form of the premises without considering universal/particular or affirmative/negative; darb adds those. The poem says 'The form is called from the two premises without considering the quantifiers; then it is indicated by the mood.'
2
Identify the roles: minor term (A), middle term (B), major term (C)
The first premise has the form [A is B] (minor is subject, middle is predicate). The second premise has [B is C] (middle is subject, major is predicate) in the first figure. This is the 'natural' arrangement.
The mnemonic: middle is subject in minor and predicate in major.
3
List all four figures using the notation
Figure 1: [A B] + [B C]. Figure 2: [A B] + [C B] (middle predicate in both). Figure 3: [B A] + [B C] (middle subject in both). Figure 4: [B A] + [C B] (converse of first).
The poem says 'And the fourth is the converse of the first.'
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is haram.' Identify the middle term (intoxicant). It is subject in the minor ('every nabidh is intoxicant') and predicate in the major ('every intoxicant is haram'). That is the first figure.
We ignore quantifiers (every) and quality (affirmative) for figure identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فضل في الأشكال إن القياس الذي قلنا عنه إنه عبارة عن قول، وهو قضايا مرتبة بحيث تنتج لذاتها قولاً آخر، تتغير بعض أحكامه عند تغير القضايا التي يتألف منها؛ فإننا قد عرفنا أن القضايا أنواع مختلفة، فمنها المسورة كلياً وجزئياً، ومنها الموجبة ومنها السالبة ويختلف أيضاً من حيث طريقة ترتيب القضايا بعضها مع بعض ويؤثر على قوة إنتاجه عوامل أخر كقوة كل قضية بالنسبة لصدقها، أي مطابقتها مع الواقع أو عدم مطابقتها، وقوة مطابقتها، فإن القضية قد تكون ظنية وقد تكون قطعية. ولهذه الأسباب كلها ولغيرها، فقد اهتم المناطقة بالشكل الذي يتألف منه القياس، من الحيثيات السابقة، وضبطوا كافة أوضاعه ليتمكنوا من التحكم بكيفية الإنتاج. وهذا الفصل نشرح فيه بعض هذه الهيئات. قال:

الشكل عند هؤلاء الناس يطلق عن قضيتي قياس
من غير أن تعتبر الأسوار إذ ذاك بالضرب له يشار
وللمقدمات أشكال فقط أربعة بحسب الحد الوسط
حمل بصغراه وضعه بكبرى يدعى بشكل أول ويدرى
وحمله في الكل ثانيا عرف ووضعه في الكل ثالثا ألف
ورابع الأشكال عكس الأول وهي على الترتيب في التكمل
فحيث عن هذا النظام يعدل ففاسد النظام
أما الأول فشرطه الإيجاب في صغراه وأن ترى كلية كبراه
والثان أن يختلفا في الكيف مع كلية الكبرى له شرط وقع
والثالث الإيجاب في صغراهما وأن ترى كلية إحداهما
ورابع عدم جمع السلبين إلا بصورة فيها يستبين
صغراهما موجبة جزئية كبراهما سالبة كلية
فمنتج لأول أربعة كالثاني ثم ثالث فستة
ورابع بخمسة قد أنتجا وغير ما ذكرته لم ينتجا
وتتبع النتيجة الأخس من تلك المقدمات هكذا زكن
وهذه الأشكال بالحملي مختصة وليس بالشرطي
والحذف في بعض المقدمات أو النتيجة لعلم آت
وتنتهي إلى ضرورة لما من دور أو تسلسل قد لزما

أقول: الشكل اسم يطلق على هيئة قضيتي القياس من دون اعتبار الأسوار، وذلك باعتبار ترتيب الحد الأوسط والأكبر والأصغر في كل منهما. وأما إذا اعتبرت الأسوار في القضايا أيضاً، فإن ذلك يسمى بالضرب وسوف نبين كل ذلك في محله.

أما الأشكال فإنها وبحسب ترتيب القضايا بالاعتبار السابق أربعة أشكال فقط. فلو رمزنا للأصغر بالحرف [أ]، وللأوسط بالحرف [ب]، وللأكبر بالحرف [ج]، فإن القضية الأولى يكون صورتها كما يلي [أ هو ب]، والقضية الثانية صورتها [ب هو ج]. فالحاصل بناء على هذا الفرض وجود احتمالات أربعة هي أشكال القياس وأشكالها الصورية كما يلى:
1- [أ ب]، [ب ج]
2- [أ ب]، [ج ب]
3- [ب أ]، [ب ج]
4- [ب أ]، [ج ب]
هذه هي أشكال القياس، من دون اعتبار السور ولا الكيف، ولا أي أمر آخر.

(1) والشكل يطلق لغة على هيئة الشيء أي صورته فشبهت صورتا القياس بذلك.
A section on the forms. Indeed the syllogism which we said is a statement, and it is propositions arranged such that they produce of themselves another statement, some of its rulings change when the propositions composing it change; for we have known that propositions are of different types: some are quantified universally and partially, some affirmative and some negative, and it also differs in terms of the method of arranging the propositions with each other, and other factors affect the strength of its production, such as the strength of each proposition with respect to its truth, i.e., its correspondence to reality or lack thereof, and the strength of its correspondence; for a proposition may be probable and may be certain. For all these reasons and others, the logicians have concerned themselves with the form which constitutes the syllogism, from the aforementioned aspects, and they have defined all its positions to be able to control how production occurs. This chapter explains some of these forms. He said:

"The form according to these people is called from the two premises of a syllogism, without considering the quantifiers; then it is indicated by the mood.
The premises have only four forms according to the middle term.
(First:) middle term subject in minor and predicate in major: called first form, and it is known.
(Second:) middle term predicate in both: known as second.
(Third:) middle term subject in both: third.
(Fourth form is the converse of the first. They are in order of perfection.
So wherever one deviates from this system, his system is corrupt.
As for the first, its condition is that its minor premise be affirmative and its major premise be universal.
The second, that the two differ in quality, with the major being universal, that is a condition for it.
The third condition: minor premise affirmative and one of them universal.
Fourth: not combining two negations except in a form where it is clear: its minor premise affirmative particular, its major premise negative universal.
So the first produces four moods, the second also four, the third six, and the fourth produced five. Other than what I mentioned do not produce.
The conclusion follows the weaker of those premises, thus know.
And these forms are specific to categorical, not to conditional. And the omission of some premises or the conclusion will be explained in a coming science.
And they lead to necessity, because of the vicious circle or infinite regress that would result if not."

I say: The term 'form' is applied to the structure of the two premises of a syllogism without considering the quantifiers, that is by considering the arrangement of the middle term, the major term, and the minor term in each premise. If we also consider the quantifiers in the propositions, that is called 'mood', and we will explain all that in its place.

As for the forms, they are according to the arrangement of the premises by the previous consideration only four forms. So if we symbolize the minor by [A], the middle by [B], and the major by [C], then the first premise has the form [A is B], and the second premise has the form [B is C]. So based on this assumption, there are four possibilities, which are the forms of the syllogism and their formal shapes as follows:
1. [A B], [B C]
2. [A B], [C B]
3. [B A], [B C]
4. [B A], [C B]
These are the forms of the syllogism, without considering quantifier or quality or any other matter.

(1) The term 'form' in language is applied to the structure of a thing, i.e., its shape; so the images of the syllogism were compared to that.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of logical form (شكل) as applied to the syllogism, distinguishes it from mood (ضرب), and enumerates the four figures (أشكال) of the categorical syllogism with their validity conditions and productive moods. 

The opening prose explains why the arrangement of premises matters: syllogistic production depends on the types (quantified, affirmative/negative) and arrangement of premises. The logicians thus set out to define all possible forms. 

The matn (poem) then gives the core teaching: 'The form' is defined by the two premises ignoring quantifiers; when quantifiers are considered it's called 'mood'. There are only four figures based on the position of the middle term: 
1. First figure: middle subject in minor, predicate in major. 
2. Second figure: middle predicate in both. 
3. Third figure: middle subject in both. 
4. Fourth figure: the converse of the first (middle predicate in minor, subject in major). 
Deviating yields no valid syllogism. 
Then the poem lists conditions for each figure to produce conclusions: 
- First: minor affirmative, major universal. 
- Second: premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and major universal. 
- Third: minor affirmative, and at least one premise universal. 
- Fourth: not both negative (except one form) where minor is affirmative particular and major is negative universal. 
Then the numbers of productive moods: first figure 4, second 4, third 6, fourth 5. Others produce nothing. The conclusion follows the weaker premise (i.e., if one premise is particular and the other universal, the conclusion is particular). These forms are only for categorical syllogisms, not conditional. Omissions and the necessity of the conclusion (to avoid infinite regress or circularity) are mentioned. 

The commentary (أقول) clarifies: 'form' is the arrangement of the two premises ignoring quantifiers; if quantifiers are considered, it becomes 'mood'. Using symbols: minor (أ), middle (ب), major (ج). The first premise is [أ is ب], second is [ب is ج]. Based on possible positions of the middle term, exactly four combinations (figures) arise. The footnote adds the linguistic definition: الشكل is the shape of something; the syllogism's two premises are likened to that.

To apply this to an Islamic example: In the first figure, we can say: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram) – major premise universal affirmative; Every nabidh is an intoxicant – minor premise affirmative; Therefore, every nabidh is forbidden.' This satisfies the condition that the minor is affirmative and major universal.

Key Terms
شكل
ضرب
حد وسط
صغرى
كبرى

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of the second figure according to the passage?
A) The middle term is subject in both premises.
B) The middle term is predicate in both premises.
C) The middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
D) The middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major.

Key Takeaway
The figure of a categorical syllogism is determined solely by the position of the middle term in the two premises, without considering quantifiers or quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the figure of this syllogism from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Every khamr is intoxicant. Therefore every khamr is haram.' Determine the figure by locating the middle term (intoxicant) in each premise.
?
Rewrite the following syllogism in abstract notation using A, B, C: 'All humans are mortal. All mortals are created. So all humans are created.' Then state the figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Conditions for Valid Syllogism in Each Figure
Lists conditions for each figure and numbers of productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required for each figure to produce a valid conclusion, and identify whether a given syllogism meets those conditions.

Core Idea
Each figure has strict conditions on quantity and quality of premises; violating them yields no valid syllogism.

After presenting the four figures, the passage lists the specific conditions each figure must satisfy to yield a conclusion. Conditions concern the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the premises. Additionally, the number of productive moods for each figure is given. This lesson unpacks those rules.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four figures
We will now apply conditions to each. The poem gives them sequentially: first figure requires minor affirmative and major universal; second requires premises differ in quality and major universal; third requires minor affirmative and at least one universal; fourth requires not both negative except one case where minor affirmative particular and major negative universal.
2
Analyze the first figure condition
Condition: Minor premise must be affirmative, major premise must be universal. This prevents fallacies. For example: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (affirmative universal major) and 'Every nabidh is intoxicant' (affirmative universal minor) yields valid conclusion. But if the minor were negative or the major particular, the syllogism would be invalid.
The poem says: 'As for the first, its condition is that its minor premise be affirmative and its major premise be universal.'
3
Examine the second figure condition
Condition: The two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. For example: 'No stone is alive' (universal negative) and 'All humans are alive' (universal affirmative) differ in quality and major is universal, yielding 'No human is stone'.
The poem: 'The second, that the two differ in quality, with the major being universal.'
4
Study the third and fourth figure conditions
Third: minor affirmative, and one premise universal. Fourth: not both negative (except one subtype where minor is affirmative particular and major is negative universal). These are more permissive.
Use the poem's wording.
5
Apply to an Islamic example to test conditions
Take: 'Every khamr is intoxicant' (universal affirmative), 'Some intoxicants are forbidden' (particular affirmative). This is first figure? Check: middle term 'intoxicant' is subject in major? Actually careful. If we have 'Every khamr is intoxicant' (minor: khamr is subject, intoxicant predicate), 'Some intoxicants are forbidden' (major: intoxicant subject, forbidden predicate) → first figure. But major is particular, violating first figure condition (major must be universal). So this syllogism is invalid.
This shows why conditions matter.

To apply this to an Islamic example: In the first figure, we can say: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram) – major premise universal affirmative; Every nabidh is an intoxicant – minor premise affirmative; Therefore, every nabidh is forbidden.' This satisfies the condition that the minor is affirmative and major universal.

The Syllogism

Productive Moods and the Weaker Premise Rule
Gives number of productive moods and the weaker premise rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state how many productive moods exist for each figure and apply the principle that the conclusion follows the weaker premise.

Core Idea
The number of valid moods per figure is fixed; the conclusion always takes the quantity/quality of the weaker premise.

After presenting the conditions, the poem gives the count of productive moods for each figure: first produces 4, second 4, third 6, fourth 5. It also states a general rule: the conclusion follows the weaker premise – if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if one is negative, the conclusion is negative. This lesson explores those numbers and the practical application of the weaker premise rule.

Guided Path
1
Learn the numbers: 4, 4, 6, 5
The poem says: 'The first produces four, the second four, the third six, and the fourth five.' These are the total valid moods in each figure given the conditions.
Memorize: 1-4, 2-4, 3-6, 4-5.
2
Understand what a 'mood' is
A mood is a specific combination of quantity and quality for the two premises within a figure. For example, in the first figure, one mood is 'universal affirmative, universal affirmative' (Barbara). The conditions restrict which combinations are valid.
The poem does not list moods individually, but states the totals.
3
Apply the 'weaker premise' rule
The poem says: 'The conclusion follows the weaker of those premises.' This means if one premise is particular and the other universal, the conclusion is particular. If one is negative and the other affirmative, the conclusion is negative. The 'weaker' means the less comprehensive (particular over universal, negative over affirmative).
For example, if one premise is particular, the conclusion must be particular even if the other is universal.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider a third figure syllogism: 'Some scholars are wise' (particular affirmative), 'All scholars are humans' (universal affirmative). The minor is affirmative but particular; the major is universal. According to the rule, the conclusion should be particular because of the weaker premise: 'Some humans are wise' or 'Some wise are humans' (depending on figure). The third figure conditions require minor affirmative and one universal; here satisfied. The conclusion is particular because one premise is particular.
This shows how the rule applies.

فضل في الأشكال إن القياس الذي قلنا عنه إنه عبارة عن قول، وهو قضايا مرتبة بحيث تنتج لذاتها قولاً آخر، تتغير بعض أحكامه عند تغير القضايا التي يتألف منها؛ فإننا قد عرفنا أن القضايا أنواع مختلفة، فمنها المسورة كلياً وجزئياً، ومنها الموجبة ومنها السالبة ويختلف أيضاً من حيث طريقة ترتيب القضايا بعضها مع بعض ويؤثر على قوة إنتاجه عوامل أخر كقوة كل قضية بالنسبة لصدقها، أي مطابقتها مع الواقع أو عدم مطابقتها، وقوة مطابقتها، فإن القضية قد تكون ظنية وقد تكون قطعية. ولهذه الأسباب كلها ولغيرها، فقد اهتم المناطقة بالشكل الذي يتألف منه القياس، من الحيثيات السابقة، وضبطوا كافة أوضاعه ليتمكنوا من التحكم بكيفية الإنتاج. وهذا الفصل نشرح فيه بعض هذه الهيئات. قال:

الشكل عند هؤلاء الناس يطلق عن قضيتي قياس
من غير أن تعتبر الأسوار إذ ذاك بالضرب له يشار
وللمقدمات أشكال فقط أربعة بحسب الحد الوسط
حمل بصغراه وضعه بكبرى يدعى بشكل أول ويدرى
وحمله في الكل ثانيا عرف ووضعه في الكل ثالثا ألف
ورابع الأشكال عكس الأول وهي على الترتيب في التكمل
فحيث عن هذا النظام يعدل ففاسد النظام
أما الأول فشرطه الإيجاب في صغراه وأن ترى كلية كبراه
والثان أن يختلفا في الكيف مع كلية الكبرى له شرط وقع
والثالث الإيجاب في صغراهما وأن ترى كلية إحداهما
ورابع عدم جمع السلبين إلا بصورة فيها يستبين
صغراهما موجبة جزئية كبراهما سالبة كلية
فمنتج لأول أربعة كالثاني ثم ثالث فستة
ورابع بخمسة قد أنتجا وغير ما ذكرته لم ينتجا
وتتبع النتيجة الأخس من تلك المقدمات هكذا زكن
وهذه الأشكال بالحملي مختصة وليس بالشرطي
والحذف في بعض المقدمات أو النتيجة لعلم آت
وتنتهي إلى ضرورة لما من دور أو تسلسل قد لزما

أقول: الشكل اسم يطلق على هيئة قضيتي القياس من دون اعتبار الأسوار، وذلك باعتبار ترتيب الحد الأوسط والأكبر والأصغر في كل منهما. وأما إذا اعتبرت الأسوار في القضايا أيضاً، فإن ذلك يسمى بالضرب وسوف نبين كل ذلك في محله.

أما الأشكال فإنها وبحسب ترتيب القضايا بالاعتبار السابق أربعة أشكال فقط. فلو رمزنا للأصغر بالحرف [أ]، وللأوسط بالحرف [ب]، وللأكبر بالحرف [ج]، فإن القضية الأولى يكون صورتها كما يلي [أ هو ب]، والقضية الثانية صورتها [ب هو ج]. فالحاصل بناء على هذا الفرض وجود احتمالات أربعة هي أشكال القياس وأشكالها الصورية كما يلى:
1- [أ ب]، [ب ج]
2- [أ ب]، [ج ب]
3- [ب أ]، [ب ج]
4- [ب أ]، [ج ب]
هذه هي أشكال القياس، من دون اعتبار السور ولا الكيف، ولا أي أمر آخر.

(1) والشكل يطلق لغة على هيئة الشيء أي صورته فشبهت صورتا القياس بذلك.
A section on the forms. Indeed the syllogism which we said is a statement, and it is propositions arranged such that they produce of themselves another statement, some of its rulings change when the propositions composing it change; for we have known that propositions are of different types: some are quantified universally and partially, some affirmative and some negative, and it also differs in terms of the method of arranging the propositions with each other, and other factors affect the strength of its production, such as the strength of each proposition with respect to its truth, i.e., its correspondence to reality or lack thereof, and the strength of its correspondence; for a proposition may be probable and may be certain. For all these reasons and others, the logicians have concerned themselves with the form which constitutes the syllogism, from the aforementioned aspects, and they have defined all its positions to be able to control how production occurs. This chapter explains some of these forms. He said:

"The form according to these people is called from the two premises of a syllogism, without considering the quantifiers; then it is indicated by the mood.
The premises have only four forms according to the middle term.
(First:) middle term subject in minor and predicate in major: called first form, and it is known.
(Second:) middle term predicate in both: known as second.
(Third:) middle term subject in both: third.
(Fourth form is the converse of the first. They are in order of perfection.
So wherever one deviates from this system, his system is corrupt.
As for the first, its condition is that its minor premise be affirmative and its major premise be universal.
The second, that the two differ in quality, with the major being universal, that is a condition for it.
The third condition: minor premise affirmative and one of them universal.
Fourth: not combining two negations except in a form where it is clear: its minor premise affirmative particular, its major premise negative universal.
So the first produces four moods, the second also four, the third six, and the fourth produced five. Other than what I mentioned do not produce.
The conclusion follows the weaker of those premises, thus know.
And these forms are specific to categorical, not to conditional. And the omission of some premises or the conclusion will be explained in a coming science.
And they lead to necessity, because of the vicious circle or infinite regress that would result if not."

I say: The term 'form' is applied to the structure of the two premises of a syllogism without considering the quantifiers, that is by considering the arrangement of the middle term, the major term, and the minor term in each premise. If we also consider the quantifiers in the propositions, that is called 'mood', and we will explain all that in its place.

As for the forms, they are according to the arrangement of the premises by the previous consideration only four forms. So if we symbolize the minor by [A], the middle by [B], and the major by [C], then the first premise has the form [A is B], and the second premise has the form [B is C]. So based on this assumption, there are four possibilities, which are the forms of the syllogism and their formal shapes as follows:
1. [A B], [B C]
2. [A B], [C B]
3. [B A], [B C]
4. [B A], [C B]
These are the forms of the syllogism, without considering quantifier or quality or any other matter.

(1) The term 'form' in language is applied to the structure of a thing, i.e., its shape; so the images of the syllogism were compared to that.

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of logical form (شكل) as applied to the syllogism, distinguishes it from mood (ضرب), and enumerates the four figures (أشكال) of the categorical syllogism with their validity conditions and productive moods. 

The opening prose explains why the arrangement of premises matters: syllogistic production depends on the types (quantified, affirmative/negative) and arrangement of premises. The logicians thus set out to define all possible forms. 

The matn (poem) then gives the core teaching: 'The form' is defined by the two premises ignoring quantifiers; when quantifiers are considered it's called 'mood'. There are only four figures based on the position of the middle term: 
1. First figure: middle subject in minor, predicate in major. 
2. Second figure: middle predicate in both. 
3. Third figure: middle subject in both. 
4. Fourth figure: the converse of the first (middle predicate in minor, subject in major). 
Deviating yields no valid syllogism. 
Then the poem lists conditions for each figure to produce conclusions: 
- First: minor affirmative, major universal. 
- Second: premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and major universal. 
- Third: minor affirmative, and at least one premise universal. 
- Fourth: not both negative (except one form) where minor is affirmative particular and major is negative universal. 
Then the numbers of productive moods: first figure 4, second 4, third 6, fourth 5. Others produce nothing. The conclusion follows the weaker premise (i.e., if one premise is particular and the other universal, the conclusion is particular). These forms are only for categorical syllogisms, not conditional. Omissions and the necessity of the conclusion (to avoid infinite regress or circularity) are mentioned. 

The commentary (أقول) clarifies: 'form' is the arrangement of the two premises ignoring quantifiers; if quantifiers are considered, it becomes 'mood'. Using symbols: minor (أ), middle (ب), major (ج). The first premise is [أ is ب], second is [ب is ج]. Based on possible positions of the middle term, exactly four combinations (figures) arise. The footnote adds the linguistic definition: الشكل is the shape of something; the syllogism's two premises are likened to that.

To apply this to an Islamic example: In the first figure, we can say: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram) – major premise universal affirmative; Every nabidh is an intoxicant – minor premise affirmative; Therefore, every nabidh is forbidden.' This satisfies the condition that the minor is affirmative and major universal.

Definition of Syllogistic Figure and Its Four Types
Author defines 'shakl' and enumerates four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism by locating the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is defined solely by the arrangement of the middle term, ignoring quantifiers and quality.

In the Islamic logical tradition, understanding the figure of a syllogism is the first step to evaluating its validity. Before we consider quantifiers or quality, the bare arrangement of terms already determines the potential deductive power. This lesson introduces the definition of 'shakl' (figure) and the classification into four types, based strictly on where the middle term appears relative to the minor and major terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of 'shakl'
The passage states that 'shakl' is the form of the two premises without considering quantifiers (aswar) or quality (kayf). This means figure is purely about term order, not quantity or quality.
Contrast with 'darb' (mood) which includes quantifiers and quality.
2
Distinguish figure from mood
The author explicitly says that when quantifiers are considered, it is called 'mood' (darb), which will be treated later. This distinction is crucial for precise analysis.
3
Map the symbols: A (minor), B (middle), C (major)
The passage uses Arabic letters: أ for the minor term, ب for the middle term, ج for the major term. The first premise is always 'A is B' and the second is 'B is C', but the order can vary in the premises.
This symbolic abstraction helps focus on term positions regardless of content.
4
List the four term arrangements (figures)
Based on the positions of B (middle term) relative to A and C, exactly four possibilities exist: (1) A-B, B-C; (2) A-B, C-B; (3) B-A, B-C; (4) B-A, C-B.
Each arrangement defines a figure: first, second, third, and fourth respectively.
5
Describe each figure conceptually
The author describes: first figure has B as predicate in minor and subject in major; second has B as predicate in both; third has B as subject in both; fourth is the reverse of the first.
Memorizing these descriptions helps quickly identify figures in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: الشكل اسم يطلق على هيئة قضيتي القياس من دون اعتبار الأسوار، وذلك باعتبار ترتيب الحد الأوسط والأكبر والأصغر في كل منهما. وأما إذا اعتبرت الأسوار في القضايا أيضاً، فإن ذلك يسمى بالضرب وسوف نبين كل ذلك في محله. أما الأشكال فإنها وبحسب ترتيب القضايا بالاعتبار السابق أربعة أشكال فقط. فلو رمزنا للأصغر بالحرف [أ]، وللأوسط بالحرف [ب]، وللأكبر بالحرف [ج]، فإن القضية الأولى يكون صورتها كما يلي [أ هو ب]، والقضية الثانية صورتها [ب هو ج]. فالحاصل بناء على هذا الفرض وجود احتمالات أربعة هي أشكال القياس وأشكالها الصورية كما يلى: 1- [أ ب]، [ب ج] 2- [أب]، [ج ب] 3- [ب أ]، [ب ج] 4- [ب أ]، [ج ب] هذه هي أشكال القياس، من دون اعتبار السور ولا الكيف، ولا أي أمر آخر. وكما نرى فإن الأشكال الأربعة تنتج بحسب تكرار الحد الوسط فيه، فإن كان محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى، فهو الشكل الأول، وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث، وإن كان عكس الشكل الأول فهو الشكل الرابع.
I say: The term 'figure' is applied to the form of the two premises of a syllogism without consideration of the quantifiers, and that is according to the arrangement of the middle term, the major term, and the minor term in each of them. As for when the quantifiers are also considered in the propositions, then that is called 'mood', and we will explain all of that in its place. As for the figures, they are, according to the arrangement of the propositions by the previous consideration, only four figures. So if we symbolize the minor term with the letter [A], the middle term with the letter [B], and the major term with the letter [C], then the first proposition has the form [A is B], and the second proposition has the form [B is C]. So the result based on this assumption is the existence of four possibilities, which are the figures of the syllogism and their formal forms as follows: 1- [A B], [B C]; 2- [A B], [C B]; 3- [B A], [B C]; 4- [B A], [C B]. These are the figures of the syllogism, without consideration of the quantifier or the quality, or any other matter. And as we see, the four figures are productive according to the repetition of the middle term in them: if it is a predicate in the minor premise and a subject in the major premise, then it is the first figure; if it is a predicate in both, then it is the second figure; if it is a subject in both, then it is the third figure; and if it is the reverse of the first figure, then it is the fourth figure.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'shakl' (figure) in categorical syllogisms. The author begins by explaining that 'figure' refers to the arrangement of the three terms (minor, middle, major) across the two premises, without considering quantifiers (like 'all' or 'some') or quality (affirmative or negative). That further specification is called 'mood' (darb), which will be discussed later. He then states that there are exactly four figures possible based on the ordering of the middle term. To illustrate, he uses symbols: A for minor term, B for middle term, C for major term. The minor premise is always A is B, and the major premise is B is C, but the order of terms can change. He lists four possibilities: (1) A–B, B–C (first figure); (2) A–B, C–B (second figure); (3) B–A, B–C (third figure); (4) B–A, C–B (fourth figure). He then provides a conceptual description: the first figure has the middle term as predicate in the minor and subject in the major; the second has it as predicate in both; the third as subject in both; the fourth is the reverse of the first. This classification is foundational for understanding which syllogistic forms are valid.

Key Takeaway

Recognizing and Ranking the Four Syllogistic Figures
Author provides concrete examples of each figure and ranks them by strength of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four syllogistic figures from given examples and rank them according to their strength of proof as established by the author.

Core Idea
The first figure is the strongest because it yields a conclusion directly without conversion.

In this passage, the author provides concrete examples to illustrate each of the four syllogistic figures and then ranks them by their evidentiary strength. By working through these examples, you will learn to recognize the characteristic pattern of each figure and understand why the first figure is considered the most productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
For each example, find the subject (S), predicate (P), and middle term (M) that appears in both premises. For the first example: 'كل إنسان حيوان، كل حيوان جسم' – the conclusion is 'كل إنسان جسم'. So S=إنسان, P=جسم, M=حيوان.
2
Locate the middle term's position
In the first example, M (حيوان) is predicate in the first premise and subject in the second – that makes it the first figure. In the second example, M is predicate in both premises – second figure. In the third, M is subject in both – third figure. In the fourth, M is subject in first, predicate in second – fourth figure.
The author lists the premises with the minor first.
3
Check productivity
All given examples are productive (muntij), meaning they yield a necessary conclusion. The author notes that they are specific moods within each figure.
Not all moods are productive; some figures have non-productive moods.
4
Rank the figures by strength
The author states the ranking: first figure is strongest, then second, then third, then fourth. This is because the first figure provides a direct demonstration without needing conversion or other transformations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاك أمثلة حقيقية على الأشكال الأربعة: الشكل الأول: كل إنسان حيوان، كل حيوان جسم، هذا ضرب خاص من الشكل الأول، وإنما قلنا إنه ضرب خاص لأننا اعتبرنا فيه السور والنفي والإيجاب، وسوف يأتي الكلام تفصيلاً على الأسوار والنفي والإيجاب في كل شكل من الأشكال ونبين حينئذ ما هي الأضرب المنتجة وغير المنتجة من كل شكل. الشكل الثاني: مثاله كل إنسان حيوان، لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان. وهذا ضرب خاص منتج من أضرب الشكل الثاني. الشكل الثالث: مثاله كل إنسان حيوان، كل إنسان ناطق، هذا ضرب خاص منتج من الشكل الثالث. الشكل الرابع: مثاله بعض الحيوان إنسان، كل ناطق حيوان، هذا ضرب خاص منتج من الشكل الرابع. وأقوى الأشكال إنتاجاً الشكل الأول، لما سنبين لاحقاً، ثم الثاني ثم الثالث ثم الرابع، فكل واحد أوضح في الإنتاج مما بعده.
And here are real examples of the four figures: First Figure: Every human is an animal, every animal is a body. This is a specific mood of the first figure, and we only say it is a specific mood because we considered in it the quantifier and negation and affirmation, and detailed discussion will come on quantifiers and negation and affirmation in each figure, and we will then clarify which moods are productive and which are not from each figure. Second Figure: For example, every human is an animal, nothing from stone is an animal. And this is a specific productive mood from the moods of the second figure. Third Figure: For example, every human is an animal, every human is rational. This is a specific productive mood from the third figure. Fourth Figure: For example, some animals are human, every rational is an animal. This is a specific productive mood from the fourth figure. And the strongest of the figures in productivity is the first figure, as we will explain later, then the second, then the third, then the fourth, so each one is clearer in productivity than what comes after it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples illustrating each of the four syllogistic figures. The author starts with the first figure, giving the mood 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body' (which yields 'Every human is a body'). He notes that this is a particular mood because quantifiers and quality are specified. Then he gives the second figure example: 'Every human is an animal, nothing from stone is an animal' (implying 'No human is a stone'? Actually second figure typically yields a negative conclusion). The third figure example: 'Every human is an animal, every human is rational' (yielding 'Some animal is rational'). The fourth figure example: 'Some animals are human, every rational is an animal' (yielding 'Some rational is human' or similar). After the examples, the author ranks the figures by strength of proof: the first figure is strongest, followed by the second, third, and fourth. This ranking is standard in Aristotelian logic, as the first figure is the only one that directly yields a conclusion without conversion.

Key Terms
ضرب (darb): A specific mood of a syllogism, defined by the quantity and quality of its premises within a given figure. [Note: Here 'darb' is specifically a subtype of a figure; in other contexts, it can be synonymous with 'figure'.]
منتج (muntij): Productive; a syllogism that yields a necessary conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The four syllogistic figures are distinguished by the position of the middle term, and the first figure is the most powerful for demonstration.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Conditions for Productivity of the First Figure
Author states the two conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism and derives four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a first-figure syllogism is productive by checking the affirmation of the minor premise and the universality of the major premise, and identify the four productive moods.

Core Idea
Two conditions make the first figure productive: minor affirmative, major universal.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the four figures of the categorical syllogism. Now we take a closer look at the first figure, which is the most fundamental. The author lays down exactly two conditions that guarantee its productivity. Understanding these conditions will allow you to quickly evaluate any first-figure argument and distinguish valid from invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the Arabic passage and locate the two conditions stated: (1) the minor premise (صغرى) must be affirmative, and (2) the major premise (كبرى) must be universal. Note that the minor can be universal or particular, and the major can be affirmative or negative.
The conditions are explicitly introduced with 'الشرط الأول' and 'الشرط الثاني'.
2
Understand the logical rationale
Why must the minor be affirmative? If the minor were negative, the middle term would not be distributed, and no conclusion would follow. Why must the major be universal? The major term needs to be distributed to connect with the minor term correctly.
These conditions are derived from the rules of distribution and are necessary for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion.
3
Generate the four combinations
From the two conditions, list the possible forms: minor can be universal affirmative (U.A.) or particular affirmative (P.A.); major can be universal affirmative (U.A.) or universal negative (U.N.). Multiply to get four moods.
The author uses 'صورة' (form) for each premise type and 'ضرب' (mood) for each combination.
4
Name the four productive moods
In classical logic, these four moods are: Barbara (U.A.+U.A.), Celarent (U.A.+U.N.), Darii (P.A.+U.A.), Ferio (P.A.+U.N.). Associate the Arabic names if available.
The Latin names are mnemonics; in Arabic they are often referred to by their premise types.
5
Test a sample syllogism
Take an example from Islamic theology: 'Every contingent being has a cause. The world is contingent. Therefore, the world has a cause.' This is Barbara (minor universal affirmative, major universal affirmative). Check both conditions and confirm it is productive.

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a first-figure syllogism to be productive (i.e., to yield a valid conclusion). The author first notes that if the middle term is not repeated (i.e., does not appear in both premises), the syllogism is invalid. Then he states two specific conditions for the first figure: (1) The minor premise must be affirmative, regardless of whether it is universal or particular. From these conditions, we can form four possible combinations: (a) minor universal affirmative + major universal affirmative; (b) minor universal affirmative + major universal negative; (c) minor particular affirmative + major universal affirmative; (d) minor particular affirmative + major universal negative. The Syllogism

The Four Figures of the Syllogism and Their Strength Order
Author defines the four figures by middle term position and gives examples, then ranks them by productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four figures of the categorical syllogism by the position of the middle term and rank them by productivity.

Core Idea
The first figure is the strongest because the middle term directly connects subject and predicate.

We have already learned that a categorical syllogism consists of three terms: subject, predicate, and middle. Now we will see how the position of the middle term in the premises determines the figure, and why the first figure is the most powerful and productive.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In each example, find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. For the first figure example, 'animal' appears in both 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is a body'.
The middle term is the key to identifying the figure.
2
Determine its position in each premise
Check whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in the minor premise (the premise with the subject of the conclusion) and in the major premise (the premise with the predicate of the conclusion). For the first figure, 'animal' is predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
Minor premise contains the subject term; major contains the predicate term.
3
Match the position to the figure definition
Compare your findings with the definitions: first figure: predicate in minor, subject in major; second: predicate in both; third: subject in both; fourth: subject in minor, predicate in major (reverse of first).
If the pattern does not match any of the first three, it is likely the fourth figure.
4
Assess the productivity ranking
The author states that the first figure is most productive, then second, third, and fourth. Productivity refers to the number of valid moods and the certainty of the conclusion. The first figure is the most natural because the middle term directly links the extremes.
Later lessons will explain why certain moods are productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما نرى فإن الأشكال الأربعة تنتج بحسب تكرار الحد الوسط فيه، فإن كان محمولاً في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى، فهو الشكل الأول، وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث، وإن كان عكس الشكل الأول فهو الشكل الرابع. وهاك أمثلة حقيقية على الأشكال الأربعة: الشكل الأول: [كل إنسان حيوان]، [كل حيوان جسم]، هذا ضرب خاص من الشكل الأول، وإنما قلنا إنه ضرب خاص لأننا اعتبرنا فيه السور والنفي والإيجاب، وسوف يأتي الكلام تفصيلاً على الأسوار والنفي والإيجاب في كل شكل من الأشكال ونبين حينئذ ما هي الأضرب المنتجة وغير المنتجة من كل شكل. الشكل الثاني: مثاله [كل إنسان حيوان]، [لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]. وهذا ضرب خاص منتج من أضرب الشكل الثاني. الشكل الثالث: مثاله [كل إنسان حيوان]، [كل إنسان ناطق]، هذا ضرب خاص منتج من الشكل الثالث. الشكل الرابع: مثاله [بعض الحيوان إنسان]، [كل ناطق حيوان]، هذا ضرب خاص منتج من الشكل الرابع. وأقوى الأشكال إنتاجاً الشكل الأول، لما سنبين لاحقاً، ثم الثاني ثم الثالث ثم الرابع، فكل واحد أوضح في الإنتاج مما بعده.
And as we see, the four figures are productive according to the recurrence of the middle term in them. If it is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it is the first figure. If it is predicate in both, it is the second figure. If it is subject in both, it is the third figure. If it is the reverse of the first figure, it is the fourth figure. Here are real examples of the four figures: First figure: [Every human is an animal], [Every animal is a body]; this is a special mood of the first figure, and we only say it is special because we considered in it the quantifier and negation/affirmation, and detailed discussion of quantifiers and negation/affirmation in each figure will come, and we will then clarify what are the productive and non-productive moods of each figure. Second figure: example [Every human is an animal], [No stone is an animal]; this is a productive special mood of the moods of the second figure. Third figure: example [Every human is an animal], [Every human is rational]; this is a productive special mood of the third figure. Fourth figure: example [Some animal is a human], [Every rational is an animal]; this is a productive special mood of the fourth figure. And the strongest of the figures in productivity is the first figure, as we will explain later, then the second, then the third, then the fourth — so each one is clearer in productivity than the one after it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism and provides examples of each. The author begins by stating that the figures are determined by the position of the middle term (الحد الأوسط) in the two premises. If the middle term is predicate in the minor premise (الصغرى) and subject in the major premise (الكبرى), it is the first figure. If it is predicate in both premises, it is the second figure. If it is subject in both, it is the third figure. If the order of the middle term is reversed compared to the first figure (i.e., subject in minor and predicate in major), it is the fourth figure. The author then gives a concrete example for each figure: For the first figure, 'Every human is an animal' (minor) and 'Every animal is a body' (major); the middle term 'animal' is predicate in the minor and subject in the major. For the second figure, 'Every human is an animal' and 'No stone is an animal'; the middle term 'animal' is predicate in both. For the third figure, 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'; the middle term 'human' is subject in both. For the fourth figure, 'Some animal is a human' and 'Every rational is an animal'; here the middle term 'animal' is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, the reverse of the first figure. The author notes that these are special moods (أضرب خاصة) because they already consider quantifier and quality; a full discussion of all moods will come later. Finally, the author ranks the figures by strength: the first figure is most productive (i.e., yields the most certain conclusions), followed by the second, third, and fourth. This ranking is based on how directly the middle term connects the extremes.

Key Terms
الشكل (al-shakl): One of the four figures of the categorical syllogism, defined by the position of the middle term in the premises. [Note: In earlier logic, figures were often reduced to three; the fourth figure was sometimes disputed or considered a variant of the first.]
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): The middle term that appears in both premises but is eliminated in the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift — stable across logical texts.]
ضرب (darb): A mood of a syllogistic figure, determined by the quantity and quality of the premises. [Note: In later texts, 'darb' specifically refers to the combination of quantifier and quality; here it is used more loosely for any specific form.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The four figures are distinguished by the position of the middle term; the first figure is most productive because the middle term directly links the extremes.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Conditions for the First Figure
Author states the two conditions for the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism and explain why each is necessary.

Core Idea
A first-figure syllogism is valid only if its minor is affirmative and its major is universal.

In the first figure of categorical syllogisms, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. The author lays down two straightforward conditions that guarantee productivity. By understanding these conditions, you can quickly test any first-figure syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. This arrangement is essential for the conditions to apply.
2
Examine the minor premise
The minor premise must be affirmative. If it were negative, the middle term would not be distributed in the minor, breaking the rule that the middle term must be distributed at least once.
Affirmative means either universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I).
3
Examine the major premise
The major premise must be universal. The predicate of the conclusion (the major term) must be distributed in the major premise; only a universal major can guarantee this.
Universal means either universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E).
4
Apply to a real example
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is haram; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram.' The minor 'every nabidh is an intoxicant' is affirmative, and the major 'every intoxicant is haram' is universal. Both conditions are satisfied, so the syllogism is productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If this system is not adhered to, in that the middle term is not repeated, then the syllogism is non-productive. And we will now explain the conditions of productivity for each of these figures. Conditions for the productivity of the first figure: Two conditions are required for the first figure for it to be productive. The first condition: that its minor premise be affirmative, and it does not matter whether it is universal or particular. The second condition: that its major premise be universal, and it does not matter whether it is negative or affirmative. So the first premise is either universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and the second premise is either universal negative or universal affirmative; thus from these conditions four possible forms result from the product of the two forms of the first premise and the two forms of the second premise, and each of these four forms is called a mood. And since we have taken the conditions of productivity into consideration, all these four moods are productive. So the productive moods of the first figure are four. The moods that are excluded by the conditions: By the requirement of the minor being affirmative, whatever is universal negative or particular negative is excluded, so they have no productivity with the four majors; these are eight altogether, all sterile. And by the requirement of the major being universal, whatever is particular affirmative or particular negative is excluded, so they have no productivity from the two affirmative minors; these are four sterile moods as well. So it is known that the productive moods of the first figure are four, and the sterile ones are twelve: eight excluded by the requirement that the minor be affirmative, and four excluded by the requirement that the major be universal. Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid syllogism in the first figure. The author begins by stating that if the middle term is not repeated (i.e., if the figure is not properly formed), the syllogism is unproductive. Then the focus shifts to the first figure, which has two specific conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (whether universal or particular), and (2) the major premise must be universal (whether affirmative or negative). Given these constraints, the possible combinations of premises are: the minor can be universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I); the major can be universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E). This yields four possible moods: AA, IA, AE, IE. All four are productive because they satisfy the conditions. The author then calculates the excluded moods: if the minor were negative (E or O), there are four possible majors, giving 8 sterile moods (since the minor cannot be negative). If the major were particular (I or O), there are two possible affirmatives for the minor, giving 4 more sterile moods. Total sterile = 8 + 4 = 12. The table at the end summarizes this: for each combination of minor and major quantity/quality, the result is either productive (منتج) or sterile (عقیم). For example, when the minor is affirmative universal and the major is affirmative universal, it is productive; but if the major is particular affirmative, it is sterile because the major is not universal. The student should memorize these conditions and the resulting 4 productive moods (AAA, AII, EAE, EIO? Actually careful: The moods are: minor A major A = AAA; minor A major E = AEE? Actually minor universal affirmative, major universal negative gives EAE? The conclusion would be universal negative. Yes. Minor I (particular affirmative) major A gives AII? Actually minor I major A: minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative gives AII? Yes. Minor I major E gives EIO? Yes. So the four are: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The author's table matches: row major universal affirmative and universal negative, columns minor universal and particular affirmative. The table shows 'productive' for the four correct cells.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Productive and Sterile Moods of the First Figure
Author counts the productive and sterile moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four productive moods of the first figure and explain why the other twelve are sterile.

Core Idea
Only four of sixteen premise combinations in the first figure yield valid conclusions.

Once the two conditions are applied, only four moods remain productive. The author systematically excludes the rest. Understanding this enumeration is essential for mastering syllogistic logic.

Guided Path
1
List all possible premise pairs
The minor can be A, E, I, O; the major can be A, E, I, O. This gives 16 combinations.
2
Apply condition 1: minor must be affirmative
Eliminate all pairs where the minor is negative (E or O). That removes 8 combinations (minor E or O with any major).
3
Apply condition 2: major must be universal
From the remaining 8, eliminate pairs where the major is particular (I or O). That removes 4 more combinations.
4
Identify the remaining four moods
The remaining four are: AA, IA, AE, IE. These correspond to the standard moods: AAA (Barbara), AII (Darii), EAE (Celarent), EIO (Ferio).
5
Verify with a table
The author provides a table: minor rows, major columns. The four productive cells are: minor A major A, minor A major E, minor I major A, minor I major E.

If this system is not adhered to, in that the middle term is not repeated, then the syllogism is non-productive. And we will now explain the conditions of productivity for each of these figures. Conditions for the productivity of the first figure: Two conditions are required for the first figure for it to be productive. The first condition: that its minor premise be affirmative, and it does not matter whether it is universal or particular. The second condition: that its major premise be universal, and it does not matter whether it is negative or affirmative. So the first premise is either universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and the second premise is either universal negative or universal affirmative; thus from these conditions four possible forms result from the product of the two forms of the first premise and the two forms of the second premise, and each of these four forms is called a mood. And since we have taken the conditions of productivity into consideration, all these four moods are productive. So the productive moods of the first figure are four. The moods that are excluded by the conditions: By the requirement of the minor being affirmative, whatever is universal negative or particular negative is excluded, so they have no productivity with the four majors; these are eight altogether, all sterile. And by the requirement of the major being universal, whatever is particular affirmative or particular negative is excluded, so they have no productivity from the two affirmative minors; these are four sterile moods as well. So it is known that the productive moods of the first figure are four, and the sterile ones are twelve: eight excluded by the requirement that the minor be affirmative, and four excluded by the requirement that the major be universal. Table of the productive and sterile moods of the first figure by applying the conditions of productivity: Major: particular negative, particular affirmative, universal negative, universal affirmative; Minor: universal affirmative: productive, productive, productive, sterile; universal negative: sterile, sterile, sterile, sterile; particular affirmative: sterile, productive, sterile, productive; particular negative: sterile, sterile, sterile, sterile.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid syllogism in the first figure. The author begins by stating that if the middle term is not repeated (i.e., if the figure is not properly formed), the syllogism is unproductive. Then the focus shifts to the first figure, which has two specific conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (whether universal or particular), and (2) the major premise must be universal (whether affirmative or negative). Given these constraints, the possible combinations of premises are: the minor can be universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I); the major can be universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E). This yields four possible moods: AA, IA, AE, IE. All four are productive because they satisfy the conditions. The author then calculates the excluded moods: if the minor were negative (E or O), there are four possible majors, giving 8 sterile moods (since the minor cannot be negative). If the major were particular (I or O), there are two possible affirmatives for the minor, giving 4 more sterile moods. Total sterile = 8 + 4 = 12. The table at the end summarizes this: for each combination of minor and major quantity/quality, the result is either productive (منتج) or sterile (عقیم). For example, when the minor is affirmative universal and the major is affirmative universal, it is productive; but if the major is particular affirmative, it is sterile because the major is not universal. The student should memorize these conditions and the resulting 4 productive moods (AAA, AII, EAE, EIO? Actually careful: The moods are: minor A major A = AAA; minor A major E = AEE? But note: in Aristotelian logic, the first figure moods are AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. Here the author agrees: minor A major A (AAA), minor A major E (EAE? Wait: minor A major E gives EAE? Actually minor universal affirmative, major universal negative gives EAE? The conclusion would be universal negative. Yes. Minor I (particular affirmative) major A gives AII? Actually minor I major A: minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative gives AII? Yes. Minor I major E gives EIO? Yes. So the four are: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The author's table matches: row major universal affirmative and universal negative, columns minor universal and particular affirmative. The table shows 'productive' for the four correct cells.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Conditions for Productivity in the First Figure
Author states conditions for productive moods in the first figure and counts sterile combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions for a productive first-figure categorical syllogism and calculate the number of sterile and productive moods.

Core Idea
Minor affirmative and major universal: the only path to a productive first-figure mood.

The first figure is the only one that yields a conclusion directly and with certainty. But not every combination of premises works. The author of al-Sullam al-Munawraq now lays out the exact conditions—minor premise must be affirmative, major premise must be universal—and counts how many moods succeed or fail. Understanding this enumeration is essential for mastering the foundation of categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The text states two explicit conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative (ijab al-sughra) and the major premise must be universal (kulliyyat al-kubra). Locate these in the Arabic.
Affirmative includes both universal and particular affirmatives; negative includes both universal and particular negatives.
2
Understand why each condition is necessary
In the first figure, the minor is the subject of the conclusion and the major is the predicate. If the minor were negative, the conclusion would have a negative predicate unjustified; if the major were particular, the conclusion could not be universal. This logical rationale underlies the conditions.
3
Calculate sterile moods from a negative minor
The minor can be either universal negative or particular negative (2 types). Each fails with all four possible major premises (A, E, I, O). That gives 2 × 4 = 8 sterile moods.
These are the eight mentioned in the text as 'خارجة باشتراط إيجاب الصغرى'.
4
Calculate sterile moods from a particular major
With the minor affirmative (two types: universal and particular), if the major is particular (either affirmative or negative), no conclusion follows. That gives 2 × 2 = 4 sterile moods.
These are the four mentioned as 'خارجة باشتراط كلية الكبرى'.
5
Verify the totals
Total sterile moods: 8 + 4 = 12. Total possible combinations in first figure: 4 (quantities of major) × 4 (quantities of minor) = 16. Productive moods: 16 - 12 = 4. Confirm these numbers match the text.
The four productive moods correspond to the classic AAA, EAE, AII, EIO (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخرج بإيجاب الصغرى ما لو كانت سالبة كلية أو جزئية، فلا إنتاج لها مع الكبريات الأربع، فهذه ثمانية كلها عقيمة، وخرج باشتراط كلية الكبرى ما لو كانت الكبرى جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فلا إنتاج لها من الموجبتين الصغريين، فهذه أربعة أضرب عقيمة أيضاً، فعلم أن المنتج من الشكل الأول أربعة أضرب، وأن العقيم منه اثنا عشر : ثمانية خارجة باشتراط إيجاب الصغرى وأربعة خارجة باشتراط كلية الكبرى.
By requiring the minor premise to be affirmative, whatever is a universal negative or particular negative is excluded, so there is no production with the four major premises. These are eight in total, all sterile. By requiring the major premise to be universal, whatever major premise is particular, affirmative or negative, is excluded, so there is no production from the two affirmative minors. These are also four sterile moods. Thus it is known that the productive moods of the first figure are four, and the sterile ones are twelve: eight excluded by requiring the minor to be affirmative and four excluded by requiring the major to be universal.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the conditions for productive moods in the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author explains that the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. He then counts the sterile combinations: if the minor is negative (universal or particular), it fails with all four major premises (4×2=8). If the major is particular (affirmative or negative), it fails with the two affirmative minors (2×2=4). This yields 12 sterile moods, leaving 4 productive ones. The conditions are derived from the first figure's defining property: the minor is subject of the major and predicate of the conclusion, requiring its affirmative quality, and the major is predicate of the conclusion, requiring its universal quantity.

A first-figure syllogism is productive only if the minor premise is affirmative and the major premise is universal; any deviation yields sterility.

Examples of the Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author gives concrete syllogistic examples for each productive mood of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mood of any first-figure categorical syllogism by its premise pattern and verify its productivity.

Core Idea
The first figure has only four productive moods, each yielding a distinct conclusion type.

This passage provides concrete examples for each of the four productive moods of the first figure. These examples demonstrate how the combination of universal/particular and affirmative/negative premises determines the conclusion. Understanding these patterns is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood pattern
For each example, note the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the minor and major premises. The mood is named by three letters (e.g., AAA).
The source gives AAA, EAE, AII, and EIO.
2
Determine the conclusion pattern
From the mood, derive the conclusion's quantity and quality. AAA yields universal affirmative, EAE universal negative, AII particular affirmative, EIO particular negative.
The fourth mood's conclusion 'not some' is equivalent to 'some...not'.
3
Verify syllogistic conditions
For the first figure to be productive, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal. Check each example against these rules.
All four examples satisfy these conditions.
4
Transfer to Islamic sciences
Replace the terms with examples from kalam or fiqh. For instance, for AAA: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, wine is intoxicant, so wine is forbidden.'
This demonstrates the real use in legal deduction (qiyas).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جدول الأمثلة على الشكل الأول من أشكال القياس
الضرب الأول: صغراه موجبة كلية، وكبراه موجبة كلية. مثال: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان جسم، فكل إنسان جسم.
الضرب الثاني: صغراه موجبة كلية، وكبراه سالبة كلية. مثال: كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر، فلا شيء من الإنسان بحجر.
الضرب الثالث: صغراه موجبة جزئية، وكبراه موجبة كلية. مثال: بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل إنسان ناطق، فبعض الحيوان ناطق.
الضرب الرابع: صغراه موجبة جزئية، وكبراه سالبة كلية. مثال: بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، فليس بعض الحيوان بفرس.
Table of examples for the first figure of the syllogism.
First mood: its minor is universal affirmative, its major is universal affirmative. Example: Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body, so every human is a body.
Second mood: its minor is universal affirmative, its major is universal negative. Example: Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone, so no human is a stone.
Third mood: its minor is particular affirmative, its major is universal affirmative. Example: Some animal is human, and every human is rational, so some animal is rational.
Fourth mood: its minor is particular affirmative, its major is universal negative. Example: Some animal is human, and no human is a horse, so not some animal is a horse.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides examples for each of the four productive moods of the first figure. The first figure is defined as having the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) as subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. The moods are: (1) AAA (Barbara): both premises universal affirmative; (2) EAE (Celarent): minor universal affirmative, major universal negative; (3) AII (Darii): minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative; (4) EIO (Ferio): minor particular affirmative, major universal negative. The examples use terms from natural science (human, animal, body, stone, rational, horse) to illustrate how the middle term (e.g., 'animal') connects the extremes. Note that the fourth mood concludes with 'not some' (laysa ba'd), which is equivalent to 'some...not' (the O type). These examples verify the conditions taught earlier: minor affirmative, major universal.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-darb): A mood of the syllogism, characterized by the quantity and quality of its two premises.
موجبة كلية (mujiba kulliyya): A universal affirmative proposition (A-type).
سالبة كلية (saliba kulliyya): A universal negative proposition (E-type).
موجبة جزئية (mujiba juz'iyya): A particular affirmative proposition (I-type).
سالبة جزئية (saliba juz'iyya): A particular negative proposition (O-type).
النتيجة (al-natija): The conclusion necessarily drawn from the premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every human is a stone.
B) No human is a stone.
C) Some human is not a stone.
D) No animal is human.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's four productive moods are AAA, EAE, AII, and EIO; each must have an affirmative minor and a universal major premise.

Conditions for Productivity in the Third Figure
Lists two conditions for third figure productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a third-figure syllogism satisfies the two conditions for productivity.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the minor must be affirmative and one premise universal.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has its own specific rules. Understanding these conditions allows you to quickly test whether any syllogism in this figure will yield a valid conclusion. The author lists two essential conditions that must be met for a third-figure syllogism to produce a necessary inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Locate the premise that contains the subject of the conclusion. In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises, so the minor premise is usually the one with the predicate that becomes the subject of the conclusion.
The minor premise is called الصغرى.
2
Check if the minor premise is affirmative
The first condition states that the minor premise must be affirmative (موجبة). It can be either universal or particular, but it cannot be negative.
If the minor is negative, the syllogism is not productive.
3
Check if at least one premise is universal
The second condition requires that one of the two premises (minor or major) must be universal (كلية). This universality can be in the minor or major premise.
If both premises are particular, no conclusion follows.
4
Determine productivity
If both conditions are satisfied, the syllogism is productive. Note the special cases: if the minor is universal affirmative, it works with all four major types; if the minor is particular affirmative, it only works with universal majors.
Productive moods total six.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الثالث: شروط الإنتاج:
الأول: أن تكون صغراه موجبة، ولا يهم أن تكون كلية أو جزئية.
الثاني: أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية، سواء كانت الصغرى أم الكبرى.
فإن كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية أنتجت مع الكبريات الأربع لوجود الشرطين فيها، وإن كانت موجبة جزئية لم تنتج إلا مع الكليتين الكبريين. فضروبه المنتجة إذن ستة.
الضرب الأول: من موجبتين كليتين نحو كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان جسم، والنتيجة جزئية وهي بعض الحيوان جسم.
The third figure: Conditions for productivity: First: its minor premise must be affirmative, whether universal or particular. Second: one of the two premises must be universal, whether the minor or the major. If the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with the four majors because both conditions are present; if it is particular affirmative, it produces only with the two universal majors. So its productive moods are six. The first mood: from two universal affirmatives, e.g., Every human is an animal and every human is a body, so the conclusion is particular: some animal is a body.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage introduces the third figure. The conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (universal or particular), and (2) one of the premises must be universal. If the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with all four types of major premises. If the minor is particular affirmative, it only produces with universal major premises. Thus the total productive moods are six. The author then gives the first mood: both premises universal affirmative, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion, e.g., 'Every human is an animal, every human is a body, so some animal is a body.' This illustrates that the third figure always yields a particular conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The third figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative and at least one premise to be universal.
