The First Mood of the Third Figure
Gives the first mood example of the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid first-mood third-figure syllogism and state its conclusion correctly.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmative premises yield a particular affirmative conclusion in the third figure.

After learning the general conditions, we now explore the first productive mood of the third figure. This mood demonstrates a key feature: the conclusion is always particular, even though both premises are universal. The author gives a concrete example using 'human', 'animal', and 'body' to illustrate this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's characteristics
The first mood has both premises universal affirmative (موجبتان كليتان). The middle term is the subject in both premises.
This is known as the first mood (الضرب الأول).
2
Set up the syllogism example
Take the example: minor premise: 'Every human is an animal' (كل إنسان حيوان); major premise: 'Every human is a body' (كل إنسان جسم). The middle term is 'human'.
The order of premises does not affect validity.
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the middle term is the subject in both, the conclusion must be about the predicates: 'animal' and 'body'. The conclusion is particular affirmative: 'Some animal is a body' (بعض الحيوان جسم).
The conclusion is always particular in the third figure.
4
Verify validity
Check that both conditions are met: minor is affirmative (yes), at least one premise is universal (both are universal). So the syllogism is productive.
This mood is always valid in the third figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Second Figure of Syllogism: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author states conditions for Second Figure productivity and lists its four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid Second Figure syllogisms in Islamic theological arguments by applying the two conditions and distinguishing the four productive moods.

Core Idea
In the Second Figure, difference in quality and a universal major always yield a negative conclusion.

The Second Figure of the categorical syllogism is a powerful tool for disproof and refutation in Islamic debates. It requires that the two premises differ in quality and that the major premise be universal. Understanding its four productive moods allows you to recognise when an argument's conclusion necessarily follows, especially in cases where you need to negate a claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and the middle term
Locate the two premises and their terms. The middle term is the one repeated in both premises (e.g., 'contingent' in the kalam example). In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
2
Check the quality difference
Premises must differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no valid conclusion is possible.
This condition eliminates eight sterile moods.
3
Verify that the major premise is universal
The major premise (containing the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal (either 'all' or 'no'). If it is particular, the syllogism is sterile.
This condition eliminates four more sterile moods.
4
Determine which productive mood the syllogism matches
Match the combination of minor and major (quantities and qualities) to one of the four moods: (1) A minor, E major; (2) E minor, A major; (3) I minor, E major; (4) O minor, A major.
The mood determines the type of negative conclusion.
5
Derive the negative conclusion
The conclusion is always negative: universal if both premises are universal, particular if the minor is particular.
Conclusion quality is always negative; quantity follows the 'follow the weaker' rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الثاني: شروط الإنتاج:
الأول: أن تختلف المقدمتان في الكيف، بأن تكون إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة. الثاني: أن تكون الكبرى كلية موجبة أو سالبة. فإن كانت موجبة لم تنتج إلا مع السالبتين الصغريين وإن كانت سالبة لم تنتج إلا مع الموجبتين الصغريين.
الضروب المنتجة: الضروب المنتجة أربعة فقط:
الضرب الأول: موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]. والنتيجة سالبة كلية، وهي [لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر].
الضرب الثاني: عكس الضرب الأول، فيتألف من سالبة كلية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى. نحو [لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]، و[كل إنسان حيوان]، والنتيجة سالبة كلية وهي [لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان].
الضرب الثالث: يتألف من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى نحو [بعض الحيوان إنسان]، و[لا شيء من الفرس بإنسان]، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بفرس].
الضرب الرابع: يتألف من سالبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كلية كبرى، نحو [ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان]، و[كل ناطق إنسان]، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بناطق].
الضروب الخارجة بالشروط:
يخرج بشرط اختلافهما في الكيف ما لو اتفقتا بأن كانتا موجبتين أو سالبتين كليتين أو جزئيتين، أو الأولى كلية والثانية جزئية أو بالعكس، فلا إنتاج لها، فهذه ثمانية أضرب خرجت باختلاف الكيف، وكلها عقيمة. ويخرج باشتراط كلية الكبرى ما لو كانت جزئية موجبة، فلا إنتاج لها مع السالبتين الصغريين أو جزئية سالبة فلا إنتاج لها مع الموجبتين الصغريين. فهذه أربعة عقيمة أيضاً خرجت باشتراط كلية الكبرى.
فجملة أضربه العقيمة اثنا عشر كالأول.
Second Figure: Conditions for productivity: First: The two premises differ in quality, such that one is affirmative and the other negative. Second: The major premise must be universal, either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it produces only with the two negative minor premises; if it is negative, it produces only with the two affirmative minor premises.
Productive moods: The productive moods are only four:
First mood: Universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, e.g., 'Every human is an animal', and 'No stone is an animal'. The conclusion is universal negative: 'No human is a stone'.
Second mood: The converse of the first mood, composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., 'No stone is an animal', and 'Every human is an animal'. The conclusion is universal negative: 'No stone is a human'.
Third mood: Composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Some animal is a human', and 'No horse is a human'. The conclusion is particular negative: 'Not some animal is a horse'.
Fourth mood: Composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., 'Not some animal is a human', and 'Every rational is a human'. The conclusion is particular negative: 'Not some animal is rational'.
Moods excluded by the conditions:
By the condition of differing in quality, if they agree (both affirmative or both negative, universal or particular, or one universal and the other particular or vice versa), they produce nothing. So eight moods are excluded by the difference in quality, all sterile. And by the condition of universality of the major, if it is particular affirmative, it produces nothing with the two negative minors; or particular negative, it produces nothing with the two affirmative minors. So these are also four sterile moods excluded by the condition of universality of the major.
Thus the total of its sterile moods is twelve, like the first figure.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage explains the Second Figure of the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani hamli). It begins by stating two conditions for productivity: first, the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); second, the major premise must be universal. Then it lists the four productive moods, each with an example from ordinary language (using 'human', 'animal', 'stone', 'horse', 'rational'). These moods always yield negative conclusions (either universal or particular). The author then enumerates the sterile (unproductive) moods: eight that arise from violating the quality condition, and four from violating the universal-major condition, totaling twelve sterile moods. This mirrors the structure of the First Figure's treatment. Understanding these moods helps the student recognise valid argument forms in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thānī): The second figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard across Aristotelian and Islamic logic.]
الكبرى (al-kubrā): The major premise of the syllogism, which contains the major term (predicate of the conclusion) and the middle term. [Note: Stable term across classical logic texts.]
الضرب (al-ḍarb): A mood or valid combination of premises in a given figure, producing a specific conclusion. [Note: In some later texts, 'ḍarb' may also refer to the conjunction of quantity and quality; here it means a specific mood.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Second Figure, which of the following is a necessary condition for productivity?
A) Both premises must be affirmative
B) The major premise must be universal
C) The minor premise must be universal
D) The conclusion must be affirmative

Key Takeaway
The Second Figure always produces a negative conclusion and requires that the two premises differ in quality and the major premise be universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Examine the following argument from a debate on free will: 'Every voluntary action is within human power, no compelled action is within human power, therefore no voluntary action is compelled.' Does it satisfy the conditions of the Second Figure? Which mood is it?

Productive and Sterile Moods of the Third Figure
Lists six productive moods (second to sixth) and counts sterile moods of the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six productive moods of the third figure and explain the conditions that make the other ten moods sterile.

Core Idea
The third figure has exactly six productive moods and ten sterile moods, determined by the affirmativity of the minor and universality of one premise.

In this lesson, we dive into the third figure of the categorical syllogism, detailing its productive and sterile moods. You will learn to recognize each valid mood by its premise types and understand why certain combinations fail to yield necessary conclusions. The passage enumerates the moods explicitly, so pay close attention to the quantity and quality of each premise.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. The minor premise has the middle as subject and the minor term as predicate; the major premise has the middle as subject and the major term as predicate.
The conclusion always has the minor term as subject and major as predicate.
2
Identify the six productive moods
For each mood from the second to the sixth, note the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises. The passage gives examples with concrete terms. Track the pattern: the minor is always affirmative, and at least one premise is universal.
The first mood is missing from this excerpt but follows the same pattern.
3
Apply the condition of affirmativity of the minor
According to the text, the minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative (universal or particular), then with any of the four major types, the mood is sterile. This yields eight sterile combinations.
Check each major type: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
4
Apply the condition of universality of one premise
The text also states that one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the mood is sterile. This gives two additional sterile combinations: particular affirmative minor with particular affirmative major, and particular affirmative minor with particular negative major.
The minor here is affirmative but particular, so it fails universality.
5
Count the sterile and productive moods
Total sterile moods: 8 from the first condition + 2 from the second = 10. The productive moods are 6 (from the first mood on the previous page and the six listed here).
Memorizing these numbers helps in quickly evaluating syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثاني: من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، و [لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر]، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بحجر]. الضرب الثالث: من موجبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى نحو [بعض الحيوان إنسان]، وكل حيوان جسم، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الإنسان جسم]. الضرب الرابع: من موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى نحو [كل حيوان جسم]، وبعض الحيوان إنسان، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية، وهي [بعض الجسم إنسان]. الضرب الخامس: من موجبة جزئية صغرى، وسالبة كلية كبرى، نحو [بعض الحيوان إنسان]، و[لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر]، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الإنسان بحجر]. الضرب السادس: من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى، نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، وبعض الإنسان ليس بكاتب، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بكاتب].

الأضرب العقيمة بناء على الشروط: يخرج باشتراط إيجاب الصغرى ما لو كانت سالبة كلية أو جزئية، فلا تنتج مع الكبريات الأربع، فهذه ثمانية كلها عقيمة. وباشتراط كلية إحداهما يخرج ما لو كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية مع الجزئيتين الكبريين الموجبة والسالبة فلا إنتاج لها، فهذان ضربان عقيمان. فجملة عقيم هذا الشكل عشرة، والمنتج ستة قد تقدمت.
The second mood: from universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, e.g., [Every human is an animal] and [Nothing from human is a stone], and the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every animal is a stone]. The third mood: from particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, e.g., [Some animal is human] and every animal is a body, and the conclusion is particular affirmative: [Some human is a body]. The fourth mood: from universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, e.g., [Every animal is a body] and some animal is human, and the conclusion is particular affirmative: [Some body is human]. The fifth mood: from particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, e.g., [Some animal is human] and [Nothing from animal is a stone], and the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every human is a stone]. The sixth mood: from universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, e.g., [Every human is an animal] and some human is not a writer, and the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every animal is a writer].

The sterile moods based on conditions: By the condition of the affirmativity of the minor, what if the minor is universal or particular negative? Then it does not produce with the four majors; these are eight, all sterile. And by the condition of the universality of one of them, what if the minor is particular affirmative with the two particular majors, affirmative and negative? Then there is no production for them; these are two sterile moods. So the total sterile of this figure is ten, and the productive are six as have preceded.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage enumerates the productive and sterile moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by listing six productive moods, from the second to the sixth (the first is missing from this excerpt). Each mood specifies the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises, and gives an example with a conclusion. For instance, the second mood has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The third mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The fifth mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The sixth mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion.

After listing the productive moods, the text explains the sterile moods. Two conditions are given: first, the minor must be affirmative (so if the minor is negative, universal or particular, all four majors fail, giving eight sterile combinations). Second, one of the premises must be universal (so if the minor is particular affirmative and both majors are particular, two combinations fail). Thus, the third figure has ten sterile moods and six productive moods, as previously mentioned. The student should memorize these moods as they are foundational for valid reasoning.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Productivity in the Fourth Figure
States the condition for avoiding the combination of the two defects and the special rule for particular affirmative minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of avoiding the combination of the two defects (particularity and negativity) to determine whether a fourth figure syllogism is potentially productive.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's productivity hinges on avoiding the deadly combination of particularity and negativity.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has specific conditions that determine whether a given mood yields a necessary conclusion. Understanding these conditions is crucial for verifying arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where syllogistic reasoning is employed. This lesson walks through the rule of avoiding the two defects and the special case of the particular affirmative minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise's quality and quantity
Look at the minor premise (the one where the middle term is the predicate). Determine if it is affirmative or negative, universal or particular.
The minor premise is the first premise mentioned in the text's ordering.
2
Check if the minor is particular affirmative
If the minor is particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some intoxicant is nabidh'), then the condition shifts: the major must be universal negative.
This is the exceptional case that leads to a productive mood despite combining the two defects.
3
If minor is not particular affirmative, avoid the two defects
The two defects are particularity and negativity. They must not both appear together in the premises, whether in one premise or across both. For instance, you cannot have a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major because the combination of particularity (from minor) and negativity (from minor) would violate the condition.
The text says: 'even in one premise' – a single premise cannot be both particular and negative if the other premise also has one of these defects.
4
Verify the major premise when minor is particular affirmative
If the minor is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative. Otherwise, the mood is sterile.
This is the only case where the two defects can combine and still produce a conclusion.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the minor: 'Some intoxicant is khamr' (particular affirmative). The major must be 'No forbidden thing is khamr' (universal negative) for productivity. If the major were 'Every forbidden thing is khamr' (universal affirmative), the mood would be sterile.
This example mirrors the exceptional fifth mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الرابع شروط الإنتاج: إن لم تكن الصغرى موجبة جزئية، فيشترط عدم الجمع بين الخستين وهما الجزئية والسلب، سواء كان ذلك من جنس واحد كسالبتين أو جزئيتين، أو من جنسين كسالبة وجزئية، ولو في مقدمة واحدة. وإن كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية فشرطه كون الكبرى سالبة كلية. فإن كانت الصغرى سالبة كلية أنتجت مع الموجبة الكلية الكبرى وإن كانت سالبة جزئية لم تنتج لاجتماع الخستين فيها. فحصل من ذلك أربعة أضرب ثلاثة مع الموجبة الكلية الصغرى وواحد مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى أيضاً. ويستثنى من الصور العقيمة صورة واحدة فقط تجتمع فيها الخستان، ولكنها منتجة، وهي ماكانت صغراها موجبة جزئية، وكبراها سالبة كلية.

الضروب المنتجة: الحاصل أن الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل خمسة، كما مر:
الضرب الأول: يتألف من موجبتين كليتين نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[كل ناطق إنسان]، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الحيوان ناطق].
الضرب الثاني: يتألف من موجبتين الصغرى كلية والكبرى جزئية، كقولنا [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[بعض الجسم إنسان]، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الحيوان جسم].
الضرب الثالث: يتألف من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، نحو [لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس]، و[كل ناطق إنسان]، والنتيجة سالبة كلية وهي [لا شيء من الفرس بناطق].
الضرب الرابع: يتألف من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[لا شيء من الفرس بإنسان]، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بفرس].
الضرب الخامس: وهو صورة الاستثناء، يتألف من موجبة جزئية صغرى ومن سالبة كلية كبرى، نحو [بعض الحيوان إنسان] و[لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]. والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الإنسان بحجر].

الضروب العقيمة: يخرج باشتراط عدم جمع الخستين إن لم تكن الصغرى موجبة جزئية والكبرى سالبة جزئية، ما لو اجتمعتا فلا إنتاج، وذلك صادق بكون الصغرى موجبة كلية والكبرى سالبة جزئية، ويكون الصغرى سالبة كلية والكبرى غير الموجبة الكلية، وبكون الصغرى سالبة جزئية مع الكبريات الأربع. فهذه ثمانية كلها عقيمة.
يخرج باشتراط كون الكبرى سالبة كلية فيما إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية، ما لو كانت الكبرى غير السالبة الكلية، بأن كانت موجبة كلية أو جزئية أو سالبة جزئية، فلا إنتاج حينئذ. فهذه ثلاثة أضرب عقيمة أيضاً. فجملة عقيم هذا الشكل أحد عشر.
The fourth figure: conditions for production: If the minor is not particular affirmative, then it is required to avoid combining the two defects, which are particularity and negativity, whether that is from one kind like two negatives or two particulars, or from two kinds like negative and particular, even in one premise. And if the minor is particular affirmative, its condition is that the major be universal negative. So if the minor is universal negative, it produces with the universal affirmative major; but if it is particular negative, it does not produce due to the combination of the two defects in it. So from that arise four moods: three with the universal affirmative minor and one with the universal negative major as well. And one image is excepted from the sterile forms, only one in which the two defects combine but it is productive, which is when its minor is particular affirmative and its major is universal negative.

The productive moods: The result is that the productive moods in this figure are five, as mentioned:
The first mood: composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., [Every human is an animal] and [Every rational is human]; the conclusion is particular affirmative: [Some animal is rational].
The second mood: composed of two affirmatives, the minor universal and the major particular, as our saying [Every human is an animal] and [Some body is human]; the conclusion is particular affirmative: [Some animal is body].
The third mood: composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., [Nothing from human is a horse] and [Every rational is human]; the conclusion is universal negative: [Nothing from horse is rational].
The fourth mood: composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., [Every human is an animal] and [Nothing from horse is human]; the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every animal is a horse].
The fifth mood: and it is the exceptional form, composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., [Some animal is human] and [Nothing from stone is animal]; the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every human is a stone].

The sterile moods: By the condition of not combining the two defects if the minor is not particular affirmative and the major is particular negative, what if they combine? Then no production. That applies to the minor being universal affirmative and the major particular negative, and the minor being universal negative and the major other than universal affirmative, and the minor being particular negative with the four majors. These are eight, all sterile.
By the condition that the major be universal negative when the minor is particular affirmative, what if the major is other than universal negative, whether it is universal affirmative, particular affirmative, or particular negative? Then no production in that case. These are also three sterile moods. So the total sterile of this figure is eleven.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It first gives the conditions for a mood to be productive. The key rule is to avoid the combination of two defects: particularity (juz'iyya) and negativity (salb). If the minor is not particular affirmative, then you must avoid having both defects in the premises (i.e., you cannot have two particulars, two negatives, or one of each in a single premise). If the minor is particular affirmative, then the major must be universal negative. There is an exception: a minor that is universal negative can produce with a universal affirmative major, and a minor that is particular affirmative combined with a universal negative major is actually productive (the exceptional case).

Then the text lists the five productive moods:
1. Two universal affirmatives: All A are B, All C are A → Some B are C (particular affirmative).
2. Universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major: All A are B, Some C are A → Some B are C.
3. Universal negative minor, universal affirmative major: No A is B, All C are A → No B is C (universal negative).
4. Universal affirmative minor, universal negative major: All A are B, No C is A → Some B is not C (particular negative).
5. (Exceptional) Particular affirmative minor, universal negative major: Some A is B, No C is B → Some A is not C (particular negative).

Finally, the sterile moods are counted. There are eight where the two defects combine (when the minor is not particular affirmative) and three where the major is not universal negative while the minor is particular affirmative, for a total of eleven sterile moods. The student should carefully note the exceptional productive case where both defects appear (minor particular, major universal negative).

The Five Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Lists the five productive moods with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five productive moods of the fourth figure from their premise combinations and conclusion types.

Core Idea
Five specific premise combinations yield valid conclusions in the fourth figure, including an exceptional mood that breaks the general rule.

The text enumerates five productive moods in the fourth figure, each with a unique combination of quantity and quality in the premises and conclusion. This lesson presents each mood with an example and explains why it is productive, focusing on the logical structure and the exceptional fifth mood where the two defects combine.

Guided Path
1
List the five productive moods from the text
The text mentions five moods: first (two universal affirmatives), second (universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major), third (universal negative minor + universal affirmative major), fourth (universal affirmative minor + universal negative major), and fifth (particular affirmative minor + universal negative major).
The fifth is called 'ṣūrat al-istithnā' (the exceptional form).
2
For each mood, identify the conclusion type
The conclusion varies: first and second yield particular affirmative; third yields universal negative; fourth and fifth yield particular negative. The conclusion always has the subject from the major premise and the predicate from the minor premise.
This is characteristic of the fourth figure's arrangement.
3
Compare with the conditions from the previous lesson
Notice how the first mood avoids the two defects (both universal affirmative), the second avoids them (minor universal, major particular but affirmative), the third has a universal negative minor but the major is universal affirmative (no combination of defects), the fourth has universal affirmative minor and universal negative major (no combination), and the fifth has particular affirmative minor (one defect) and universal negative major (other defect) but is still productive because it is the exception.
The fifth mood is the only one where both defects appear together.
4
Apply to an Islamic example for the fifth mood
Minor: 'Some intoxicant is khamr' (particular affirmative). Major: 'No forbidden thing is khamr' (universal negative). Conclusion: 'Not every intoxicant is forbidden' (particular negative). This is valid because the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal negative.
Replace with your own example using fiqh terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الرابع شروط الإنتاج: إن لم تكن الصغرى موجبة جزئية، فيشترط عدم الجمع بين الخستين وهما الجزئية والسلب، سواء كان ذلك من جنس واحد كسالبتين أو جزئيتين، أو من جنسين كسالبة وجزئية، ولو في مقدمة واحدة. وإن كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية فشرطه كون الكبرى سالبة كلية. فإن كانت الصغرى سالبة كلية أنتجت مع الموجبة الكلية الكبرى وإن كانت سالبة جزئية لم تنتج لاجتماع الخستين فيها. فحصل من ذلك أربعة أضرب ثلاثة مع الموجبة الكلية الصغرى وواحد مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى أيضاً. ويستثنى من الصور العقيمة صورة واحدة فقط تجتمع فيها الخستان، ولكنها منتجة، وهي ماكانت صغراها موجبة جزئية، وكبراها سالبة كلية.

الضروب المنتجة: الحاصل أن الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل خمسة، كما مر:
الضرب الأول: يتألف من موجبتين كليتين نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[كل ناطق إنسان]، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الحيوان ناطق].
الضرب الثاني: يتألف من موجبتين الصغرى كلية والكبرى جزئية، كقولنا [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[بعض الجسم إنسان]، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الحيوان جسم].
الضرب الثالث: يتألف من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، نحو [لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس]، و[كل ناطق إنسان]، والنتيجة سالبة كلية وهي [لا شيء من الفرس بناطق].
الضرب الرابع: يتألف من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، نحو [كل إنسان حيوان]، و[لا شيء من الفرس بإنسان]، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بفرس].
الضرب الخامس: وهو صورة الاستثناء، يتألف من موجبة جزئية صغرى ومن سالبة كلية كبرى، نحو [بعض الحيوان إنسان] و[لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]. والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الإنسان بحجر].

الضروب العقيمة: يخرج باشتراط عدم جمع الخستين إن لم تكن الصغرى موجبة جزئية والكبرى سالبة جزئية، ما لو اجتمعتا فلا إنتاج، وذلك صادق بكون الصغرى موجبة كلية والكبرى سالبة جزئية، ويكون الصغرى سالبة كلية والكبرى غير الموجبة الكلية، وبكون الصغرى سالبة جزئية مع الكبريات الأربع. فهذه ثمانية كلها عقيمة.
يخرج باشتراط كون الكبرى سالبة كلية فيما إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية، ما لو كانت الكبرى غير السالبة الكلية، بأن كانت موجبة كلية أو جزئية أو سالبة جزئية، فلا إنتاج حينئذ. فهذه ثلاثة أضرب عقيمة أيضاً. فجملة عقيم هذا الشكل أحد عشر.
The fourth figure: conditions for production: If the minor is not particular affirmative, then it is required to avoid combining the two defects, which are particularity and negativity, whether that is from one kind like two negatives or two particulars, or from two kinds like negative and particular, even in one premise. And if the minor is particular affirmative, its condition is that the major be universal negative. So if the minor is universal negative, it produces with the universal affirmative major; but if it is particular negative, it does not produce due to the combination of the two defects in it. So from that arise four moods: three with the universal affirmative minor and one with the universal negative major as well. And one image is excepted from the sterile forms, only one in which the two defects combine but it is productive, which is when its minor is particular affirmative and its major is universal negative.

The productive moods: The result is that the productive moods in this figure are five, as mentioned:
The first mood: composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., [Every human is an animal] and [Every rational is human]; the conclusion is particular affirmative: [Some animal is rational].
The second mood: composed of two affirmatives, the minor universal and the major particular, as our saying [Every human is an animal] and [Some body is human]; the conclusion is particular affirmative: [Some animal is body].
The third mood: composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., [Nothing from human is a horse] and [Every rational is human]; the conclusion is universal negative: [Nothing from horse is rational].
The fourth mood: composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., [Every human is an animal] and [Nothing from horse is human]; the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every animal is a horse].
The fifth mood: and it is the exceptional form, composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., [Some animal is human] and [Nothing from stone is animal]; the conclusion is particular negative: [Not every human is a stone].

The sterile moods: By the condition of not combining the two defects if the minor is not particular affirmative and the major is particular negative, what if they combine? Then no production. That applies to the minor being universal affirmative and the major particular negative, and the minor being universal negative and the major other than universal affirmative, and the minor being particular negative with the four majors. These are eight, all sterile.
By the condition that the major be universal negative when the minor is particular affirmative, what if the major is other than universal negative, whether it is universal affirmative, particular affirmative, or particular negative? Then no production in that case. These are also three sterile moods. So the total sterile of this figure is eleven.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It first gives the conditions for a mood to be productive. The key rule is to avoid the combination of two defects: particularity (juz'iyya) and negativity (salb). If the minor is not particular affirmative, then you must avoid having both defects in the premises (i.e., you cannot have two particulars, two negatives, or one of each in a single premise). If the minor is particular affirmative, then the major must be universal negative. There is an exception: a minor that is universal negative can produce with a universal affirmative major, and a minor that is particular affirmative combined with a universal negative major is actually productive (the exceptional case).

Then the text lists the five productive moods:
1. Two universal affirmatives: All A are B, All C are A → Some B are C (particular affirmative).
2. Universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major: All A are B, Some C are A → Some B are C.
3. Universal negative minor, universal affirmative major: No A is B, All C are A → No B is C (universal negative).
4. Universal affirmative minor, universal negative major: All A are B, No C is A → Some B is not C (particular negative).
5. (Exceptional) Particular affirmative minor, universal negative major: Some A is B, No C is B → Some A is not C (particular negative).

Finally, the sterile moods are counted. There are eight where the two defects combine (when the minor is not particular affirmative) and three where the major is not universal negative while the minor is particular affirmative, for a total of eleven sterile moods. The student should carefully note the exceptional productive case where both defects appear (minor particular, major universal negative).

الضرب الخامس
صورة الاستثناء

Sterile Moods of the Fourth Figure and the Exception
Lists the conditions for sterility and counts eleven sterile moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eleven sterile mood patterns of the fourth figure and explain why each is sterile using the condition of the two defects.

Core Idea
Eleven mood combinations in the fourth figure are sterile because they violate the condition of avoiding the two defects, except for the one exception already noted.

After listing the productive moods, the text turns to the sterile ones, counting eleven in total. Understanding why these moods fail is essential for avoiding errors in syllogistic reasoning. This lesson breaks down the sterile patterns into two groups: those where the minor is not particular affirmative and the two defects combine, and those where the minor is particular affirmative but the major is not universal negative.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two groups of sterile moods
The text divides sterile moods into two categories: 1) When the minor is not particular affirmative, any mood that combines the two defects (particularity and negativity) is sterile. 2) When the minor is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative; if the major is any other type, the mood is sterile.
The exception is the fifth productive mood where minor is particular affirmative and major is universal negative.
2
Enumerate the eight sterile moods from the first group
From the condition 'avoid combining the two defects when minor is not particular affirmative', the following eight combinations are sterile: (a) minor universal affirmative, major particular negative; (b) minor universal negative with major that is not universal affirmative (i.e., major particular affirmative, major universal negative? Actually careful: the text says 'with the major other than universal affirmative' so we need to list all majors except universal affirmative: that is three: major particular affirmative, major universal negative? Wait, universal negative is not universal affirmative, so it would be included, but that would be productive? Let's re-read. The text: 'وبكون الصغرى سالبة كلية والكبرى غير الموجبة الكلية' (minor universal negative and major other than universal affirmative). So majors: particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. But the universal negative major with universal negative minor would combine negativity in both? That is sterile. And (c) minor particular negative with any of the four majors (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) because the minor itself has both defects. So total 1 + 3 + 4 = 8.
The text explicitly says 'these are eight, all sterile'.
3
Enumerate the three sterile moods from the second group
When the minor is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative. If the major is any other type (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, particular negative), the mood is sterile. That gives three sterile moods.
These are additional to the eight.
4
Confirm the count of eleven
8 + 3 = 11 sterile moods, leaving only 5 productive moods out of 16 possible combinations in the fourth figure.
This completes the enumeration.

The Weaker Premise Rule
States the first general rule about the conclusion following the weaker premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quality and quantity of a syllogism's conclusion based on the weaker premise.

Core Idea
The conclusion must follow the weaker premise: if any premise is negative or particular, the conclusion is too.

This lesson covers the first general rule: the conclusion of any categorical syllogism must be no stronger than its weakest premise. In quality, negative is weaker; in quantity, particular is weaker. This rule applies regardless of the figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality and quantity of each premise
Examine both premises: are they affirmative or negative? Are they universal or particular?
2
Determine the weaker premise
Negative is weaker than affirmative in quality; particular is weaker than universal in quantity. The conclusion takes the weaker from each category.
If one premise is negative and the other particular, the conclusion is particular negative.
3
Apply to an example
Consider the premises: 'Every intoxicant is unlawful' (universal affirmative) and 'Some nabidh is an intoxicant' (particular affirmative). The weaker in quantity is the particular, so the conclusion must be particular. The weaker in quality is none (both affirmative), so the conclusion is affirmative. Thus, the conclusion is 'Some nabidh is unlawful.'
This example illustrates the rule in a fiqh context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قواعد عامة للأشكال الأربعة: أولاً: النتيجة تتبع الأخس من المقدمتين، وذلك في الكيف والكم، فإن الأخس من الكيف هو السالب والأخش من الكم هو الجزئي. فإن كانت إحدى المقدمات جزئية، كانت النتيجة جزئية، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمات سالبة، كانت النتيجة سالبة. ثانياً: هذه الأشكال الأربعة ليست مختصة بالحملي، بل هي أيضاً صحيحة في الشرطي، وذلك على خلاف ما قاله الناظم، وهذا هو التحقيق كما أشرنا من قبل. ثالثاً: يجوز حذف إحدى المقدمات من القياس وحذف النتيجة إذا كان العلم بها واضحاً جلياً، وذلك كقولنا: [هذا يُحدُّ لأنه زان]، فإن المعنى وكلُّ زان يُحد]، فقد حذفت الكبرى. ونحو قولنا: [هذا زان، وكل زان يُحد]، فقد حذفت النتيجة، لأن المعنى [فهذا يُحد]، فحذفت للعلم بها من القياس.
General rules for the four figures: First: The conclusion follows the weaker of the two premises, and that is in quality and quantity, for the weaker in quality is the negative and the weaker in quantity is the particular. So if one of the premises is particular, the conclusion is particular; and if one of the premises is negative, the conclusion is negative. Second: These four figures are not specific to categorical [syllogisms], but rather they are also valid in conditional [syllogisms], and that is contrary to what the versifier said, and this is the verified opinion as we indicated before. Third: It is permissible to omit one of the premises from the syllogism and to omit the conclusion if knowledge of it is clearly obvious, such as our saying: [This one is to be flogged because he is a fornicator], for the meaning is [and every fornicator is to be flogged], so the major [premise] has been omitted. And like our saying: [This one is a fornicator, and every fornicator is to be flogged], so the conclusion has been omitted, for the meaning is [so this one is to be flogged], so it is omitted for being known from the syllogism.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents three general rules that apply to all four figures of the syllogism. The first rule is about the conclusion following the weaker premise: in terms of quality (affirmative vs. negative), the negative is weaker, so if any premise is negative, the conclusion must be negative. In terms of quantity (universal vs. particular), the particular is weaker, so if any premise is particular, the conclusion must be particular. This applies regardless of the figure.

The second rule states that the four figures are not limited to categorical (hamli) syllogisms but also apply to conditional (sharti) syllogisms. The author notes that this is contrary to what the versifier (al-nazim, likely the author of a poem on logic) said, and the author affirms that the correct view is that they are applicable to conditionals as well.

The third rule addresses the practical convenience of omitting premises or the conclusion when they are obvious. For example, if one says 'This one is to be flogged because he is a fornicator', the major premise 'every fornicator is to be flogged' is implied and thus omitted. Similarly, if one says 'This one is a fornicator and every fornicator is to be flogged', the conclusion 'so this one is to be flogged' is so obvious that it is omitted. This is a common practice in everyday argumentation and legal reasoning.

Key Terms
الأخس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general rules, if one premise in a syllogism is particular and the other is negative, what will be the quantity and quality of the conclusion?
A) Universal affirmative.
B) Universal negative.
C) Particular negative.
D) Particular affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a syllogism must always be no stronger than its weakest premise in both quality and quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises 'Every intoxicant is unlawful' (universal affirmative) and 'Some nabidh is an intoxicant' (particular affirmative), determine the quality and quantity of the conclusion using the weaker premise rule. Write the conclusion as a categorical proposition.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Figures Applicable to Conditionals
States the second general rule that figures apply to conditional syllogisms, contrary to the versifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify that the four figures of syllogisms apply to conditional syllogisms as well as categorical ones.

Core Idea
Contrary to the versifier, the four figures work for conditional syllogisms too.

The second general rule clarifies a disagreement: the four figures are not limited to categorical syllogisms but also hold for conditional ones. This expands the utility of the figures, allowing the same structural analysis for hypothetical and disjunctive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between categorical and conditional syllogisms
Categorical syllogisms use propositions like 'A is B'; conditional syllogisms use 'If A then B' or 'Either A or B'.
2
Recall the four figures based on middle term position
The figures are defined by the role of the middle term (subject or predicate in premises). The same logic applies to conditionals if we treat the antecedent and consequent as terms.
3
Examine the disagreement with the versifier
The versifier (al-nazim) claimed figures are only for categoricals. The commentator argues they apply to conditionals, which is the verified opinion.
This shows that logical rules can be contested and refined.
4
Test with an example
Consider a conditional syllogism: 'If the world is created, it has a creator; if it has a creator, it is contingent; therefore, if the world is created, it is contingent.' The middle term 'has a creator' appears in the antecedent of one premise and the consequent of the other, fitting the first figure.
This example from kalam demonstrates the applicability.

The Syllogism

Omission of Premises or Conclusion
States the third general rule that omission of premises or conclusion is allowed when obvious.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the missing premise or conclusion in an enthymeme using the logical structure of a syllogism.

Core Idea
An argument can be valid even when a premise or conclusion is omitted, as long as it is obvious.

The third general rule addresses everyday argumentation: we often leave out premises or conclusions that are clear. This is called an enthymeme in Western logic, and Islamic logicians also permit it. Recognizing and filling in missing parts is a key skill for analyzing real-world arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the given statements
Look at what is explicitly stated. It might be a single statement with 'because' or a pair of statements.
2
Determine what is missing
Is the missing part a premise (major or minor) or the conclusion? If a conclusion is present, one premise is likely omitted; if a conclusion is absent, it is omitted.
In the example 'This one is to be flogged because he is a fornicator', the conclusion is present, but the major premise 'Every fornicator is to be flogged' is missing.
3
Supply the missing part
Using the known statements and the standard syllogistic form, fill in the missing proposition. Ensure it makes sense logically.
The example 'This one is a fornicator, and every fornicator is to be flogged' has the conclusion missing: 'Therefore, this one is to be flogged.'
4
Check validity
Once the missing part is supplied, verify that the argument is a valid syllogism in one of the four figures.
The example with missing major premise is a first-figure syllogism: minor premise: 'This is a fornicator', major: 'Every fornicator is to be flogged', conclusion: 'Therefore, this one is to be flogged'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الأخس (al-akhass): The weaker (premise) in the sense of determining the quality or quantity of the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]
الحملي (al-ḥamlī): Categorical syllogism (predicative). [Note: Stable.]
الشرطي (al-sharṭī): Conditional syllogism (including hypothetical and disjunctive). [Note: Some texts treat conditional separately.]
الناظم (al-nāẓim): The versifier, the author of a logical poem (possibly al-Sullam al-Murawnaq or similar). [Note: Refers to the author of the logical poem being commented on.]

The Syllogism

The First Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author enumerates the first four moods with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first four moods of the first figure, list their premise combinations and conclusions, and provide an Islamic example for each.

Core Idea
The first figure's moods are defined by the quantity and quality of premises, with the middle term as subject in the major and predicate in the minor.

In the first figure of the categorical syllogism, the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. The author lists five productive moods; here we examine the first four, which cover universal and particular affirmatives and negatives. Understanding these moods allows you to validate many arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Mood 1: Two Universal Affirmatives
The first mood has both premises universal affirmative (A propositions). The conclusion is particular affirmative (I). Example: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore some haram thing is nabidh.' Notice that the conclusion is particular because the middle term is not distributed in the major.
This mood is named Barbara in Latin logic.
2
Mood 2: Universal Affirmative Minor, Particular Affirmative Major
The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major. The conclusion is particular affirmative. Example: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Some drink is intoxicant. Therefore some haram thing is drink.'
3
Mood 3: Universal Negative Minor, Universal Affirmative Major
The third mood has a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major. The conclusion is universal negative. Example: 'No intoxicant is halal. Every wine is an intoxicant. Therefore no wine is halal.'
4
Mood 4: Universal Affirmative Minor, Universal Negative Major
The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major. The conclusion is particular negative. Example: 'Every wine is an intoxicant. No water is wine. Therefore some intoxicant is not water.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الأول : يتألف من موجبتين كليتين نحو كل إنسان حيوان]، وكل ناطق إنسان]، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الحيوان ناطق].
الضرب الثاني: يتألف من موجبتين الصغرى كلية والكبرى جزئية، كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان]، و[بعض الجسم إنسان]، والنتيجة موجبة جزئية وهي [بعض الحيوان جسم].
الضرب الثالث: يتألف من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، نحو الا شيء من الإنسان بفرس]، واكل ناطق إنسان، والنتيجة سالبة كلية وهي الا شيء من الفرس بناطق.
الضرب الرابع: يتألف من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، نحو أكل إنسان حيوان]، و[لا شيء من الفرس بإنسان، والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الحيوان بفرس].
الضرب الخامس: وهو صورة الاستثناء، يتألف من موجبة جزئية صغرى ومن سالبة كلية كبرى، نحو [ بعض الحيوان إنسان] و[لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان]. والنتيجة سالبة جزئية وهي [ليس بعض الإنسان بحجر].
The first mood: composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is a human'; the conclusion is a particular affirmative, namely 'Some animal is rational.'
The second mood: composed of two affirmatives, the minor universal and the major particular, as we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some body is a human'; the conclusion is a particular affirmative, namely 'Some animal is a body.'
The third mood: composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., 'Nothing of human is a horse' and 'Every rational is a human'; the conclusion is a universal negative, namely 'Nothing of horse is rational.'
The fourth mood: composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'No horse is a human'; the conclusion is a particular negative, namely 'Not some animal is a horse.'
The fifth mood: which is the form of exception, composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Some animal is a human' and 'No stone is an animal'; the conclusion is a particular negative, namely 'Not some human is a stone.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the five productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The first figure is characterized by the middle term being subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. The author lists each mood by the quantity and quality of its premises and gives an example. In the first mood, both premises are universal affirmative, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, also yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The third mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The fifth mood, called 'the form of exception', has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. Note that the examples are drawn from universal concepts (human, animal, rational, horse, stone) to illustrate logical relationships. All these moods are productive because they entail a necessary conclusion following from the premises.

Key Terms
ضرب
موجبة كلية
سالبة كلية
موجبة جزئية
سالبة جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Some alcohol is not halal.
B) No wine is halal.
C) Some wine is halal.
D) No alcohol is wine.

Key Takeaway
The first four moods of the first figure produce conclusions from universal to particular, affirmative and negative, depending on the premise types.

The Fifth Mood: The Form of Exception
Author describes the fifth mood with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid fifth-mood syllogism and explain why it is called the form of exception.

Core Idea
The fifth mood uses a particular affirmative and a universal negative to produce a particular negative conclusion.

The fifth mood is distinctive because it combines a particular affirmative premise with a universal negative, leading to a particular negative conclusion. It is called the form of exception because it excludes some members of the subject of the minor premise from the predicate of the major. This mood is less common but useful for arguments about partial exclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise as particular affirmative
The minor premise says 'Some S is M'. For example, 'Some drink is wine.'
The minor term is S, middle is M.
2
Identify the major premise as universal negative
The major premise says 'No M is P'. For example, 'No wine is water.'
The major term is P.
3
Derive the conclusion as particular negative
The conclusion is 'Some S is not P', i.e., 'Some drink is not water.' This follows because the minor gives some S that are M, and the major says all M are not P, so those S that are M are not P.
4
Construct an Islamic example
Example: 'Some animals are permissible to eat' (minor: particular affirmative), 'No pig is permissible to eat' (major: universal negative). Conclusion: 'Some animals are not pigs' (particular negative).
This shows that not all animals are pigs, but some are not pigs.

The third mood: composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., 'Nothing of human is a horse' and 'Every rational is a human'; the conclusion is a universal negative, namely 'Nothing of horse is rational.'
The fourth mood: composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'No horse is a human'; the conclusion is a particular negative, namely 'Not some animal is a horse.'
The fifth mood: which is the form of exception, composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Some animal is a human' and 'No stone is an animal'; the conclusion is a particular negative, namely 'Not some human is a stone.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the five productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The first figure is characterized by the middle term being subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. The author lists each mood by the quantity and quality of its premises and gives an example. In the first mood, both premises are universal affirmative, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, also yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The third mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The fifth mood, called 'the form of exception', has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. Note that the examples are drawn from universal concepts (human, animal, rational, horse, stone) to illustrate logical relationships. All these moods are productive because they entail a necessary conclusion following from the premises.

موجبة جزئية
سالبة كلية
سالبة جزئية
صورة الاستثناء

The 11 Sterile Moods of the First Figure
Author enumerates the 11 sterile moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 11 sterile moods of the first figure and explain why each premise pair yields no necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Not every premise pair produces a conclusion; 11 of 16 moods in the first figure are sterile.

Now that you know the five productive moods of the first figure, it is equally important to recognize the sterile ones. The author lists two conditions that generate 11 sterile moods. Mastering these will prevent you from crafting invalid syllogisms in theological and juristic arguments, where a flawed mood could undermine a proof.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four proposition types
There are four categorical propositions: A (universal affirmative), E (universal negative), I (particular affirmative), and O (particular negative). The first figure has 4×4 = 16 possible premise pairs (moods).
2
Understand the first condition for sterility
The first set of sterile moods occurs when the minor premise is not I and the major premise is not O. The specific combinations are: (1) minor A + major O, (2) minor E + major not A (i.e., E, I, O), and (3) minor O with all four majors (A, E, I, O). This yields 1 + 3 + 4 = 8 sterile moods.
These 8 cover cases where the minor is not particular affirmative and the major is not particular negative.
3
Learn the second condition for sterility
The second set applies when the minor is I and the major is not E. The possible majors are A, I, or O, giving three moods: (I, A), (I, I), (I, O). These are sterile because a particular affirmative minor cannot combine with a non-universal negative major in the first figure.
Together with the previous 8, we have 11 sterile moods.
4
Verify the total and check the pattern
The first figure has 16 possible moods; 5 are productive (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio, and perhaps Barbari? Actually the first figure has 5 productive: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO, and sometimes AAI or EAO? Check standard logic: first figure productive moods: AAA (Barbara), EAE (Celarent), AII (Darii), EIO (Ferio), plus two weakened: AAI (Barbari) and EAO (Celaront) are also valid but with particular conclusions. The author's list includes the 5 standard ones? The passage here only covers sterile. So 11 sterile + 5 productive = 16.
The 11 sterile moods are exactly those that violate the conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism (e.g., middle term must be subject in minor and predicate in major).

The Syllogism
intermediate

General Rules for the Four Figures
Author states four general rules for the four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four general rules governing all categorical syllogisms and apply the first rule to determine the quality and quantity of a conclusion from given premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion of a syllogism is never stronger than its weakest premise.

Syllogisms are not arbitrary; they follow strict rules that guarantee valid inference. This passage presents four foundational rules that every logician must memorize and apply. Mastering these rules will allow you to evaluate any categorical syllogism, whether it comes from a theological proof or a legal argument, and to avoid common fallacies like circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the weaker premise (rule 1)
For a given pair of premises, determine which is 'weaker': negative beats affirmative (in quality), particular beats universal (in quantity). Then the conclusion must match that weaker premise in both aspects.
2
Check applicability to conditional syllogisms (rule 2)
Note that the four figures are not limited to categorical (hamli) syllogisms; they also apply to conditional (sharti) ones. This corrects a common misconception from the poet al-Nazim.
3
Recognize permissible omissions (rule 3)
In everyday reasoning, we often drop a premise or the conclusion if it is obvious. For example, 'This person is flogged because he is a fornicator' implies the major premise 'Every fornicator is flogged.' Identify the missing part and reconstruct the full syllogism.
4
Understand the necessity of necessary premises (rule 4)
If premises are not self-evidently necessary, they must be traced back to necessary premises through a chain of syllogisms, otherwise circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) occurs. Use the example of the world's attributes being originated to see how a chain ends in necessary premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قواعد عامة للأشكال الأربعة
:أولاً: النتيجة تتبع الأخس من المقدمتين، وذلك في الكيف والكم، فإن الأخس من الكيف هو السالب والأخش من الكم هو الجزئي. فإن كانت إحدى المقدمات جزئية، كانت النتيجة جزئية، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمات سالبة، كانت النتيجة سالبة. ثانياً: هذه الأشكال الأربعة ليست مختصة بالحملي ، بل هي أيضاً صحيحة في الشرطي، وذلك على خلاف ما قاله الناظم، وهذا هو التحقيق كما أشرنا من قبل. ثالثاً: يجوز حذف إحدى المقدمات من القياس وحذف النتيجة إذا كان العلم بها واضحاً جلياً، وذلك كقولنا: [هذا يُحدُّ لأنه زان]، فإن المعنى وكلُّ زان يُحد]، فقد حذفت الكبرى. ونحو قولنا: [هـذا زان، وكل زان يُحد]، فقد حذفت النتيجة، لأن المعنى [فهذا يُحد]، فحذفت للعلم بها من القياس.
رابعاً: إذا لم تكن المقدمات ضرورية فيجب انتهاؤها إلى مقدمة راجعة إلى الضرورة، لئلا يلزم الدور وهو توقف الآخر على ما يتوقف عليه، أو التسلسل وهو ترتب أمر على أمر إلى ما لا نهاية له] فيلزم الدور فيما إذا استدل على المتأخر بما يتوقف عليه ذلك المتأخر ، ويلزم التسلسل فيما إذا توقف الأول على أدلة مترتبة لا غاية لها، فإن انتهى الأمر إلى دليل غير ضروري المقدمات ولا ،مسلَّمة لم يَكْفِ.
مثالُ ما مقدماته ضرورية: [هذا العدد ينقسم إلى متساويين]، وأكل منقسم كذلك زوج].
ومثال ما مقدماته نظرية: قولك [العالم صفاته حادثة] [وكل من صفاته حادثة فهو حادث]. فنستدل على الصغرى بقولنا: [صفاته متغيرة]، واكل متغير حادث. والأولى من هاتين المقدمتين ضرورية، ونستدل على الثانية منهما بأن نقول: [التغير إن كان من عدم إلى وجود كان الوجود طارئاً، وإن كان من وجود إلى عدم كان الوجود جائزاً]، و[الجائز لا يقع إلا حادثاً ]. ونستدل على الكبرى من القياس الأول بقولنا : كل من كان صفاته حادثة لا يعرى عن الحوادث]، وأكل من لا يعرى عن الحوادث لا يسبقها]، وكل من لا يسبق الحوادث فهو حادث ]. فقد انتهينا إلى الضرورة، ولا عبرة باعتراض بعض الفلاسفة على بعض تلك المقدمات فإن ذلك مكابرة.
(1) لزوم الدور إن رجعنا للمتوقف عليه الأول. ولزوم التسلسل إن ذهبنا لا إلى نهاية. فيتعين أن تكون المقدمات ضرورية، أو تنتهى إلى ضرورية.
General rules for the four figures:
First: The conclusion follows the weaker of the two premises, and that is in quality and quantity. For the weaker in quality is the negative, and the weaker in quantity is the particular. So if one of the premises is particular, the conclusion is particular; and if one of the premises is negative, the conclusion is negative.
Second: These four figures are not exclusive to categorical syllogisms, but are also valid in conditional syllogisms, contrary to what the poet (al-nāẓim) said. And this is the verified view, as we indicated before.
Third: It is permissible to delete one of the premises from the syllogism, and to delete the conclusion if knowledge of it is clear and obvious. For example, we say: 'This person is to be flogged because he is a fornicator'; the meaning is 'Every fornicator is to be flogged' — the major premise was deleted. Similarly, our statement: 'This is a fornicator, and every fornicator is flogged' — the conclusion was deleted because the meaning is 'Therefore this is flogged'; it was deleted because it is known from the syllogism.
Fourth: If the premises are not necessary, then they must end with a premise that goes back to necessity, lest circularity (dawr) occur — which is the dependence of one thing on what it depends on — or infinite regress (tasalsul) — which is the ordering of one thing upon another ad infinitum. Circularity occurs when the later is proven by that on which that later depends; infinite regress occurs when the first depends on an endless chain of proofs. If the matter ends with a proof whose premises are neither necessary nor conceded, it is insufficient.
Example of necessary premises: 'This number is divisible into two equal parts' and 'Everything divisible in that way is even'.
Example of theoretical premises: Your statement: 'The world: its attributes are originated' and 'Everything whose attributes are originated is originated.' Then we prove the minor by our statement: 'Its attributes are changing' and 'Everything changing is originated.' The first of these two premises is necessary. And we prove the second of them by saying: 'Change: if it is from non-existence to existence, then existence is accidental; and if it is from existence to non-existence, then existence is contingent' and 'The contingent only occurs as originated.' And we prove the major of the first syllogism by our statement: 'Everything whose attributes are originated is not free from originated events' and 'Everything not free from originated events does not precede them' and 'Everything that does not precede originated events is originated.' So we have arrived at necessity, and no heed is given to the objection of some philosophers to some of those premises, for that is stubbornness.
(1) Circularity occurs if we return to the first thing depended upon. Infinite regress occurs if we go on without end. So it is necessary that the premises be necessary or end in necessity.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage states four general rules for all four figures of the categorical syllogism. First, the conclusion follows the weaker premise: if a premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if a premise is negative, the conclusion is negative. This is because the conclusion cannot be stronger than the weakest premise. Second, these figures apply not only to categorical syllogisms but also to conditional ones, correcting the poet (likely the author of the matn). Third, it is permissible to omit a premise or the conclusion if it is obvious, as in the example of flogging a fornicator: saying 'This person is flogged because he is a fornicator' implies the major premise 'Every fornicator is flogged'. Fourth, premises must ultimately be necessary to avoid circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The passage then gives examples: 'This number is divisible into two equal parts, therefore it is even' has necessary premises. A more complex example about the world's attributes being originated shows how theoretical premises can be traced back to necessary premises through a chain of syllogisms. The footnote reiterates the two fallacies to avoid.

Key Terms
النتيجة تتبع الأخس (al-natīja tattabiʿ al-akhass): The conclusion follows the weaker premise in quality and quantity; the weaker is negative in quality and particular in quantity.
دور (dawr): Circular reasoning; when the proof of something depends on that which itself depends on the thing being proved.
تسلسل (tasalsul): Infinite regress; an endless chain of dependencies in proofs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The four rules ensure that categorical syllogisms are valid and grounded in necessary premises, preventing circularity and infinite regress.

Introduction to the Conditional Syllogism
Author introduces the conditional syllogism, its names, structure, and components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a conditional syllogism and distinguish its major and minor premises.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism gets its name from the exception particle in the minor premise, not from being exceptional.

The author introduces a new type of syllogism: the conditional (or exceptional) syllogism. Unlike the categorical syllogism which uses universal propositions, this type relies on a conditional major premise and a minor premise introduced by the particle 'but'. Understanding its structure is the first step to mastering propositional logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two names
The text states it is called 'exceptional' because it contains the particle of exception 'but', and 'conditional' because its major premise is a conditional proposition.
The names reflect its components.
2
Locate the major premise
The major premise is a conditional proposition (if...then), and it is called 'al-kubra'.
3
Locate the minor premise
The minor premise contains the exception particle 'but' and is called 'al-sughra'. It affirms or denies one part of the conditional.
The exception particle is 'lākin'.
4
Memorize the matn verse
The verse says: 'And from it is what is called by exception, Known by the condition, without doubt.' This anchors the definition.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Valid and Invalid Moods of the Connected Conditional Syllogism
Definition and inference rules for connected conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid moods in a connected conditional syllogism and construct valid inferences using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields a valid conclusion; the reverse does not.

The conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) is unique because its conclusion appears explicitly in the premises. In the connected conditional (sharti muttasil), the two valid forms are modus ponens and modus tollens. However, students often mistakenly think that affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent are valid. This lesson will show you why those are fallacious, using the classical example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the 'if-then' statement in the syllogism. It is called the major premise and has two parts: antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). The antecedent is the necessary condition (malzum), and the consequent is the necessary concomitant (lazim).
In the example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal' – 'human' is the antecedent, 'animal' is the consequent.
2
Spot the exceptive premise
Find the premise that begins with an exceptive particle like 'but' or 'however'. This is the minor premise, which either affirms or denies one of the two parts.
In the text: 'but it is a human' or 'but it is not an animal'.
3
Apply the valid rules
If the minor premise affirms the antecedent, conclude the consequent (modus ponens). If it denies the consequent, conclude the denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). Check that the inference follows the pattern: affirming malzum yields lazim; denying lazim yields denial of malzum.
Example: 'but it is a human' → 'therefore it is an animal'. 'but it not an animal' → 'therefore it is not a human'.
4
Check for fallacious moods
If the minor premise affirms the consequent or denies the antecedent, the syllogism is infertile. Reason: the consequent may be broader than the antecedent, so affirming it does not guarantee the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not guarantee denial of the consequent.
Example: 'but it is an animal' does not prove it is a human; 'but it is not a human' does not prove it is not an animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

102
وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ وَلا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى
تعريف القياس الاستثنائي: هو القياس الذي دلّ على النتيجة أو ضدها بأن تكون مذكورة فيه بالفعل أي بصورتها لا بالقوة أي لا تكون مفرقة الأجزاء، كما في القياس الاقتراني، فإن نتيجته موجودة لكنها متفرقة الأجزاء في مقدمتيه، موضوعها في الصغرى ومحمولها في الكبرى.
وأما القياس الاستثنائي ففيه عين النتيجة أو نقيضها بصورته، كما يأتي.
القياس الشرطي المتصل:
ضروبه المنتجة: القضية الشرطية إن كانت متصلة أنتج إثبات المقدم إثبات التالي، وأنتج نفي التالي نفي المقدم. ومثال ذلك كلما كان هذا إنساناً، كان حيواناً ، لكنه إنسان] ينتج [فهو حيوان]، فقد أنتج إثبات المقدم إثبات التالي، لأن المقدم ملزوم والتالي لازم ويلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم. ولو قلت في هذا المثال الكنه ليس بحيوان ] أنتج فهو ليس بإنسان لأن رفع اللازم يوجب رفع الملزوم، فعلم أن المنتج منه ضربان. الضروب العقيمة:
ولا يلزم الإنتاج من عكسها، أي من وضع التالي أو رفع المقدم، فلو قلت في المثال المتقدم [لكنه حيوان] لم ينتج أنه إنسان، لأن اللازم قد يكون أعم من الملزوم، ولا يلزم من إثبات الأعم إثبات الأخص، وكذا لو قلت الكنه ليس بإنسان ] لا ينتج شيئاً، لأن رفع الأخص لا يوجب رفع الأعم، والملزوم هنا أخص من لازمه. وقد يكون اللازم أعم من ملزومه فلا يلزم من إثباته إثبات ملزومه ولا من نفي ملزومه الأخص نفيه، فهذان ضربان عقيمان.
Chapter on the conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). This type is called istithna'i because it includes the particle of exception, which is [lākin]. And this type is known as conditional because it includes a conditional premise, which is called the major, and the premise that contains the particle of exception is called the minor. He said: 99. And from it is what is called istithna'i, known by the conditional without doubt. 100. And it is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite in actuality, not in potentiality. 101. So if the conditional is connected, then positing that [the antecedent] produces positing the consequent, 102. And removing the consequent removes the first, but the converse is not necessary, as is clear. Definition of the conditional syllogism: It is the syllogism that indicates the conclusion or its opposite by being mentioned in it actually, i.e., in its form, not potentially, i.e., not with its parts scattered, as in the conjunctive syllogism, for its conclusion exists but its parts are scattered in its two premises, its subject in the minor and its predicate in the major. As for the conditional syllogism, it contains the very conclusion or its contradictory in its form, as will come. The connected conditional: Its productive modes: If the conditional is connected, then affirming the antecedent affirms the consequent, and denying the consequent denies the antecedent. An example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human' yields 'So it is an animal.' So affirming the antecedent affirmed the consequent, because the antecedent is a necessary condition (malzum) and the consequent is necessary (lazim), and from the existence of the malzum follows the existence of the lazim. And if you say in this example 'but it is not an animal', it yields 'So it is not a human' because removing the lazim necessitates removing the malzum. So know that the productive ones are two. The infertile modes: And production does not result from its converse, i.e., from positing the consequent or removing the antecedent. So if you say in the previous example 'but it is an animal', it does not produce that it is a human, because the lazim may be more general than the malzum, and from affirming the general, the specific is not affirmed. Similarly, if you say 'but it is not a human', it produces nothing, because removing the specific does not necessitate removing the general, and here the malzum is more specific than its lazim. And the lazim may be more general than its malzum, so from affirming it, its malzum is not affirmed, nor from denying the more specific malzum is it denied. So these are two infertile modes.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a combination of matn (poetry) and sharh (commentary) that defines the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and then explains the connected conditional (muttasil) in detail. The author first explains that this type of syllogism is called 'istithna'i' because it uses an exceptive particle (like 'but' or 'except') and 'conditional' because it contains a conditional premise. The poetry states that it is a syllogism that explicitly brings the conclusion or its opposite in actuality, not in potentiality like the conjunctive syllogism where the conclusion's parts are scattered. The commentary then elaborates: in the istithna'i, the conclusion (or its contradictory) appears as one of the premises, whereas in the conjunctive syllogism, the conclusion is only implicit. The author then turns to the connected conditional, which is a conditional with 'if-then' structure. He gives the two productive moods: affirming the antecedent yields affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent. He illustrates with a classical example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human' (affirming antecedent) yields 'it is an animal'. The reason is that the antecedent is the necessary condition (malzum) and the consequent is the necessary concomitant (lazim). Similarly, 'but it is not an animal' (denying consequent) yields 'it is not a human'. Then he presents the two infertile moods: denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent are invalid, because the consequent may be broader than the antecedent. Using the same example: if we say 'it is an animal', we cannot conclude it is a human, because many animals are not human. Conversely, 'it is not a human' does not allow us to conclude it is not an animal, because a non-human could still be an animal. This is the standard logic of the conditional syllogism in the Islamic tradition.

الشرطي المتصل

A) But it is forbidden.
B) But it is not forbidden.
C) But it is wine.
D) Both B and C are valid.

Key Takeaway
In a connected conditional syllogism, only affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens) are valid; the other two are fallacies due to the generality of the consequent.

Construct a valid connected conditional syllogism on a fiqh topic: use 'If something is nabidh (date drink), it is intoxicating; but it is nabidh' and state the valid conclusion.
?
