The Three Types of Disjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types and gives inference rules in poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each type of disjunctive conditional (haqiqiyya, mani' jam', mani' khuluw) and determine which inferences are valid for each type.

Core Idea
Each disjunction type licenses a different inference pattern.

In this lesson, we examine the three types of disjunctive conditionals as classified in the classical logical tradition. The poetry concisely states the valid inference rules for each type. Mastering these distinctions is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where disjunctive reasoning frequently appears.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the commentary, note the three types: haqiqiyya (real exclusive), mani' jam' (preventer of conjunction), and mani' khuluw (preventer of emptiness). Understand their defining characteristics: haqiqiyya prevents both conjunction and emptiness; mani' jam' only prevents conjunction; mani' khuluw only prevents emptiness.
2
Understand the rule for haqiqiyya
As the poetry says: positing one yields removal of the other, and the converse is likewise. Both directions are valid: if A is true then B is false, and if A is false then B is true.
This is the most common type in exclusive disjunctions.
3
Understand the rule for mani' jam'
By positing this, removal of that is known, but not the converse. So if A is true then B is false, but if A is false it does not imply B is true (since both could be false).
Example: 'This is either a book or a pen' – if it is a book, it is not a pen; but if it is not a book, it could be a pen or something else.
4
Understand the rule for mani' khuluw
The opposite of mani' jam': removing one implies positing the other, but positing one does not imply removing the other. So if A is false then B is true, but if A is true it does not imply B is false (since both could be true).
Example: 'This is either an animal or alive' – if it is not an animal, it must be alive; but if it is an animal, it could still be alive.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a kalam example: 'Either the world is temporally originated or it is eternal.' This is haqiqiyya (both cannot be true and both cannot be false). So valid inferences: if it is originated, it is not eternal; if it is not originated, it is eternal.
Practice by constructing similar examples from fiqh or usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً فَوَضْعُ يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ وَالعَكْسُ كَذا
وَذَاكَ فِي الْأَخَصُ ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْع فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ رَفْعٌ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا
القياس الشرطي المنفصل:
إن كانت القضية الشرطية منفصلة، فهي على ثلاثة أقسام: حقيقية ومانعة جمع ومانعة خلو.
And if it is disjunctive, then positing one produces the removal of the other, and the converse is likewise. And that is in the most specific (haqiqiyya). Then if it is a preventer of conjunction (mani' jam'), then by positing this, the removal of that is known without the converse. And if it is a preventer of emptiness (mani' khuluw), then it is the opposite of that. The disjunctive conditional: If the conditional statement is disjunctive, then it is of three types: real (haqiqiyya), preventer of conjunction, and preventer of emptiness.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage contains the poetic summary of the inference rules for the disjunctive conditional syllogism and a short prose classification. The poetry gives three rules corresponding to three types: For the real exclusive disjunction (haqiqiyya), which is the 'most specific' type, positing one disjunct produces the removal of the other, and conversely, removing one produces positing the other. That is, both directions are valid: if A or B (exclusive), then not-A implies B and not-B implies A, and also A implies not-B and B implies not-A. Next, for the type called 'preventer of conjunction' (mani' jam'), which is a disjunction that only excludes the coexistence of both (but allows both to be false), the rule is: positing one disjunct implies the removal of the other; but the converse (removing one implying positing the other) does not hold. For example, 'This is either a book or a pen' (but it could be neither) — if it is a book, it is not a pen; but if it is not a book, it could still be a pen or something else. Finally, for the type called 'preventer of emptiness' (mani' khuluw), which is a disjunction that only excludes the absence of both (i.e., at least one must be true, but both could be true), the rule is the opposite of the previous: removing one implies positing the other, but positing one does not imply removing the other. For example, 'This is either an animal or alive' — if it is not an animal, it must be alive; but if it is an animal, it could also be alive. The commentary simply states the three types without further explanation, relying on the poetry for the rules.

Key Terms
الشرطي المنفصل
الحقيقية
مانعة جمع
مانعة خلو

B) If A is false, then B is true.
C) If B is true, then A is true.
D) If B is false, then A is false.

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunction each have distinct inference rules; mixing them destroys argument validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of disjunction in the following statement: 'Either the act is wajib or mubah.' (Assume an act cannot be both wajib and mubah, but could it be neither? If it could be neither, then it is mani' jam'.) Then state the valid inference: if it is wajib, it is not mubah. But if it is not wajib, it does not imply it is mubah.
?
Construct a valid disjunctive syllogism using a haqiqiyya disjunction from usul al-fiqh. For example: 'Either the cause of prohibition is excess or delay. It is not excess. Therefore, it is delay.'

The Conditional Exceptional Syllogism: Productive and Non-Productive Forms
Author defines exceptional syllogism and explains productive and non-productive forms of conditional connected syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two productive and two non-productive forms of the connected conditional exceptional syllogism and explain why the non-productive forms fail.

Core Idea
In a conditional syllogism, only affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent yield valid conclusions; the converse forms are fallacies.

In this passage, the author defines the exceptional syllogism and then focuses on the connected conditional type. You will learn the two valid forms (modus ponens and modus tollens) and the two invalid forms that are often mistakenly used. This understanding is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of exceptional syllogism
Notice that the exceptional syllogism presents the conclusion explicitly in its form, unlike the conjunctive syllogism where the conclusion is dispersed across premises. This is a key distinction.
The author contrasts it with the conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyās al-iqtirānī).
2
Distinguish the connected conditional
The connected conditional has the form 'if A then B' where A is the antecedent (muqaddam) and B is the consequent (tālī). The antecedent is considered the necessary condition (malzūm) and the consequent the necessary concomitant (lāzim).
Understand the Arabic terms: المقدم (muqaddam) and التالي (tālī).
3
Learn the two productive forms
Affirming the antecedent (waḍ' al-muqaddam) yields the consequent, and denying the consequent (raf' al-tālī) yields denial of the antecedent. The author gives the example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal; it is a human, therefore it is an animal' (ponens) and 'but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human' (tollens).
The logical reason: existence of the condition necessitates the concomitant; absence of the concomitant necessitates absence of the condition.
4
Recognize the non-productive forms
Affirming the consequent (e.g., 'it is an animal') does not yield the antecedent because the consequent may be more general; denying the antecedent (e.g., 'it is not a human') does not deny the consequent because the antecedent is more specific. The author calls these 'infertile' (ʿuqaym).
Generality/specificity relation prevents the inference; this is a common fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهْوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ أَوْ ضِدّها بِالفِعْل لا بِالقُوَّةِ فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا اتِّصَالِ أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ التَّالِي وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ وَلا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً فَوَضْعُ يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ وَالعَكْسُ كَذا وَذَاكَ فِي الْأَخَصُ ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْع فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ رَفْعٌ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا. تعريف القياس الاستثنائي: هو القياس الذي دلَّ على النتيجة أو ضدها بأن تكون مذكورة فيه بالفعل أي بصورتها لا بالقوة أي لا تكون مفرقة الأجزاء، كما في القياس الاقتراني، فإن نتيجته موجودة لكنها متفرقة الأجزاء في مقدمتيه، موضوعها في الصغرى ومحمولها في الكبرى. وأما القياس الاستثنائي ففيه عين النتيجة أو نقيضها بصورته، كما يأتي. القياس الشرطي المتصل: ضروبه المنتجة: القضية الشرطية إن كانت متصلة أنتج إثبات المقدم إثبات التالي، وأنتج نفي التالي نفي المقدم. ومثال ذلك كلما كان هذا إنساناً، كان حيواناً ، لكنه إنسان ينتج فهو حيوان، فقد أنتج إثبات المقدم إثبات التالي، لأن المقدم ملزوم والتالي لازم ويلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم. ولو قلت في هذا المثال لكنه ليس بحيوان أنتج فهو ليس بإنسان لأن رفع اللازم يوجب رفع الملزوم، فعلم أن المنتج منه ضربان. الضروب العقيمة: ولا يلزم الإنتاج من عكسها، أي من وضع التالي أو رفع المقدم، فلو قلت في المثال المتقدم لكنه حيوان لم ينتج أنه إنسان، لأن اللازم قد يكون أعم من الملزوم، ولا يلزم من إثبات الأعم إثبات الأخص، وكذا لو قلت لكنه ليس بإنسان لا ينتج شيئاً، لأن رفع الأخص لا يوجب رفع الأعم، والملزوم هنا أخص من لازمه. وقد يكون اللازم أعم من ملزومه فلا يلزم من إثباته إثبات ملزومه ولا من نفي ملزومه الأخص نفيه، فهذان ضربان عقيمان.
And it is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite in actuality not in potentiality. If the conditional is connected, placing that (the antecedent) yields placing the consequent, and removing the consequent removes the first (antecedent), and the reverse does not follow because it is clear. And if it is disjunctive, placing one yields removing that, and the reverse likewise, and that is in the exclusive (haqiqiyya). Then if it is a preventing of conjunction, by placing this it is known that removal of that without reversal; and if it is a preventing of void, then it is the reverse of that. Definition of the exceptional syllogism: It is the syllogism which indicates the conclusion or its opposite by being mentioned in it actually i.e. in its form, not potentially i.e. not with its parts dispersed, as in the conjunctive syllogism; for its conclusion exists but its parts are dispersed in its two premises, its subject in the minor and its predicate in the major. As for the exceptional syllogism, it contains the very conclusion or its contradictory in its form, as will come. The connected conditional syllogism: Its productive forms: If the conditional proposition is connected, affirming the antecedent entails affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent entails denying the antecedent. An example of that: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human' yields 'so it is an animal.' Thus affirming the antecedent entailed affirming the consequent, because the antecedent is the necessary condition and the consequent is the necessary concomitant, and the existence of the necessary condition entails the existence of the necessary concomitant. And if you said in this example 'but it is not an animal' it yields 'so it is not a human' because removing the necessary concomitant necessitates removing the necessary condition. So it is known that the productive from it is two types. The non-productive forms: The production does not follow from their converse, i.e. from placing the consequent or removing the antecedent. For example, if you said in the previous example 'but it is an animal' it does not yield that it is a human, because the necessary concomitant may be more general than the necessary condition, and proving the more general does not entail proving the more specific. Likewise if you said 'but it is not a human' it does not yield anything, because removing the more specific does not necessitate removing the more general, and the necessary condition here is more specific than its necessary concomitant. And the necessary concomitant may be more general than its necessary condition, so proving it does not entail proving its necessary condition, nor does removing its more specific necessary condition entail removing it. So these are two non-productive types.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptional syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī) and goes into detail on the connected conditional (sharṭī muttaṣil) form. First, the matn lines give the poetic summary: In a conditional syllogism, affirming the antecedent gives the consequent, and denying the consequent denies the antecedent; the converse does not hold. For disjunctive, it will come later. The commentary then expands: The exceptional syllogism differs from the conjunctive (iqtirānī) in that the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in its form, not split between premises. For the connected conditional, there are two productive forms: 1) affirming the antecedent yields the consequent (modus ponens), 2) denying the consequent yields denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). The example 'If it is a human, it is an animal; it is a human, therefore it is an animal' illustrates ponens. The example 'but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human' illustrates tollens. The reason is that the antecedent is the necessary condition (malzūm) and the consequent is the necessary concomitant (lāzim). Existence of the condition necessitates existence of the concomitant, and absence of the concomitant necessitates absence of the condition. Then two non-productive forms are explained: affirming the consequent (e.g., 'it is an animal') does not yield the antecedent, because the consequent may be more general (a'amm) than the antecedent, and proving the general does not prove the specific. Denying the antecedent (e.g., 'it is not a human') does not deny the consequent, because the antecedent is more specific (akhass) and its removal does not remove the general. These two are 'infertile' types. The discussion uses the logical relation of generality/specificity, which is crucial in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway
In a connected conditional syllogism, only two inferences are valid: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields denial of the antecedent; the other two are fallacies.

The Syllogism

Exclusive Disjunctive Syllogism (Haqiqiyya)
Author explains exclusive disjunction and its inference rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an exclusive disjunctive syllogism and verify the validity of both affirming and denying one disjunct.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction means exactly one true—so each side tells you the opposite.

The first type of disjunctive syllogism is the exclusive (haqiqiyya), where the two alternatives cannot both be true nor both false. This is the most familiar 'either-or' in logical reasoning, used frequently in kalam and fiqh to eliminate impossible options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Look for words like 'إما ... أو' and whether the alternatives are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. In haqiqiyya, affirming one forces denial of the other, and vice versa.
2
Apply the rule of affirmation
If you affirm one disjunct (e.g., 'al-mawjud qadim'), you must deny the other ('laysa bi hadith'). Check the example in the text.
3
Apply the rule of denial
If you deny one disjunct (e.g., 'laysa bi qadim'), you must affirm the other ('huwa hadith'). Verify that the author shows this.
4
Contrast with other types
In exclusive disjunction, both operations are valid. This is not the case in the other two types, which we will study separately.
Keep this distinction in mind.

القياس الشرطي المنفصل: إن كانت القضية الشرطية منفصلة، فهي على ثلاثة أقسام: حقيقية ومانعة جمع ومانعة خلو. الشرطية الحقيقية: إثبات أحد طرفيها ينتج رفع الطرف الآخر، ورفع أحد طرفيها ينتج وضع الآخر ، كقولنا [الموجود إما قديم أو حادث لكنه قديم ] ينتج [أنه ليس بحادث]، أو [لكنه حادث] ينتج [أنه ليس بقديم]، فلو قلت [لكنه ليس بقديم] أنتج [أنه حادث]، أو أنه ليس بحادث أنتج [أنه قديم]. فقد أنتج وضع أحد الطرفين رفع الآخر، ورفع أحد الطرفين وضع الآخر. القضية الشرطية مانعة الجمع: إن أثبت أحد طرفيها ، ينفى الطرف الآخر ، دون العكس، فلا يلزم من رفع أحد طرفيها وضع الآخر لمنعها الجمع بينهما. مثالها أن تقول: [هذا إما أسود أو أبيض، لكنه أسود ينتج أنه غير أبيض ] أو [لكنه أبيض ينتج أنه غير أسود]. ولو قلت [ لكنه ليس بأسود] لم ينتج أنه أبيض، ولا غير أبيض، وكذا لو قلت [لكنه ليس بأبيض ] لم ينتج أنه [أسود أو غير أسود]. القضية الشرطية مانعة الخلو: القضية مانعة الخلو عكس مانعة الجمع، بمعنى أن رفع أحد طرفيها ينتج وضع الأخرى، لمنعها الخلو عنهما، ووضع أحد طرفيها لا ينتج شيئاً لجواز الجمع بينهما. مثالها أن تقول: [هذا الشيء إما غير أبيض أو غير أسود لكنه أبيض ينتج أنه غير أسود] أو [لكنه أسود ينتج أنه غير أبيض، فقد لزم من رفع أحد طرفيها ، ثبوت الآخر، ولو قلت [لكنه غير أبيض]، لم ينتج [أنه أسود]، ولا غيره، أو قلتَ [لكنه غير أسود ، لم ينتج [أنه أبيض] ولا غيره.
The disconnected conditional syllogism: If the conditional proposition is disjunctive, it has three types: exclusive (haqiqiyya), preventing conjunction, and preventing void. The exclusive conditional: Affirming one of its two ends yields the removal of the other end, and removing one of its two ends yields the positing of the other, as our saying: 'The existent is either eternal or originated, but it is eternal' yields 'it is not originated'; or 'but it is originated' yields 'it is not eternal'. So if you said 'but it is not eternal' it yields 'it is originated', or 'it is not originated' yields 'it is eternal'. Thus affirming one of the two ends yielded the removal of the other, and removing one of the two ends yielded the positing of the other. The conditional proposition preventing conjunction: If you affirm one of its two ends, the other end is negated, without the converse, so it does not follow from removing one of its two ends that the other is posited, due to its preventing conjunction between them. Its example is that you say: 'This is either black or white, but it is black' yields 'it is not white'; or 'but it is white' yields 'it is not black'. And if you said 'but it is not black', it does not yield that it is white, nor not white; likewise if you said 'but it is not white', it does not yield that it is black or not black. The conditional proposition preventing void: The proposition preventing void is the reverse of preventing conjunction, in the sense that removing one of its two ends yields the positing of the other, due to its preventing void from both, and positing one of its two ends does not yield anything because conjunction between them is permissible. Its example is that you say: 'This thing is either not white or not black, but it is white' yields 'it is not black'; or 'but it is black' yields 'it is not white'. So from removing one of its two ends, the establishment of the other follows. And if you said 'but it is not white', it does not yield that it is black, nor other; or if you said 'but it is not black', it does not yield that it is white, nor other.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage covers the disjunctive exceptional syllogism (sharṭī munfaṣil). The author first states that if the conditional premise is disjunctive ('either A or B' type), it comes in three kinds: 1) Exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya) – where exactly one of the two is true (true exclusive or). 2) Preventing conjunction (māni'at al-jam') – where both cannot be true together, but both can be false. 3) Preventing void (māni'at al-khuluw) – where both cannot be false together, but both can be true. For each, the inference rules are given. For exclusive: affirming one yields denying the other; denying one yields affirming the other. The example uses 'the existent is either eternal or originated' (a classic kalām example). For preventing conjunction: only affirmation is productive (affirming one denies the other); denying one gives nothing because both could be false. Example: 'black or white' – affirming one denies the other; denying one does not imply the other. For preventing void: only denial is productive (denying one affirms the other); affirming one gives nothing because both could be true. Example: 'this thing is either not white or not black' – if you deny one (e.g., affirm it is white, which removes the first disjunct 'not white'), you get the affirmation of the other (not black). If you affirm one disjunct, say 'it is not white', you cannot conclude because both could be true (both 'not white' and 'not black' could be true). This analysis is crucial for distinguishing valid from invalid inferences in disjunctive reasoning, especially in legal and theological arguments.

Key Terms
الشرطية الحقيقية
طرف
القياس الشرطي المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an exclusive disjunctive syllogism (haqiqiyya), if we deny one disjunct, what follows?
A) The other disjunct is affirmed
B) The other disjunct is denied
C) Nothing follows
D) It depends on the context

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunction allows both affirming and denying one side to produce a valid conclusion about the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an exclusive disjunctive syllogism using the pair 'the Qur'an is either created or uncreated' and test the two valid inferences.

Preventing Conjunction Disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam')
Author explains preventing conjunction disjunction and its one-way inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a preventing conjunction disjunction and determine that only affirming a disjunct yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
When both disjuncts cannot be true but can be false, only affirmation gives information.

The second type of disjunction prevents the conjunction (both true) but allows both to be false. This is common in classifications where an item cannot have two contradictory properties but may have neither—like 'black or white' where something can be neither if it is red.

Guided Path
1
Understand the property
In mani'at al-jam', the two disjuncts cannot both be true, but they can both be false. So if you know one is true, the other must be false. But if you know one is false, you cannot infer the truth of the other.
2
Apply affirmation rule
If you affirm one disjunct (e.g., 'aswad', black), you can conclude the other is false ('laysa abyad', not white). Verify with the text's example.
3
Check denial rule
If you deny one disjunct (e.g., 'laysa aswad', not black), you cannot conclude anything about the other. The text says 'لا ينتج أنه أبيض، ولا غير أبيض'.
4
Compare with haqiqiyya
In haqiqiyya, denial is productive; here it is not. This is the key difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Preventing Void Disjunction (Mani'at al-Khuluw)
Author explains preventing void disjunction and its one-way inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a preventing void disjunction and determine that only denying a disjunct yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
When both disjuncts cannot be false but can be true, only denial gives information.

The third type prevents void (both false) but allows both to be true. This is the inverse of preventing conjunction. It appears in cases where at least one of two options must hold, but both could hold—like 'either not white or not black' where an object cannot be both white and black (so cannot be false in both negations) but can be neither white nor black? Actually, the example forces understanding: if it is white, then 'not white' is false, so to prevent void, 'not black' must be true. So denial of 'not white' affirms 'not black'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the property
In mani'at al-khuluw, the two disjuncts cannot both be false, but they can both be true. So if one is false, the other must be true. But if one is true, the other may be true or false.
2
Apply denial rule
If you deny one disjunct (i.e., make it false), you can conclude the other is true. In the example, denying 'not white' (by saying 'it is white') yields 'it is not black'.
3
Check affirmation rule
If you affirm one disjunct (e.g., 'it is not white'), you cannot conclude about the other. The text: 'لو قلت لكنه غير أبيض، لم ينتج أنه أسود ولا غيره'.
4
Relate to the other types
This is the mirror image of preventing conjunction. Understanding this pattern helps in argument analysis in kalam and usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Compound Syllogism: Structure and Mechanics
Author defines compound syllogism, explains its structure with an example, and mentions induction and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a compound syllogism from a chain of premises and verify its stepwise validity.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms chain simple syllogisms together; each step's conclusion becomes the next premise.

This lesson introduces the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab), which extends the simple syllogism by chaining multiple premises. You will learn how to decompose a chain into simple steps and understand the difference between certain syllogisms and weaker forms like induction and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of premises
Find the series of universal propositions in the passage (e.g., 'Every human is animal', 'Every animal is sentient', 'Every sentient is sleeping', etc.). These form the chain.
2
Combine the first two premises
Apply categorical syllogism rules to the first two premises to derive an intermediate conclusion (e.g., 'Every human is sentient').
This is a Barbara syllogism (AAA-1).
3
Chain the conclusion with the next premise
Use the intermediate conclusion as a minor premise and combine it with the next major premise (e.g., 'Every sentient is sleeping') to get a new conclusion.
4
Repeat until all premises are used
Continue the process: each new conclusion becomes a premise for the next syllogism, until all original premises are exhausted.
5
Verify the final conclusion follows
Check that the ultimate conclusion (e.g., 'Every human is composite') is logically derived from the chain and that each sub-syllogism is valid.
All sub-syllogisms in this example are valid first-figure Barbara.

Chapter on the Appendices of Syllogism. The syllogism is either from two premises or more. The first is called a simple syllogism, and the second is called a compound syllogism. He said: And from it is what they call compound, because it is composed of proofs that have been combined. Its composition, if you wish to know it: take the conclusion with its premise. A conclusion necessarily follows from combining it with another, so on continuously. The connected series of conclusions it contains, or its separated ones, all the same. And if by a particular on a universal one argues, then that is induction (istiqra') as they understand. Its opposite is called the logical syllogism, which I have presented, so verify. And when a particular is predicated of a particular for a commonality, then that is called analogy (tamthīl). And the syllogism of induction and analogy does not yield certainty by proof. I say: The compound syllogism is what is composed of more than two premises, and in reality it is composed of simple syllogisms. Example of that is our saying: 'Every human is an animal', 'Every animal is sentient', 'Every sentient is sleeping', 'Every sleeping is body', 'Every body is composite'. The analysis of the syllogism: you combine it from two premises, so you consider the first premise and take with it the second premise, and the conclusion from them you make as a premise for the one after it, and so on. So you say in the previous example: 'Every human is animal' and 'Every animal is sentient' therefore 'Every human is sentient'. This is the conclusion of the first two premises. Make it a minor premise and attach it to what follows: say 'Every human is sentient' and 'Every sentient is sleeping', and extract from these two a conclusion: 'Every human is sleeping'. Then make this a premise for a second syllogism: say 'Every human is sleeping' and 'Every sleeping is body', and so on.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage introduces the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab). The author first distinguishes between simple syllogisms (two premises) and compound syllogisms (more than two premises). The poem then gives rules for composing them: 'take the conclusion with its premise' meaning that after deriving a conclusion from two premises, use that conclusion as a premise in the next syllogism. The poem also mentions induction (istiqra') where a particular is argued from a universal (actually the text says 'by a particular on a universal' meaning arguing from particular instances to a universal rule? Actually it says 'إذا بجزي على كل استدل' meaning 'if one argues by a particular on a universal' that is induction. And its opposite (i.e. from universal to particular) is the logical syllogism. Then 'when a particular is predicated of a particular for a commonality' that is analogy (tamthīl). The poem states that induction and analogy do not yield certain proof. This is a standard view in Islamic logic. After the poem, the author explains the compound syllogism concretely with an example: a chain of five premises leading from 'human' to 'composite'. He then shows the step-by-step process of combining premises two at a time: first combine 'human is animal' and 'animal is sentient' to get 'human is sentient', then use that to combine with 'sentient is sleeping' to get 'human is sleeping', then combine with 'sleeping is body' to get 'human is body', then combine with 'body is composite' to get 'human is composite'. This illustrates the mechanics of a compound syllogism (also called a sorites or chain syllogism). The author notes that it is 'in reality composed of simple syllogisms'.

Compound Syllogism: Definition and Analysis
Author defines compound syllogism, explains its analysis, and distinguishes connected and disconnected results.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a compound syllogism by breaking it into simple syllogisms and distinguish between connected and disconnected types.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of simple syllogisms where each result becomes a premise for the next.

In this lesson, we explore how syllogisms can be chained together to form longer arguments. The author defines the compound syllogism and shows how to analyze it step by step. You will learn to identify the intermediate results and understand the difference between explicitly stating them or leaving them implicit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of premises
List all the premises in the compound syllogism in order. In the example: Every human is an animal, every animal is sentient, every sentient is sleeping, every sleeping is a body, every body is composite.
2
Break into simple syllogisms
Take the first two premises and derive an intermediate result. Then use that result with the third premise, and so on. This reveals the underlying simple syllogisms.
Each simple syllogism must satisfy the conditions of the first figure if applicable.
3
Track the shifting role of propositions
Notice that the result of one simple syllogism becomes a premise (usually a minor premise) in the next simple syllogism. A proposition is called a 'result' when it is sought, and a 'premise' when it is known.
This relative terminology is crucial for understanding compound reasoning.
4
Distinguish connected vs disconnected
In a connected-result compound syllogism, all intermediate results are explicitly stated. In a disconnected-result compound, only the final conclusion is given, and the intermediate results are implied. Both are equally valid.
The source says: 'both are equal in conveying the desired conclusion.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في [لَوَاحِقُ القِيَاسِ] القياس إما أن يكون من مقدمتين أو أكثر ، فالأول يسمى قياساً بسيطاً، والثاني يسمى قياساً مركباً. قال: وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ مُرَكَّبَا لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ حُجَجٍ قَدْ رُكْبَا فَرَكْبَتْهُ إِنْ تُرِدْ أَنْ تَعْلَمَهُ وَاقْلِبْ نَتِيْجَةُ بِهِ مُقَدِّمَهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَركيبهَا بِأَخْرَى نَتِيْجَةٌ إِلَى هَلَمَّ جَرًا مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِحِ الَّذِي حَـوَى يَكُونُ أَوْ مَفْصُولَها كُلٌّ سَوَا أقول: فالقياس المركب ما ألف من أكثر من مقدمتين، وهو في الحقيقة يكون مؤلفاً من قياسات بسيطة. مثال ذلك قولنا: كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حساس وكل حساس نام وكل نام جسم وكل جسم مركب. فتحليل القياس أن تركبه من مقدمتين فتعتبر المقدمة الأولى، وتأخذ معها المقدمة الثانية، والنتيجة منها تجعلها للمقدمة التي بعدها، وهكذا. فتقول في المثال السابق كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حساس فكل إنسان حساس، فهذه نتيجة المقدمتين الأوليين فاجعلها مقدمة صغرى وضمها لما بعدها فقل: كل إنسان حساس وكل حساس نام، واستخرج من هاتين نتيجة فقل: كل إنسان نام، ثم اجعل هذه مقدمة لقياس ثان فقل: كل إنسان نام وكل نام جسم، وهكذا. فالحاصل أن نتيجة قياس ما يمكن أن تجعل مقدمة لقياس آخر، فكونها نتيجة أمر اعتباري، فهي من حيث هي مطلوبة تسمى نتيجة، وإذا عرفت تسمى مقدمة وقضية أيضاً، فكل قياس مركب يمكن تحليله إلى قياسات بسيطة، فيطبق على هذه القياسات البسيطة القوانين التي سبق شرحها. منفصل النتائج ومتصلها: وفي القياس المركب، إذا لم يتم ذكر نتيجة لكل قياس بسيط فيه، يسمى هذا القياس المركب منفصل النتائج، فتكون النتائج مطوية فيه غير مذكورة، وأما ما ذكرت فيه النتائج فيسمى قياساً متصل النتائج، ولكن كل من القياس منفصل النتائج ومتصلها مستويان في إفادة المطلوب.
Chapter on the Appendages of Syllogism. The syllogism is either from two premises or more; the first is called simple syllogism, and the second is called compound syllogism. He said: 'And among it is what they call compound, because it is composed of arguments that are combined. If you wish to know its composition, make its result a premise for it. From its composition with another, a result necessarily follows, continuously. It is either connected in its results or separated; both are equal.' I say: The compound syllogism is that which is composed of more than two premises, and in reality it is composed of simple syllogisms. An example of that is our saying: Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient, and every sentient is sleeping, and every sleeping is a body, and every body is composite. The analysis of the syllogism is that you compose it from two premises: you take the first premise, and you take with it the second premise, and their result you make a premise for what comes after, and so on. So you say in the previous example: Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient, so every human is sentient. This is the result of the first two premises; make it a minor premise and join it to what comes after, saying: Every human is sentient, and every sentient is sleeping, and extract from these two a result, saying: Every human is sleeping. Then make this a premise for a second syllogism, saying: Every human is sleeping, and every sleeping is a body, and so on. The gist is that the result of a syllogism can be made a premise for another syllogism; its being a result is relative. Insofar as it is sought, it is called a result, and when known it is called a premise and a proposition also. Every compound syllogism can be analyzed into simple syllogisms, and the laws previously explained apply to these simple syllogisms. Disconnected and connected results: In the compound syllogism, if the result of each simple syllogism within it is not mentioned, this compound syllogism is called 'disconnected in its results', so the results are implicit and not mentioned. As for that in which the results are mentioned, it is called a 'connected-result syllogism'. But both the disconnected-result and connected-result syllogisms are equal in conveying the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab) and explains its analysis. The author begins by stating that a syllogism is composed of two or more premises. A simple syllogism has exactly two premises; a compound syllogism has more than two. The text then quotes the poetic matn (lines 106-109) which describes the compound syllogism and its two types regarding results: connected (muttasil al-nata'ij) where each intermediate result is stated, and disconnected (munfasil) where results are left implicit. The commentary then elaborates: a compound syllogism is actually a chain of simple syllogisms. An example is given: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sentient, every sentient is sleeping, every sleeping is a body, every body is composite.' This chain is analyzed step by step: first, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is sentient' we derive 'Every human is sentient'. This result becomes a premise for the next step: 'Every human is sentient' and 'Every sentient is sleeping' yields 'Every human is sleeping', and so on. The analysis emphasizes that a result is relative—it is a result when sought, but becomes a premise once known. Finally, the passage distinguishes between two forms of compound syllogism: if intermediate results are not explicitly stated, it is called 'munfasil' (disconnected); if they are stated, it is 'muttasil' (connected). Both are equally valid for reaching the conclusion. An Islamic example of a compound syllogism could be: 'Every act of worship requires intention, every obligatory act is an act of worship, every prayer is obligatory, therefore prayer requires intention.' The analysis would break this into two simple syllogisms.

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogisms: Connected and Disconnected Conclusions
Author defines compound syllogism and distinguishes types based on whether intermediate conclusions are stated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a compound syllogism with explicit intermediate conclusions (muttasil al-nata'ij) and one with implicit intermediate conclusions (munfasil al-nata'ij), and explain why both are equally valid in producing the final result.

Core Idea
Intermediate conclusions can be stated or folded in—both give the same final conclusion.

When analyzing complex arguments in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh or kalam, we often encounter reasoning that links multiple syllogisms. The same proposition can serve as a conclusion in one step and a premise in the next—its role is relative. This lesson introduces the compound syllogism and the distinction between stating intermediate conclusions explicitly or leaving them implicit, emphasizing that both forms are equally effective.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the relational nature of propositions
Understand that a proposition can be a conclusion in one context and a premise in another. This is purely relative: when sought it is called a conclusion, when known it is called a premise.
2
Identify the structure of a compound syllogism
A compound syllogism is made of multiple simple syllogisms. The conclusion of one becomes the premise of the next. Every compound syllogism can be analyzed into its simple parts, which follow the previously taught rules.
3
Distinguish between connected and disconnected
If the intermediate conclusions are explicitly mentioned, the compound syllogism is called 'connected in conclusions' (muttasil al-nata'ij). If they are not mentioned, it is called 'disconnected in conclusions' (munfasil al-nata'ij).
4
Note the equal validity
Both types produce the desired final conclusion equally. The difference is only in presentation, not logical force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحاصل أن نتيجة قياس ما يمكن أن تجعل مقدمة لقياس آخر ، فكونها نتيجتها أمر اعتباري، فهي من حيث هي مطلوبة تسمى نتيجة، وإذا عرفت تسمى مقدمة وقضية أيضاً، فكل قياس مركب يمكن تحليله إلى قياسات بسيطة، فيطبق على هذه القياسات البسيطة القوانين التي سبق شرحها. وفي القياس المركب ، إذا لم يتم ذكر نتيجة لكل قياس بسيط فيه، يسمى هذا القياس المركب منفصل النتائج ، فتكون النتائج مطوية فيه غير مذكورة، وأما ما ذكرت فيه النتائج فيسمى قياساً متصل النتائج، ولكن كل من القياس منفصل النتائج ومتصلها مستويان في إفادة المطلوب.
Thus, the conclusion of some syllogism can be made a premise for another syllogism. Its being a conclusion is a relative matter. As such, when sought, it is called a conclusion, and when known, it is called a premise and a proposition as well. Every compound syllogism can be analyzed into simple syllogisms, and the laws previously explained apply to these simple syllogisms. In a compound syllogism, if the conclusion for each simple syllogism is not explicitly mentioned, this compound syllogism is called 'disconnected in conclusions'; the conclusions are folded within it, not mentioned. As for what has its conclusions mentioned, it is called 'connected in conclusions'. However, both the disconnected and connected syllogisms are equal in producing the desired result.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of compound syllogisms. The author begins by noting that the same proposition can serve as a conclusion in one context and a premise in another; this is purely relational. A compound syllogism is built from multiple simple syllogisms, each of which follows the rules previously taught. The key distinction introduced is whether the intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated. If they are not stated, the compound syllogism is called 'disconnected in conclusions' (munfasil al-nata'ij), meaning the conclusions are implicit. If they are stated, it is called 'connected in conclusions' (muttasil al-nata'ij). Importantly, both types are equally effective in yielding the final desired conclusion; the difference is only in presentation. This distinction helps in analyzing complex arguments from works of fiqh or kalam into their constituent simple syllogisms.

منفصل النتائج
متصل النتائج

Al-Juwayni's View on the Premise-Conclusion Connection
Author presents the first opinion on dalala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Al-Juwayni's view that the implication from premises to conclusion is a rational, inseparable concomitance created by Allah.

Core Idea
The premises-conclusion link is a created necessity, not a mere logical form.

When we reason from premises to a conclusion, what is the nature of that 'carrying over' of truth? Is it a purely logical rule, or something deeper? In this passage, the author presents the view of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, a leading Ash'ari theologian, who argues that the connection is a rational and inseparable concomitance. He likens it to the relationship between an accident and a substance: you cannot have one without the other. And he grounds this in divine action: Allah creates knowledge of premises and conclusion together. This lesson unpacks that position and its implications for the certainty of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concept
Find the term 'dalāla' (implication). It refers to the connection between the proof (premises) and the conclusion. Notice that the author says scholars differ on its 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) and origin.
2
Understand Al-Juwayni's claim
Al-Juwayni says the connection is 'rational' (ʿaqli) and cannot be broken (lā yumkin takhallufuhu). This means whenever premises are known, the conclusion is necessarily known. There is no possibility of having the premises without the conclusion.
3
Analyze the analogy
He compares it to the concomitance between an accident and a substance. In Ash'ari ontology, an accident cannot exist without a substance. Similarly, knowledge of the conclusion cannot exist without knowledge of the premises. This highlights the inseparability.
4
Recognize the theological dimension
The passage says God creates the knowledge of premises and conclusion together. So this necessity is not a logical abstraction but a created, divinely ordained reality. God's power does not attach to only one without the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان هناك ارتباط بين الدليل وبين النتيجة وهذا الارتباط يسمى دلالة، فقد بحث العلماء في حقيقة هذه الدلالة ما منشؤها وقد اختلفوا فيها على أقوال:
القول الأول: الارتباط عقلي، لا يمكن تخلفه، وهذا قول إمام الحرمين الجويني، فلا يمكن تخلف العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة عن العلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين، بمعنى أن الله تعالى يوجد العلم بالمقدمات والعلم بالنتيجة، أو الظن بالمقدمات والظن بالنتيجة معاً، ولا تتعلق القدرة بالعلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين فقط بدون العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة، فهما متلازمان تلازما عقليا كتلازم العرض والجوهر لا يمكن وجود أحدهما بدون الآخر.
Since there is a connection between the proof and the conclusion, and this connection is called implication (dalala), the scholars have investigated the reality of this implication, what its origin is, and they have differed regarding it into three opinions:
The first opinion: The connection is rational, it cannot be separated. This is the opinion of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. So it is impossible for knowledge or conjecture of the conclusion to be separated from knowledge or conjecture of the two premises. That is, God creates the knowledge of the premises and the knowledge of the conclusion together, or the conjecture of the premises and the conjecture of the conclusion together. And [His] power does not attach to knowledge or conjecture of only the two premises without knowledge or conjecture of the conclusion. Thus they are necessarily concomitant, like the concomitance of an accident and a substance; one cannot exist without the other.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the nature of the implication (dalala) between premises and conclusion in a syllogism. The author states that scholars differ on what this connection is and its cause. He then presents the first opinion, that of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni: the connection is rational (aqli) and cannot be separated (la yumkin takhallufuhu). That is, whenever one has certain knowledge of the premises, it necessarily entails knowledge of the conclusion; similarly for conjecture. This is not a mere logical necessity but it is a created necessity by Allah: He creates knowledge of the premises and conclusion together as a pair. Thus they are like accident and substance in their inseparability. This view denies that one could have the premises without the conclusion. The passage only gives the first opinion, but the title indicates there are three. The subsequent text (not included) would present the second and third opinions. The author uses technical terms like 'tasdiq' (assent) implicitly.

Key Terms
دلالة
تلازم عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, the relationship between premises and conclusion is:
A) Conventional and could be otherwise
B) Rational and inseparable
C) Sensory and immediate
D) Based on habit and custom

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni argues that the implication from premises to conclusion is a rational, inseparable concomitance created by Allah, analogous to the relation between an accident and a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.' According to Al-Juwayni's view, if someone knows the two premises with certainty, can they still doubt the conclusion? Explain using the concept of talāzum ʿaqli.
?
Al-Juwayni likens the premises-conclusion link to the accident-substance relation. In Ash'ari kalām, an accident cannot exist without a substance. Identify one way this analogy strengthens his claim about the necessity of the implication.

The Four Opinions on the Connection Between Premises and Conclusion
Author presents four theological and philosophical views on the nature of the connection between premises and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast the four theological views on the nature of the indication (dalāla) from premises to conclusion as presented by al-Juwaynī, al-Ashʿarī, the Muʿtazila, and the philosophers.

Core Idea
The entailment from premises to conclusion is not merely logical; its metaphysical basis is debated among theologians and philosophers.

This passage presents four distinct positions on the nature of the connection between the premises and the conclusion in a syllogism. Each view reflects a deeper theological commitment about causation, divine agency, and human cognition. Understanding these views is crucial for appreciating how Islamic scholars integrated logic with their theological frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four views
List the four views as presented in the passage: rational (ʿaqlī), customary (ʿādī), generated (tawallud), and necessary (wājib).
2
Identify the proponents
Note who holds each view: al-Juwaynī for rational, al-Ashʿarī for customary, Muʿtazila for generated, philosophers for necessary.
3
Understand the key difference
The core difference is whether the connection is necessary and inseparable (rational and necessary) or contingent (customary and generated).
4
Recognize the author's preference
The author favors the rational view (al-Juwaynī) over the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلالة المقدمات على النتائج: لما كان هناك ارتباط بين الدليل وبين النتيجة وهذا الارتباط يسمى دلالة، فقد بحث العلماء في حقيقة هذه الدلالة ما منشؤها وقد اختلفوا فيها على أقوال: القول الأول: الارتباط عقلي، لا يمكن تخلفه، وهذا قول إمام الحرمين الجويني، فلا يمكن تخلف العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة عن العلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين، بمعنى أن الله تعالى يوجد العلم بالمقدمات والعلم بالنتيجة، أو الظن بالمقدمات والظن بالنتيجة معاً، ولا تتعلق القدرة بالعلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين فقط بدون العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة، فهما متلازمان تلازما عقليا كتلازم العرض والجوهر لا يمكن وجود أحدهما بدون الآخر. القول الثاني: الارتباط عادي، وهذا قول الشيخ الأشعري، ومعناه أنه يجوز تخلف العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة عن العلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين، بأن ينتهي شخص في البلادة إلى أن يعلم المقدمتين ولا يعلم النتيجة لعدم تفطنه لاندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط، وفي التصوير نظر إذ من الشروط التفطن لاندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط. القول الثالث: الارتباط تولد، بمعنى أن القدرة الحادثة أثرت في العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة بواسطة تأثيرها في العلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين، إذ التولد أن يوجد فعل لفاعله فعلاً آخر، وهذا القول للمعتزلة وهو باطل لقيام البرهان على أنه لا تأثير للعبد في شيء من الأفعال الاختيارية. القول الرابع: الارتباط واجب، وهو للفلاسفة، ومعناه أن العلم أو الظن بالمقدمتين علة أثرت بذاتها في العلم أو الظن بالنتيجة، وهو باطل لقيام البرهان على انتفاء تأثير العلة والطبيعة وأنه تعالى هو الفاعل المختار. وقد رجح الناظم القول الأول على غيره من الأقوال.
The indication of premises to conclusions: Since there is a connection between the proof and the conclusion, and this connection is called 'indication' (dalāla), the scholars investigated the reality of this indication: what is its source? They differed concerning it into the following views: The first view: the connection is rational (ʿaqlī), not subject to non-occurrence. This is the view of Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī, meaning that it is impossible for knowledge or opinion of the conclusion to be absent while knowledge or opinion of the two premises is present. In other words, Allah creates knowledge of the premises and knowledge of the conclusion together, or opinion of the premises and opinion of the conclusion together; and power does not pertain to knowledge or opinion of the two premises alone without knowledge or opinion of the conclusion. So they are necessarily concomitant in a rational way, like the concomitance of accident and substance, where one cannot exist without the other. The second view: the connection is customary (ʿādī). This is the view of Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, meaning that it is permissible for knowledge or opinion of the conclusion to be absent while knowledge or opinion of the two premises is present, such that a person of extreme dullness might know the two premises but not know the conclusion due to his failure to notice the subsumption of the minor under the middle. However, this depiction is questionable because one of the conditions is noticing the subsumption of the minor under the middle. The third view: the connection is generated (tawallud). This means that the created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) causes knowledge or opinion of the conclusion through its effect on knowledge or opinion of the two premises, for 'generation' (tawallud) is that an act produces another act from its agent. This is the view of the Muʿtazila, and it is invalid because proof has been established that the servant has no effect on any of his voluntary acts. The fourth view: the connection is necessary (wājib). This is the view of the philosophers, meaning that knowledge or opinion of the two premises is a cause that effectively produces knowledge or opinion of the conclusion by its own essence. This is invalid because proof has been established that causal and natural efficacy are negated, and that He, the Exalted, is the free agent. And the author (al-nāẓim) favored the first view over the others.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the entailment (dalāla) between premises and conclusion in a syllogism. After noting that the connection between proof and result is termed 'indication', the author presents four prominent views on its source. The first view, attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī, holds that the connection is rational (ʿaqlī) and cannot fail: whenever a person has knowledge (or opinion) of the premises, he must have knowledge (or opinion) of the conclusion, because Allah creates both simultaneously. The relationship is likened to the inseparable concomitance between accident and substance. The second view, from Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, says the connection is merely habitual (ʿādī): it is possible for someone to know the premises yet not know the conclusion if he lacks the acumen to notice that the minor term is subsumed under the middle term. However, the author objects that noticing this subsumption is actually a condition for a valid syllogism, so this scenario is questionable. The third view is the Muʿtazilī theory of generation (tawallud): the servant's created power first acts on the premises, thereby generating the conclusion. The author dismisses this as invalid because, according to Ashʿarī theology, the servant has no real causal efficacy. The fourth view, held by philosophers (falāsifa), posits that the premises are a cause that produces the conclusion by its own nature. This is also rejected because it implies causal efficacy in created things, contra the Ashʿarī doctrine that Allah alone is the true agent. The author (the versifier of the text, al-nāẓim) gives preference to the first (rational) view.

Key Terms
دلالة
ارتباط عقلي
ارتباط عادي
تولد
ارتباط واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view holds that the connection between premises and conclusion is a necessary rational concomitance created by Allah?
A) The rational connection (al-Juwaynī)
B) The customary connection (al-Ashʿarī)
C) The generated connection (Muʿtazila)
D) The necessary connection (Philosophers)

Key Takeaway
The four opinions on dalāla show that for Muslim scholars, the validity of inference is inseparable from theological commitments about causality and divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Summarize each of the four views in one sentence each.
?
2. Explain why the author considers the customary view (al-Ashʿarī) questionable given the conditions of a valid syllogism.

The Rational Connection (al-Juwaynī)
Author presents al-Juwaynī's view as the first opinion on the nature of entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Juwaynī's doctrine of rational entailment (irtibāṭ ʿaqlī) and its theological implications.

Core Idea
For al-Juwaynī, knowledge of premises and conclusion are created by Allah together, making their connection rationally inseparable.

Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī argued that the connection between premises and conclusion is not merely a human mental habit but a rational necessity. In this view, Allah creates both the knowledge of the premises and the knowledge of the conclusion simultaneously, so that one cannot exist without the other. This eliminates any possibility of knowing the premises without inferring the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Locate the rational connection view
Find the first opinion in the passage and identify its proponent: Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī.
2
Understand the claim
The connection is 'aqlī, meaning it cannot fail. Explain what cannot fail: the existence of knowledge (or opinion) of the conclusion whenever there is knowledge (or opinion) of the premises.
3
Analyze the theological underpinning
The reason is that Allah creates both together; His power cannot separate them. This aligns with Ashʿarī occasionalism where Allah is the direct creator of all events.
4
Compare with the substance-accident analogy
The text says the relation is like the concomitance of accident and substance – one cannot exist without the other.

The Customary Connection (al-Ashʿarī)
Author presents al-Ashʿarī's view as the second opinion on the nature of entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe al-Ashʿarī's customary view and the problem the author raises against it.

Core Idea
Al-Ashʿarī held that the connection is merely customary, so a dull person might know premises but not conclude.

Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī proposed that the connection between premises and conclusion is not necessary but habitual (ʿādī). In this view, it is possible for someone to possess knowledge of the premises yet fail to know the conclusion, if they lack the acumen to notice that the minor term is subsumed under the middle term. This raises questions about the conditions for a valid syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Find the customary view
Locate the second opinion in the passage and its proponent: Shaykh al-Ashʿarī.
2
Understand the scenario
A person of extreme dullness (balāda) might know the premises but not the conclusion due to not noticing the subsumption of the minor under the middle.
3
Note the author's objection
The author says this depiction is questionable because noticing the subsumption is actually a condition (shart) for a valid syllogism; thus if it is missing, the person doesn't truly have the premises in the relevant sense.
4
Theological implication
This view aligns with al-Ashʿarī's occasionalism: Allah could choose not to create knowledge of the conclusion even if premises are known, but normally does so by custom.

Checkpoint
In the customary view, why might a person fail to know the conclusion despite knowing the premises?
A) Because the premises are false
B) Because he does not notice the subsumption of the minor under the middle
C) Because Allah does not create the knowledge of the conclusion
D) Because the connection is necessary and cannot fail

Key Takeaway
Al-Ashʿarī's customary view emphasizes that entailment depends on the human cognitive act of noticing the logical structure, which Allah normally provides but could withhold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Explain the scenario of the dull person and why the author questions it.
?
2. Compare this view with the rational view: which one guarantees that a valid syllogism always yields knowledge of the conclusion?
